The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Classification of Logical Syllogism: Iqtirani and Istithna'i
Author defines two types of logical syllogism and explains their validity conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conjunctive (iqtirani) from the exceptive (istithna'i) syllogism and explain why the first figure is self-evidently valid and why only two moods of the exceptive syllogism are productive.

Core Idea
The first figure is a self-evident rational truth; exceptive syllogisms yield only modus ponens and modus tollens.

This lesson introduces the two fundamental types of syllogism in Islamic logic: the conjunctive (qiyas iqtirani) and the exceptive (qiyas istithna'i). The author explains that all figures of the conjunctive syllogism reduce to the first figure, whose validity is a necessary rational judgment. For the exceptive syllogism, only two moods are productive: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and negating the consequent (modus tollens). Understanding these distinctions is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Locate where the author states that the logical syllogism is of two types: iqtirani (conjunctive) and istithna'i (exceptive). Note the alternative names: hamli and qiyas al-shumul for the first type.
2
Analyze the first figure validity
Find the rational principle: when the minor term falls under the middle, and the middle under the major, the minor necessarily falls under the major. Recognize this as a self-evident truth that does not change with time.
This principle is the basis for the first figure's validity.
3
Examine the exceptional syllogism's conditions
Identify that the exceptive syllogism requires a conditional conjunctive premise (muttasila luzumiyya) and an exceptive premise. Then note the two productive moods: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and negating the consequent yields the negation of the antecedent.
The author does not mention the invalid moods here, but they are implicit (e.g., denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإيضاحه باختصار أن القياس المنطقي نوعان : الأول: الاقتراني، وهو المعروف بالحملي، وقياس الشمول كما سيأتي إيضاحه إن شاء الله ، وسائر أشكاله راجعة إلى الشكل الأول، ووجه الحكم العقلي بصحة إنتاج الشكل الأول هو أن الحد الأصغر إذا اندرج في الحد الأوسط، واندرج الأوسط في الأكبر، لزم عقلاً اندراج الأصغر في الأكبر، وهذا أمر عقلي لاشك فيه، ولا يتغير بتغير الزمان؛ لأنه حكم عقلي قطعي ثابت . وأما القياس الاستثنائي إن كان مركبا من متصلة لزومية واستثنائية فلا يُنتج منه إلا ضربان، وحاصلهما بالتقريب للذهن أن نفي اللازم يقتضي نفي الملزوم ، وثبوت الملزوم يقتضي ثبوت اللازم
Its explanation in brief: that the logical syllogism is of two types: First: the conjunctive (iqtirani), which is known as the categorical (hamli) and the syllogism of inclusion (qiyas al-shumul), as will be explained later, God willing. And all its other figures go back to the first figure. The basis of the rational judgment of the validity of the first figure's production is that when the minor term falls under the middle term, and the middle falls under the major, it is rationally necessary that the minor falls under the major. This is a rational matter about which there is no doubt, and it does not change with time because it is a definite and stable rational judgment. As for the exceptive (istithna'i) syllogism, if it is composed of a conditional conjunctive (muttasila luzumiyya) and an exceptive (istithna'iyya), then it produces only two modes. Their essence, approximated for the mind, is that the negation of the consequent entails the negation of the antecedent, and the affirmation of the antecedent entails the affirmation of the consequent.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the two main types of logical syllogism: the conjunctive (iqtirani) and the exceptive (istithna'i). The author begins by stating the two types. The first type is iqtirani, also known as categorical (hamli) or syllogism of inclusion (qiyas al-shumul). All its other figures reduce to the first figure. The author then explains why the first figure is valid: it is a rational necessity that if the minor term is subsumed under the middle term, and the middle under the major, then the minor is subsumed under the major. This rational judgment is self-evident and unchanging. The second type is istithna'i. If it is composed of a conditional conjunctive premise (muttasila luzumiyya, i.e., an 'if-then' statement indicating necessary concomitance) and an exceptive premise (istithna'iyya, which either affirms the antecedent or negates the consequent), then only two valid modes arise: (1) affirming the antecedent leads to affirming the consequent, and (2) negating the consequent leads to negating the antecedent. The author does not mention the invalid modes here. This passage is foundational for understanding the structure and validity conditions of syllogisms in Islamic logic. The author uses a clear argumentative order: first define the types, then explain the validity of the first figure of iqtirani, then describe the two productive modes of istithna'i.

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني
القياس الاستثنائي
الشكل الأول
المتصلة اللزومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many productive moods does the exceptive syllogism have when composed of a conditional conjunctive and an exceptive premise?
A) Four
B) Two
C) Three
D) One

Key Takeaway
The logical syllogism is of two types: conjunctive (all figures reduce to the first, which is self-evidently valid) and exceptive (only two moods are valid: modus ponens and modus tollens).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism on a Fiqh topic: using 'wine is forbidden because it intoxicates, and nabidh is also intoxicating, therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the minor, middle, and major terms.
?
For the exceptive syllogism, give an example from Usul al-Fiqh: 'If the evidence is definitive (qat'i), then the ruling is certain. This evidence is definitive. Therefore, the ruling is certain.' Verify that it matches one of the two valid moods.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Valid Moods of Real Exclusive Disjunction
Author validates the four moods of compound syllogisms from real exclusive disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to validate all four moods of a compound syllogism from a real exclusive disjunctive conditional (مانعة جمع وخُلُو).

Core Idea
In a real exclusive disjunction, the two sides are contradictories, making every mood valid.

The strongest form of a disjunctive conditional is the one that prevents both the conjunction and the absence of its two sides. This is called the real exclusive disjunction (مانعة جمع وخُلُو). In this lesson, we will see why the mind judges all four moods of a compound syllogism built from such a conditional as valid, and how this applies in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the definition of مانعة جمع وخُلُو
This disjunction prohibits both sides being true together and both being false together. The two sides are therefore strict contradictories: the truth of one is identical to the falsehood of the other.
2
List the four possible moods
1) Affirm first side → negate second; 2) Affirm second → negate first; 3) Negate first → affirm second; 4) Negate second → affirm first.
These exhaust all possibilities.
3
Verify each mood with the contradictory relationship
Since the negation of a side is the affirmation of the other (and vice versa), each mood is logically equivalent to a direct implication.
For example, negating first is exactly affirming second.
4
Apply an Islamic example
Consider the proposition: 'Either this act is obligatory (wajib) or it is not obligatory (ghayr wajib).' The two sides are contradictories. All four moods hold.
Try: 'The act is wajib' entails 'It is not ghayr wajib'; 'It is not wajib' entails 'It is ghayr wajib', etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمركب من شرطية منفصلة إن كانت حقيقية مانعة جمع وخُلُو أنتجت ضروبُه الأربعة ؛ لأن نفي كل واحد من النقيضين يستلزم وجود الآخر، ووجود كل واحد منهما يستلزم نفي الآخر، وهذه أحكام عقلية قطعية لا تتغير بحال . والمركب من مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو يقطع العقل بصحة إنتاج ضربيه المنتجين ؛ لأن مانعة الجمع المذكورة لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأخص من نقيضها، فكلا طرفيها أخص من نقيض الآخر، ولذا كان إثبات كل واحد من طرفيها يقتضي نفي الآخر؛ لأن إثبات ما هو أخص من النقيض يستلزم إثبات النقيض؛ لأن ثبوت الأخص يستلزم ثبوت الأعم بلا عكس . وإذا ثبت النقيض عُلم انتفاء نقيضه قطعاً؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فتعين أن ثبوت كل واحد من طرفيها يقتضي نفي الآخر، بخلاف نفي أحد الطرفين فلا يقتضي نفي الآخر؛ لأن نفي ما هو أخص من النقيض لا يستلزم نفي النقيض؛ لأن نفي الأخص لا يستلزم نفي الأعم.
(۱) يعني مع الاستثنائية، انظر توضیحه ص١٣٤ .
وكذلك المركب من مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع، فإن العقل الصحيح يقطع بصحة إنتاج الضربين المنتجين منه، وهما العقيمان في الذي قبله؛ لأن مانعة الخلو المجوزة للجمع لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأعم من نقيضها، فكلا طرفيها أعم من نقيض الآخر، ولذا كان نفي كل واحد منهما يقتضي وجود الآخر بلا عكس؛ لأن نفي ماهو أعم من النقيض يستلزم نفي النقيض؛ لأن نفي الأعم يستلزم نفي الأخص وإذا لزم من انتفائه انتفاء النقيض عُلم قطعا ثبوتُ النقيض الآخَرِ؛ لاستحالة عدم النقيضين معا . فتبين أن نفي كل واحد من طرفيها يستلزم ثبوت الآخر بلا عكس، وكلَّ ماذكر بحكم العقل (۱) ، وأنه لا يتغير بتغير الزمان .
(۱) أي وتبين أن كلّ ما ذُكر ثابت بحكم العقل .
And the compound [syllogism] from a disjunctive conditional, if it is a real one that prevents both combination and absence, its four moods produce [inferences]; because the negation of each of the two contradictories necessitates the existence of the other, and the existence of each of the two necessitates the negation of the other, and these are certain rational judgments that do not change in any circumstance. And the compound from a preventing-combination that permits absence, the mind judges with certainty the validity of its two productive moods; because the mentioned preventing-combination is only composed from a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory, so each of its two sides is more specific than the contradictory of the other, and therefore affirming each of its two sides requires negating the other; because affirming what is more specific than the contradictory necessitates affirming the contradictory; because the establishment of the more specific necessitates the establishment of the more general without the reverse. And if the contradictory is established, the negation of its contradictory is known with certainty, due to the impossibility of the coexistence of two contradictories; so it is determined that affirming each of its two sides requires negating the other, unlike negating one of the two sides, for it does not require negating the other; because negating what is more specific than the contradictory does not necessitate negating the contradictory; because negating the more specific does not necessitate negating the more general. (1) Meaning with the exceptive [syllogism], see its clarification p.134. And likewise, the compound from a preventing-absence that permits combination, for the sound mind judges with certainty the validity of its two productive moods, and they are the two barren ones in the previous [type]; because the preventing-absence that permits combination is only composed from a proposition and something more general than its contradictory, so each of its two sides is more general than the contradictory of the other, and therefore negating each of the two requires the existence of the other without the reverse; because negating what is more general than the contradictory necessitates negating the contradictory; because negating the more general necessitates negating the more specific, and if from its negation the negation of the contradictory follows, the existence of the other contradictory is known with certainty, due to the impossibility of the absence of both contradictories together. So it is shown that negating each of its two sides necessitates the existence of the other without the reverse, and all that has been mentioned is by rational judgment (1), and that it does not change with changing time. (1) Meaning: it is shown that all that was mentioned is established by rational judgment.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage classifies and validates the moods of compound syllogisms built from disjunctive or conjunctive conditionals. The author begins with the real exclusive disjunctive conditional (مانعة جمع وخُلُو), where the two sides cannot both be true and cannot both be false. In such a conditional, all four moods are valid: affirming one side negates the other, and negating one side affirms the other. This is because the two sides are strict contradictories; the negation of one is identical to the affirmation of the other. The author emphasizes that these are certain rational judgments, unchanging.

Next, the author considers the conditional that prevents combination but permits absence (مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو). Here, the two sides cannot be true together but can be false together. The valid moods are only the two that affirm one side to negate the other; the moods that negate one side to affirm the other are invalid. The reason given is that in this type, each side is more specific (أخص) than the contradictory of the other side. For example, if one side is 'Zaid is in the house' and the other is 'Zaid is in the mosque', the contradictory of 'in the house' is 'not in the house', which includes 'in the mosque' but also other places. 'In the mosque' is more specific than 'not in the house', so affirming 'in the mosque' necessitates 'not in the house', but negating it does not necessitate 'in the house' because he might be elsewhere. The logical rule: affirming the more specific entails affirming the more general, but not vice versa.

Then the author discusses the conditional that prevents absence but permits combination (مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع). Here the two sides cannot be false together but can be true together. The valid moods are the two that negate one side to affirm the other; the moods that affirm one side to negate the other are invalid. In this type, each side is more general (أعم) than the contradictory of the other. For instance, if one side is 'This is a man' and the other is 'This is an animal', the contradictory of 'man' is 'not man', which includes non-human animals. 'Animal' is more general than 'not man', so negating 'man' necessitates 'animal', but affirming 'man' does not negate 'animal' because it could be both. The rule: negating the more general necessitates negating the more specific, and negating the more specific does not necessitate negating the more general. Hence, only the negation-affirmation moods are valid.

All these rules are presented as certain judgments of the intellect, unchanged by time.

Key Terms
شرطية منفصلة
مانعة جمع وخُلُو
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a real exclusive disjunction (مانعة جمع وخُلُو), which of the following is true?
A) Affirming one side requires negating the other, but negating one does not require affirming the other.
B) Negating one side requires affirming the other, but affirming one does not require negating the other.
C) Both affirming one to negate the other and negating one to affirm the other are valid.
D) Neither affirmation nor negation moods are valid.

Key Takeaway
A real exclusive disjunction makes all four moods of a compound syllogism valid because its two sides are strict contradictories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a real exclusive disjunction from fiqh: 'Either this sale is valid (sahih) or it is void (bātil).' Write out the four moods of the compound syllogism and verify each by substituting the terms. Then using the rules of contradiction, confirm that each inference is necessary.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Valid Moods of the Disjunction That Prevents Combination but Permits Absence
Author explains the valid moods for the disjunction that prevents combination but permits absence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which moods are valid in a compound syllogism from a disjunction that prohibits conjunction but allows both sides to be false (مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو).

Core Idea
When the disjunction prevents conjunction but allows absence, only the moods that affirm one side to negate the other are valid.

The second type of disjunctive conditional discussed by the author is مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو, which prevents the two sides from being true together but allows both to be false. This type requires a different analysis. In this lesson, we will explore why only two of the four moods are valid, using the relationship of specificity (أخص) between each side and the contradictory of the other.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو
This disjunction prohibits both sides being true together (لا تجتمع) but permits both being false (يخلو). So the two sides cannot both be true, but can both be false.
2
Identify the relationship of specificity
Each side is more specific (أخص) than the contradictory of the other side. For example, if one side is 'Zaid is in the house', its contradictory is 'Zaid is not in the house', which includes many possibilities. The other side 'Zaid is in the mosque' is more specific than 'not in the house'.
The author states: لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأخص من نقيضها.
3
Apply the logical rule of specificity
Affirming the more specific (أخص) entails affirming the more general (أعم). So affirming one side (أخص of the other's contradictory) entails affirming the other's contradictory, which means negating the other side. But negating a specific does not entail negating the general, so negating one side does not affirm the other.
4
Determine valid moods
Therefore, only the two moods that affirm one side and negate the other are valid. The moods that negate one side to affirm the other are invalid.
The author says: فتعين أن ثبوت كل واحد من طرفيها يقتضي نفي الآخر، بخلاف نفي أحد الطرفين فلا يقتضي نفي الآخر.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Consider: 'Either this act is obligatory (wajib) or it is recommended (mandub).' These can both be false (it could be permissible). Affirming it is wajib entails it is not mandub. But negating wajib does not entail mandub; it could be permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمركب من شرطية منفصلة إن كانت حقيقية مانعة جمع وخُلُو أنتجت ضروبُه الأربعة ؛ لأن نفي كل واحد من النقيضين يستلزم وجود الآخر، ووجود كل واحد منهما يستلزم نفي الآخر، وهذه أحكام عقلية قطعية لا تتغير بحال . والمركب من مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو يقطع العقل بصحة إنتاج ضربيه المنتجين ؛ لأن مانعة الجمع المذكورة لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأخص من نقيضها، فكلا طرفيها أخص من نقيض الآخر، ولذا كان إثبات كل واحد من طرفيها يقتضي نفي الآخر؛ لأن إثبات ما هو أخص من النقيض يستلزم إثبات النقيض؛ لأن ثبوت الأخص يستلزم ثبوت الأعم بلا عكس . وإذا ثبت النقيض عُلم انتفاء نقيضه قطعاً؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فتعين أن ثبوت كل واحد من طرفيها يقتضي نفي الآخر، بخلاف نفي أحد الطرفين فلا يقتضي نفي الآخر؛ لأن نفي ما هو أخص من النقيض لا يستلزم نفي النقيض؛ لأن نفي الأخص لا يستلزم نفي الأعم.
(۱) يعني مع الاستثنائية، انظر توضیحه ص١٣٤ .
وكذلك المركب من مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع، فإن العقل الصحيح يقطع بصحة إنتاج الضربين المنتجين منه، وهما العقيمان في الذي قبله؛ لأن مانعة الخلو المجوزة للجمع لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأعم من نقيضها، فكلا طرفيها أعم من نقيض الآخر، ولذا كان نفي كل واحد منهما يقتضي وجود الآخر بلا عكس؛ لأن نفي ماهو أعم من النقيض يستلزم نفي النقيض؛ لأن نفي الأعم يستلزم نفي الأخص وإذا لزم من انتفائه انتفاء النقيض عُلم قطعا ثبوتُ النقيض الآخَرِ؛ لاستحالة عدم النقيضين معا . فتبين أن نفي كل واحد من طرفيها يستلزم ثبوت الآخر بلا عكس، وكلَّ ماذكر بحكم العقل (۱) ، وأنه لا يتغير بتغير الزمان .
(۱) أي وتبين أن كلّ ما ذُكر ثابت بحكم العقل .
And the compound [syllogism] from a disjunctive conditional, if it is a real one that prevents both combination and absence, its four moods produce [inferences]; because the negation of each of the two contradictories necessitates the existence of the other, and the existence of each of the two necessitates the negation of the other, and these are certain rational judgments that do not change in any circumstance. And the compound from a preventing-combination that permits absence, the mind judges with certainty the validity of its two productive moods; because the mentioned preventing-combination is only composed from a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory, so each of its two sides is more specific than the contradictory of the other, and therefore affirming each of its two sides requires negating the other; because affirming what is more specific than the contradictory necessitates affirming the contradictory; because the establishment of the more specific necessitates the establishment of the more general without the reverse. And if the contradictory is established, the negation of its contradictory is known with certainty, due to the impossibility of the coexistence of two contradictories; so it is determined that affirming each of its two sides requires negating the other, unlike negating one of the two sides, for it does not require negating the other; because negating what is more specific than the contradictory does not necessitate negating the contradictory; because negating the more specific does not necessitate negating the more general. (1) Meaning with the exceptive [syllogism], see its clarification p.134. And likewise, the compound from a preventing-absence that permits combination, for the sound mind judges with certainty the validity of its two productive moods, and they are the two barren ones in the previous [type]; because the preventing-absence that permits combination is only composed from a proposition and something more general than its contradictory, so each of its two sides is more general than the contradictory of the other, and therefore negating each of the two requires the existence of the other without the reverse; because negating what is more general than the contradictory necessitates negating the contradictory; because negating the more general necessitates negating the more specific, and if from its negation the negation of the contradictory follows, the existence of the other contradictory is known with certainty, due to the impossibility of the absence of both contradictories together. So it is shown that negating each of its two sides necessitates the existence of the other without the reverse, and all that has been mentioned is by rational judgment (1), and that it does not change with changing time. (1) Meaning: it is shown that all that was mentioned is established by rational judgment.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage classifies and validates the moods of compound syllogisms built from disjunctive or conjunctive conditionals. The author begins with the real exclusive disjunctive conditional (مانعة جمع وخُلُو), where the two sides cannot both be true and cannot both be false. In such a conditional, all four moods are valid: affirming one side negates the other, and negating one side affirms the other. This is because the two sides are strict contradictories; the negation of one is identical to the affirmation of the other. The author emphasizes that these are certain rational judgments, unchanging.

Next, the author considers the conditional that prevents combination but permits absence (مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو). Here, the two sides cannot be true together but can be false together. The valid moods are only the two that affirm one side to negate the other; the moods that negate one side to affirm the other are invalid. The reason given is that in this type, each side is more specific (أخص) than the contradictory of the other side. For example, if one side is 'Zaid is in the house' and the other is 'Zaid is in the mosque', the contradictory of 'in the house' is 'not in the house', which includes 'in the mosque' but also other places. 'In the mosque' is more specific than 'not in the house', so affirming 'in the mosque' necessitates 'not in the house', but negating it does not necessitate 'in the house' because he might be elsewhere. The logical rule: affirming the more specific entails affirming the more general, but not vice versa.

Then the author discusses the conditional that prevents absence but permits combination (مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع). Here the two sides cannot be false together but can be true together. The valid moods are the two that negate one side to affirm the other; the moods that affirm one side to negate the other are invalid. In this type, each side is more general (أعم) than the contradictory of the other. For instance, if one side is 'This is a man' and the other is 'This is an animal', the contradictory of 'man' is 'not man', which includes non-human animals. 'Animal' is more general than 'not man', so negating 'man' necessitates 'animal', but affirming 'man' does not negate 'animal' because it could be both. The rule: negating the more general necessitates negating the more specific, and negating the more specific does not necessitate negating the more general. Hence, only the negation-affirmation moods are valid.

All these rules are presented as certain judgments of the intellect, unchanged by time.

Valid Moods of the Disjunction That Prevents Absence but Permits Combination
Author explains the valid moods for the disjunction that prevents absence but permits combination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which moods are valid in a compound syllogism from a disjunction that prohibits both sides being false together but allows both to be true (مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع).

Core Idea
When the disjunction prevents absence but permits combination, only the moods that negate one side to affirm the other are valid.

The third type of disjunctive conditional is مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع, which prevents the two sides from being false together but allows both to be true. This is the mirror image of the previous type. In this lesson, we will learn why only the negation-affirmation moods are valid, using the relationship of generality (أعم) between each side and the contradictory of the other.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع
This disjunction prohibits both sides being false together (لا يخلو), but permits both being true (يجوز الجمع). So the two sides cannot both be false, but can both be true.
2
Identify the relationship of generality
Each side is more general (أعم) than the contradictory of the other side. For example, if one side is 'This is an animal', its contradictory 'This is not an animal' is more specific. The other side 'This is a human' is more specific than 'not animal'.
The author states: لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأعم من نقيضها.
3
Apply the logical rule of generality
Negating the more general (أعم) entails negating the more specific (أخص). So negating one side (which is more general than the other's contradictory) entails negating the other's contradictory, which means affirming the other side. But affirming a general does not entail affirming the specific, so affirming one side does not negate the other.
4
Determine valid moods
Therefore, only the two moods that negate one side to affirm the other are valid. The moods that affirm one side to negate the other are invalid.
The author says: فتبين أن نفي كل واحد من طرفيها يستلزم ثبوت الآخر بلا عكس.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Consider: 'Either this is a human or this is an animal.' These can both be true (a human is an animal). Negating 'animal' entails 'not animal' which contradicts 'human', but negating 'human' does not entail 'animal'? Actually careful: The two sides are 'This is a human' and 'This is an animal'. Since 'animal' is more general than 'not human', negating 'human' (i.e., 'not human') does not necessarily affirm 'animal' because it could be a non-human animal. But the disjunction says they cannot both be false, so at least one must be true. So if we negate 'human', we must affirm 'animal' because the only way to avoid both false is to have 'animal' true. That works. So the valid mood is: negate human -> affirm animal. Affirm animal does not negate human because it could be both true.
Better example: 'Either this is a prophet or this is a human.' They can both be true (prophets are human). Negating prophet affirms human; affirming human does not negate prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمركب من شرطية منفصلة إن كانت حقيقية مانعة جمع وخُلُو أنتجت ضروبُه الأربعة ؛ لأن نفي كل واحد من النقيضين يستلزم وجود الآخر، ووجود كل واحد منهما يستلزم نفي الآخر، وهذه أحكام عقلية قطعية لا تتغير بحال . والمركب من مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو يقطع العقل بصحة إنتاج ضربيه المنتجين ؛ لأن مانعة الجمع المذكورة لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأخص من نقيضها، فكلا طرفيها أخص من نقيض الآخر، ولذا كان إثبات كل واحد من طرفيها يقتضي نفي الآخر؛ لأن إثبات ما هو أخص من النقيض يستلزم إثبات النقيض؛ لأن ثبوت الأخص يستلزم ثبوت الأعم بلا عكس . وإذا ثبت النقيض عُلم انتفاء نقيضه قطعاً؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فتعين أن ثبوت كل واحد من طرفيها يقتضي نفي الآخر، بخلاف نفي أحد الطرفين فلا يقتضي نفي الآخر؛ لأن نفي ما هو أخص من النقيض لا يستلزم نفي النقيض؛ لأن نفي الأخص لا يستلزم نفي الأعم.
(۱) يعني مع الاستثنائية، انظر توضیحه ص١٣٤ .
وكذلك المركب من مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع، فإن العقل الصحيح يقطع بصحة إنتاج الضربين المنتجين منه، وهما العقيمان في الذي قبله؛ لأن مانعة الخلو المجوزة للجمع لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأعم من نقيضها، فكلا طرفيها أعم من نقيض الآخر، ولذا كان نفي كل واحد منهما يقتضي وجود الآخر بلا عكس؛ لأن نفي ماهو أعم من النقيض يستلزم نفي النقيض؛ لأن نفي الأعم يستلزم نفي الأخص وإذا لزم من انتفائه انتفاء النقيض عُلم قطعا ثبوتُ النقيض الآخَرِ؛ لاستحالة عدم النقيضين معا . فتبين أن نفي كل واحد من طرفيها يستلزم ثبوت الآخر بلا عكس، وكلَّ ماذكر بحكم العقل (۱) ، وأنه لا يتغير بتغير الزمان .
(۱) أي وتبين أن كلّ ما ذُكر ثابت بحكم العقل .
And the compound [syllogism] from a disjunctive conditional, if it is a real one that prevents both combination and absence, its four moods produce [inferences]; because the negation of each of the two contradictories necessitates the existence of the other, and the existence of each of the two necessitates the negation of the other, and these are certain rational judgments that do not change in any circumstance. And the compound from a preventing-combination that permits absence, the mind judges with certainty the validity of its two productive moods; because the mentioned preventing-combination is only composed from a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory, so each of its two sides is more specific than the contradictory of the other, and therefore affirming each of its two sides requires negating the other; because affirming what is more specific than the contradictory necessitates affirming the contradictory; because the establishment of the more specific necessitates the establishment of the more general without the reverse. And if the contradictory is established, the negation of its contradictory is known with certainty, due to the impossibility of the coexistence of two contradictories; so it is determined that affirming each of its two sides requires negating the other, unlike negating one of the two sides, for it does not require negating the other; because negating what is more specific than the contradictory does not necessitate negating the contradictory; because negating the more specific does not necessitate negating the more general. (1) Meaning with the exceptive [syllogism], see its clarification p.134. And likewise, the compound from a preventing-absence that permits combination, for the sound mind judges with certainty the validity of its two productive moods, and they are the two barren ones in the previous [type]; because the preventing-absence that permits combination is only composed from a proposition and something more general than its contradictory, so each of its two sides is more general than the contradictory of the other, and therefore negating each of the two requires the existence of the other without the reverse; because negating what is more general than the contradictory necessitates negating the contradictory; because negating the more general necessitates negating the more specific, and if from its negation the negation of the contradictory follows, the existence of the other contradictory is known with certainty, due to the impossibility of the absence of both contradictories together. So it is shown that negating each of its two sides necessitates the existence of the other without the reverse, and all that has been mentioned is by rational judgment (1), and that it does not change with changing time. (1) Meaning: it is shown that all that was mentioned is established by rational judgment.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage classifies and validates the moods of compound syllogisms built from disjunctive or conjunctive conditionals. The author begins with the real exclusive disjunctive conditional (مانعة جمع وخُلُو), where the two sides cannot both be true and cannot both be false. In such a conditional, all four moods are valid: affirming one side negates the other, and negating one side affirms the other. This is because the two sides are strict contradictories; the negation of one is identical to the affirmation of the other. The author emphasizes that these are certain rational judgments, unchanging.

Next, the author considers the conditional that prevents combination but permits absence (مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخُلُو). Here, the two sides cannot be true together but can be false together. The valid moods are only the two that affirm one side to negate the other; the moods that negate one side to affirm the other are invalid. The reason given is that in this type, each side is more specific (أخص) than the contradictory of the other side. For example, if one side is 'Zaid is in the house' and the other is 'Zaid is in the mosque', the contradictory of 'in the house' is 'not in the house', which includes 'in the mosque' but also other places. 'In the mosque' is more specific than 'not in the house', so affirming 'in the mosque' necessitates 'not in the house', but negating it does not necessitate 'in the house' because he might be elsewhere. The logical rule: affirming the more specific entails affirming the more general, but not vice versa.

Then the author discusses the conditional that prevents absence but permits combination (مانعة الخُلُو المجوزة للجمع). Here the two sides cannot be false together but can be true together. The valid moods are the two that negate one side to affirm the other; the moods that affirm one side to negate the other are invalid. In this type, each side is more general (أعم) than the contradictory of the other. For instance, if one side is 'This is a man' and the other is 'This is an animal', the contradictory of 'man' is 'not man', which includes non-human animals. 'Animal' is more general than 'not man', so negating 'man' necessitates 'animal', but affirming 'man' does not negate 'animal' because it could be both. The rule: negating the more general necessitates negating the more specific, and negating the more specific does not necessitate negating the more general. Hence, only the negation-affirmation moods are valid.

All these rules are presented as certain judgments of the intellect, unchanged by time.

Understanding the Contingent Conditional with Quranic Examples
Defines contingent conditional with examples and corrects misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish contingent conditionals from necessary conditionals and identify the real cause behind a contingent conditional in Quranic verses.

Core Idea
In a contingent conditional, the antecedent and consequent are causally unrelated—the real cause lies elsewhere.

Not every 'if-then' in the Quran implies a causal link. This lesson explains the contingent conditional, where the antecedent does not cause the consequent. You will learn to spot such conditionals and avoid the common mistake of treating them as necessary.

Guided Path
1
Define the contingent conditional
Understand that in a contingent conditional, the antecedent and consequent have no causal relationship; neither implies the other or its negation.
Contrast with necessary conditional (luzūmiyya) where the antecedent entails the consequent.
2
Examine the first example
Look at the fabricated example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the human is speaking.' Notice that sunrise and speech are unrelated.
This shows no causal connection.
3
Analyze Quranic examples
Read the verses from Al Imran 154, Al-Kahf 109, and Al-Kahf 57. For each, identify why the antecedent and consequent are not causally connected.
Example: In Al-Kahf 57, the call to guidance does not cause non-guidance.
4
Understand the critique of al-Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan
They mistakenly considered the verse from Al-Kahf 57 as a necessary conditional. The author explains that it is contingent.
The error arises from assuming a causal link where none exists.
5
Identify the real cause
Recognize that the true cause of the consequent is not the antecedent but something else, such as God's will or love of God.
In the example 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him', the real cause is love of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدمه ; إذ لا علاقة بينهما أصلاً كقولك كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان الإنسان ناطقا فلا علاقة أصلاً بين طلوع الشمس وبين نطق الإنسان . ومن أمثلتها في القرآن قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ لَوْ كُنتُمْ فِي بُيُوتِكُمْ لَبَرَزَ الَّذِينَ كُتِبَ عَلَيْهِمُ الْقَتْلُ إِلَى مَضَاجِعِهِمْ ﴾ [آل عمران/ ١٥٤]؛ لأن كينونتهم في بيوتهم وبروزهم إلى مضاجعهم لا علاقة بينهما، ولا يستلزم أحدهما الآخر ولا عدمه. وكقوله - تعالى -: ﴿ قُل لَّوْ كَانَ الْبَحْرُ مِدَادًا لِكَلِمَاتِ رَبِّي لَنَفِدَ الْبَحْرُ قَبْلَ أَن تَنفَدَ كَلِمَتُ رَبِّي ﴾ [الكهف/ ١٠٩] ؛ لأن كون البحر مدادًا لها لا علاقة له بنفادها ولا عدمه. وكقوله - تعالى -: ﴿ وَإِن تَدْعُهُمْ إِلَى الْهُدَى فَلَن يَهْتَدُوا إِذا أَبدا ) [الكهف/ ٥٧]؛ لأن دعاءه إياهم إلى الهدى لا علاقة بينه وبين عدم اهتدائهم . ومن هذا القبيل قولهم : (لو لم يخف الله لم يعصه)؛ لأن عدم خوفه من الله لا أثر له في عدم عصيانه . بل قد يكون سببًا لعصيانه فيما يظهر للناظر . وقد نبهنا في كتابنا (أضواء البيان) على غلط الزمخشري وأبي حيان في آية (وَإِن تَدْعُهُمْ إِلَى الْهُدَى فَلَن يَهْتَدُوا إِذا أَبَدًا ) فقد ظنا هذه الشرطية في هذه الآية لزومية؛ حيث زعما أنه جعل فيها سبب عدم اهتدائهم هو دعوته لهم إلى الهدى والمعنى أن الكفار عندهما جعلوا ما يكون سببًا للاهتداء سببًا لانتفائه، وكل ذلك غلط ؛ لأن الشرطية المذكورة في الآية اتفاقية لا ربط أصلاً بين مقدمها وتاليها . واعلم أن الشرط الحقيقي في الاتفاقية ليس هو المذكور مع الجزاء، بل سبب الجزاء شيء آخرُ غير مذكور معه في الشرطية المذكورة. كقولهم (لو لم يخف الله لم يعصه) فعدم العصيان الذي هو الجزاء في مثل هذا المثل سببه غير مذكور معه فليس سببه عدم الخوف الذي الشرط في المثال المذكور، ولكنه شيء آخر لم يذكر، وهو تعظيمه الله ومحبته له المانعة من معصيته له ، ولو لم يكن خائفا . وكذلك قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ فَلَن يَهْتَدُوا إِذَا أَبَدًا ) سببه الحقيقي غير مذكور معه، فليس هو قوله: ﴿وَإِن تَدْعُهُمْ إِلَى الْهُدَى كما ظنه الزمخشري وأبو حيان وغيرهما، بل سببه هو إرادة الله - تعالى - عدم اهتدائهم على وفق ما سبق في علمه أزلا .
Its non-existence; because there is no relationship between them at all, like your saying: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the human is speaking.' So there is no relationship at all between the rising of the sun and the speech of the human. And among its examples in the Qur'an is the saying of the Exalted: 'If you had been in your houses, those decreed to be killed would have come forth to their beds' [Al Imran 154] because their being in their houses and their coming forth to their beds have no relationship between them, and neither entails the other nor its non-existence. And like His saying the Exalted: 'Say, If the sea were ink for the words of my Lord, the sea would be exhausted before the words of my Lord are exhausted' [Al-Kahf 109] because the sea being ink for them has no relationship with their exhaustion or non-exhaustion. And like His saying the Exalted: 'And if you call them to guidance, they will never be guided' [Al-Kahf 57] because his calling them to guidance has no relationship between it and their not being guided. And of this category is their saying: 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him' because his lack of fear of God has no effect on his not disobeying Him. Rather, it might be a cause for his disobedience as appears to the observer. And we have alerted in our book (Adwa' al-Bayan) to the error of al-Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan concerning the verse 'And if you call them to guidance, they will never be guided' — they thought this conditional in this verse to be implicative; they claimed that in it the cause of their not being guided is his calling them to guidance, and the meaning according to them is that the disbelievers made what is a cause for guidance a cause for its non-existence. All of that is an error because the mentioned conditional in the verse is contingent, with no connection at all between its antecedent and its consequent. And know that the real condition in the contingent is not the one mentioned with the consequent, rather the cause of the consequent is something else not mentioned with it in the mentioned conditional. Like their saying 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him' — so the non-disobedience which is the consequent in such an example, its cause is not mentioned with it; so its cause is not the lack of fear which is the condition in the mentioned example, but it is something else not mentioned, namely his glorification of God and his love for Him which prevents him from disobeying Him, even if he was not afraid. And likewise His saying the Exalted: 'they will never be guided' — its real cause is not mentioned with it, so it is not His saying 'And if you call them to guidance' as al-Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan and others thought, rather its cause is the will of God the Exalted that they not be guided in accordance with what preceded in His knowledge from eternity.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the contingent conditional (al-shartiyya al-ittifāqiyya) — a conditional proposition where there is no causal connection between the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī). The author begins by stating that in a contingent conditional, the non-existence of the consequent does not follow from the antecedent; they are unrelated. The first example is a fabricated statement: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the human is speaking' — there is no natural link between sunrise and human speech. Then the author provides three Quranic examples. The first is from Al Imran 154: 'If you had been in your houses, those decreed to be killed would have come forth to their beds.' Being in one's house does not cause or prevent one's death; the decree is independent. The second is from Al-Kahf 109: 'If the sea were ink for the words of my Lord, the sea would be exhausted before the words of my Lord are exhausted.' The sea's being ink has no effect on the exhaustion of the words; the point is that God's words are infinite. The third is from Al-Kahf 57: 'And if you call them to guidance, they will never be guided.' The Prophet's calling has no causal connection to their lack of guidance; the actual cause is God's will. Next, the author gives a non-Quranic example: 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him.' The lack of fear is not the cause of non-disobedience; in fact, it might lead to disobedience. The real cause is love and reverence for God. The author then critiques al-Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan for misinterpreting the verse from Al-Kahf as a necessary conditional (luzūmiyya), where they thought the antecedent (calling) implied the consequent (non-guidance). The author insists it is contingent: there is no logical connection. Finally, the author explains that in contingent conditionals, the true cause of the consequent is something not mentioned in the conditional statement. Using the example, the true cause of non-disobedience is not lack of fear but rather glorification and love of God. Similarly, the true cause of the disbelievers' non-guidance is not the Prophet's call but God's eternal will that they not be guided. The passage thus corrects a common misunderstanding by distinguishing between apparent conditional form and actual causal connection.

شرطية لزومية
مقدم

Key Takeaway
A contingent conditional has no causal link between its parts; the real cause of the consequent is external to the stated condition.

Quantifiers in Conjunctive and Disjunctive Conditionals
Author classifies quantifiers for conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct quantifier (kullama, da'iman, laysa al-battata, qad yakun) for a given conditional proposition and explain why the quantifier choice depends on the type of conditional and the truth condition (inad for disjunction).

Core Idea
Quantifiers in conditionals use specialized words to show the scope of implication or exclusivity.

In categorical propositions, quantifiers like 'every' and 'some' are straightforward. But in conditional propositions—'if...then' or 'either...or'—the quantifier is expressed through terms like 'kullama' (whenever) and 'da'iman' (always). This lesson unpacks the four quantifiers as they apply to both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, using examples from logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Distinguish conjunctive (mutasila) from disjunctive (munfasila) conditionals. Conjunctive conditionals express implication (if A then B), while disjunctive conditionals express exclusive disjunction (either A or B, where A and B are mutually exclusive, i.e., inad).
In this passage, 'inad' is the mutual incompatibility that makes a universal disjunctive true.
2
Recognize universal affirmative quantifiers
For conjunctive conditionals, universal affirmative quantifiers include 'kullama' (whenever), 'mahma' (whatever), 'mata' (when). For disjunctive conditionals, the universal affirmative quantifier is 'da'iman' (always). These assert that the conditional relation holds for all instances.
Examples: 'Whenever something is human, it is animal' (conjunctive); 'Always, a number is either even or odd' (disjunctive).
3
Recognize universal negative quantifiers
The universal negative quantifier 'laysa al-battata' (not at all) applies to both types. It denies that the conditional ever holds. For disjunctive conditionals, the falsity of the universal negative depends on the absence of inad (mutual exclusivity) between the disjuncts.
Example: 'Not at all if something is human, it is stone' (conjunctive); 'Not at all a thing is either white or cold' (disjunctive) because white and cold can coexist.
4
Recognize particular quantifiers
The particular affirmative quantifier 'qad yakun' (sometimes) applies to both. For conjunctive: 'Sometimes if something is an animal, it is human.' For disjunctive: 'Sometimes a thing is either an animal or a horse.' The particular negative quantifier 'qad la yakun' (sometimes not) also applies to both, with additional specific forms: 'laysa kullama' (not whenever) for conjunctive and 'laysa da'iman' (not always) for disjunctive.
The disjunctive particular affirmative shows that inad holds only in some cases, not universally.

التنبيه الرابع : اعلم أن السور بأقسامه الأربعة يدخل على الشرطيات المتصلة والمنفصلة . فمثال الشرطية المتصلة المسوّرة بسور كلي إيجابي قولك : (كلما كان هذا إنساناً كان حيوانًا) و(مهما كان الشيء مفتقراً في وجوده للفاعل فهو مخلوق)، و(متى كان مخلوقاً فلابد له من خالق). فـ(كلما) و(مهما) و(متى) ونحوها سور كلي إيجابي للشرطية المتصلة . ومثال السور الإيجابي الكلي للشرطية المنفصلة (دائما) وما في معناها، كقولك: (دائما إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو ليس بزوج)، و(دائما إما أن يكون الشيء غنيا عن الخالق وإما أن يكون مخلوقا). ومثال السور الكلي السلبي للمتصلة والمنفصلة معا (ليس البتة) . ومثاله في المتصلة (ليس البتة إذا كان الشيء إنسانًا كان حجرًا) . ومثاله في المنفصلة (ليس البتة إما أن يكون الشيء أبيض وإما أن يكون بارداً) ؛ لأن الصدق فيها بحسب صدق العناد، وهو هنا كاذب؛ إذ لا عناد بين البياض والبرودة ؛ لأن تباينهما تباين مخالفة، فيجوز اجتماعهما كالواقع في الثلج . ومثال السور الجزئي الإيجابي للمتصلة والمنفصلة معا : (قد يكون) . كقولك في المتصلة : (قد يكون إذا كان الشيء حيوانا كان إنسانا) . وكقولك في المنفصلة : (قد يكون إما أن يكون الشيء حيوانا وإما أن يكون فرسا؛ لأنه في حال كون الحيوان غير الفرس العناد يصح وباعتبار صدق الحيوان على الفرس يبطل العناد، فصح أن صدق العناد جزئي . ومثال السور الجزئي السلبي لهما معاً (قد لا يكون)، وللمتصلة فقط (ليس كلما) وللمنفصلة فقط (ليس دائما) . فنقول في المتصلة : (قد لا يكون إذا كان هذا حيوانا كان إنسانًا) . وتقول في المنفصلة : (قد لا يكون إما أن يكون الشيء حيوانا وإما أن يكون إنسانًا ؛ لأنه في بعض الأحوال يكون جامعا بين كونه إنسانًا وحيوانًا، فلا عناد.
The fourth warning: Know that the quantifier with its four divisions applies to conditional propositions, both conjunctive and disjunctive. An example of a conjunctive conditional quantified with a universal affirmative quantifier is your saying: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal', and 'Whatever thing is dependent in its existence on an agent, it is created', and 'Whenever it is created, it must have a creator.' So 'whenever' (kullama), 'whatever' (mahma), and 'when' (mata) and the like are universal affirmative quantifiers for the conjunctive conditional. An example of the universal affirmative quantifier for the disjunctive conditional is 'always' (da'iman) and what is like it, as in your saying: 'Always, the number is either even or not even', and 'Always, a thing is either independent of the Creator or created.' An example of the universal negative quantifier for both conjunctive and disjunctive together is 'not at all' (laysa al-battata). Its example in the conjunctive: 'Not at all if something is a human it is a stone.' Its example in the disjunctive: 'Not at all the thing is either white or cold'; because truth in it is according to the truth of incompatibility (inad), and it is false here, since there is no incompatibility between whiteness and coldness; because their difference is a difference of contrariety (mukhalafa), so their conjunction is permissible, as occurs in snow. An example of the particular affirmative quantifier for both conjunctive and disjunctive together is 'sometimes' (qad yakun). As you say in the conjunctive: 'Sometimes if something is an animal it is a human.' And as you say in the disjunctive: 'Sometimes the thing is either an animal or a horse'; because in the case where the animal is not a horse, incompatibility is valid, and considering the truth of 'animal' for 'horse', incompatibility is invalidated, so the truth of incompatibility is particular. An example of the particular negative quantifier for both together is 'sometimes not' (qad la yakun), and for the conjunctive only 'not whenever' (laysa kullama), and for the disjunctive only 'not always' (laysa da'iman). So we say in the conjunctive: 'Sometimes not if this is an animal it is a human.' And you say in the disjunctive: 'Sometimes not the thing is either an animal or a human'; because in some situations it combines being a human and an animal, so there is no incompatibility.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage classifies how the four types of quantifiers—universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative—apply to conditional propositions, both conjunctive (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). The author, likely Adud al-Din al-Iji, explains by example. For conjunctive conditionals (if-then), universal affirmative quantifiers include words like 'kullama' (whenever), 'mahma' (whatever), and 'mata' (when). For example: 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal'—this is a universal conditional because every instance of the antecedent yields the consequent. For disjunctive conditionals (either-or), universal affirmative quantifiers use 'da'iman' (always), e.g., 'Always, a number is either even or not even'—meaning that for every number, it is either even or odd. Universal negative quantifiers use 'laysa al-battata' (not at all), e.g., 'Not at all if something is a human it is a stone'—meaning there is no true instance where a human is a stone. The author notes that for disjunction, the truth of the conditional depends on 'inad' (mutual exclusivity / incompatibility). For a universal negative disjunctive, the two disjuncts must be mutually exclusive and exhaustive; in his example, 'white' and 'cold' are not incompatible (they can coexist, as in snow), so the universal negative is false. Particular affirmative quantifiers use 'qad yakun' (sometimes), e.g., 'Sometimes if something is an animal it is a human'—some animals are humans. For disjunctive particular affirmative, e.g., 'Sometimes a thing is either an animal or a horse'—there are some cases where the two are mutually exclusive (when the thing is a horse, it's not an animal? Actually, the example is imperfect; the author explains that the truth of the incompatibility is only partial). Particular negative quantifiers use 'qad la yakun' (sometimes not), with specific forms for each type. This detailed classification is essential for analyzing conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) in Islamic logic, as it clarifies the scope of the conditional relation. In Islamic theology (kalam) and legal theory (usul al-fiqh), conditionals are common, e.g., 'If the world is created, then it has a creator'—knowing the quantifier helps determine the strength of the inference.

Five Differences That Prevent Contradiction (5th to 9th)
Author enumerates five additional types of differences that allow two propositions to be both true or both false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the five types of differences (action/potentiality, whole/part, relation, condition, tahsil/udul) applies to a given pair of propositions that are both true.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires exact correspondence; five specific differences break that correspondence.

In logic, two propositions are contradictory if one is true and the other false and they cannot both be true or both false. However, the author explains five special cases where two propositions can be both true or both false because they differ in a specific aspect. This lesson teaches you to recognize these differences, crucial for analyzing statements in Islamic sciences like hadith reconciliation and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify difference in action vs. potentiality
Check whether one proposition refers to the actual state and the other to the potential state. If so, both can be true. Example: 'Wine is intoxicating' (potentially) and 'Wine is not intoxicating' (actually) are both true.
2
Identify difference in whole vs. part
Check whether one proposition refers to the whole subject and the other to a part. Example: 'The black person is white' (teeth) and 'The black person is not white' (rest of body) are both true.
3
Identify difference in relation
Check whether the predicate applies to different relations or contexts. Example: 'Zayd is a father' (to Amr) and 'Zayd is not a father' (to Bakr) are both true.
4
Identify difference in condition
Check whether an implicit condition is attached. Example: 'Zayd enters Paradise' (if he dies in faith) and 'Zayd does not enter Paradise' (if he dies in disbelief) are both true.
5
Identify difference in tahsil vs. 'udul
Check whether negation affects the subject/predicate (ʿudūl) or only the relation (taḥṣīl). A determined negative and a qualified affirmative can both be true.
Tahsil: subject and predicate free from negation. 'Udul: negation dominates subject or predicate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) في المطبوع: يتناقضا)
Fifth: action and potentiality. If one proposition intends action and the other potentiality, both can be true or false. For example: 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating,' meaning potentially, and 'Wine in the jar is not intoxicating,' meaning actually; both are true. If you reverse the intention, both would be false. Sixth: the whole and the part. If they differ in whole and part, both can be true or false. For example: 'The black person is white,' meaning part of him, i.e., his teeth and the white of his eyes, and 'The black person is not white,' meaning the rest of his body. Seventh: relation. If they differ in relation, both can be false and true. For example: 'Zayd is a father' and 'Zayd is not a father,' meaning by the first that he is a father to Amr, and by the second that he is not a father to Bakr. Eighth: condition. If they differ in condition, both can be false and true. For example: 'Zayd enters Paradise' and 'Zayd does not enter Paradise,' meaning by the first on condition that he dies in faith, and by the second on condition that he dies in disbelief. Ninth: both being determined (tahsīl) or qualified (ʿudūl). If one is determined and the other is qualified, they are not contradictory, because the determined negative proposition is true with the true qualified affirmative, as is known in its place, and likewise the determined affirmative is true with the true qualified negative. Thus they say. Footnote (1): Tahsīl is the safety of the two parts of the proposition—subject and predicate—from domination of negation, even if the negation is of the relation. So your saying 'Zayd is a writer' is a determined affirmative, and your saying 'Zayd is not a writer' is a determined negative. As for ʿudūl, it is the domination of negation on the subject or the predicate or both. So your saying 'Zayd is a non-writer' is a qualified affirmative of the predicate, and your saying 'Zayd is not a non-writer' is a qualified negative of the predicate, and your saying 'Non-Zayd is a writer' is a qualified subject, and your saying 'Non-Zayd is a non-writer' is a qualified of both sides. See al-Basāʾir al-Naṣīriyya p. 97, and see the author's words on this in the second section of this book p. 282. Footnote (2): In the print: 'contradict each other.'
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage enumerates five additional types of differences between two propositions that prevent them from being contradictory, building on the earlier eight conditions. The author lists the fifth type: difference in action vs. potentiality (al-fiʿl wa al-quwwa). The example uses wine in a jar: if you say 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating' meaning potentially (i.e., it has the potential to intoxicate), and 'Wine in the jar is not intoxicating' meaning actually (i.e., it is not actually causing intoxication now), both are true. If you reverse the intention, both become false. The sixth type: difference in whole vs. part (al-kull wa al-baʿḍ). The example: 'The black person is white' meaning part of him (teeth, eyes), and 'The black person is not white' meaning the rest of his body; both are true. The seventh type: difference in relation (al-iḍāfa). Example: 'Zayd is a father' and 'Zayd is not a father' can both be true if the first means he is father to Amr and the second means he is not father to Bakr. The eighth type: difference in condition (al-sharṭ). Example: 'Zayd enters Paradise' and 'Zayd does not enter Paradise' can both be true if the first is conditioned on dying in faith and the second on dying in disbelief. The ninth type: difference in being determined (taḥṣīl) vs. qualified (ʿudūl). The footnote explains that taḥṣīl means the subject and predicate are free from the domination of negation, while ʿudūl means negation dominates the subject or predicate. The example shows that a determined negative and a qualified affirmative can both be true.

Key Terms
الفعل والقوة
الكل والبعض
الإضافة
الشرط
التحصيل والعدول

Definition of Logical Syllogism (القياس المنطقي)
Author defines the syllogism and clarifies its conditions by exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a valid logical syllogism and distinguish it from non-syllogistic statements using the definition given by logicians.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a self-contained argument: its premises alone entail the conclusion, with no outside help.

This passage opens the discussion of assents (tasdiqat), which are the primary concern of logic. The author first defines the logical syllogism (qiyas) as a statement composed of two or more propositions that entails another proposition by itself. This definition is crucial because it sets the standard for what counts as a valid deductive argument in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the Arabic term being defined. The passage defines 'al-qiyas al-mantiqi' (the logical syllogism). Notice it is introduced as the primary objective of the art of logic.
2
Break down the definition
The definition has three parts: 'a statement composed of two or more propositions' (genus), 'entails by itself' (condition), 'another proposition' (conclusion). Understand each component. The term 'statement' (qawl) indicates it is a linguistic expression.
The phrase 'by itself' (lidhatihi) is critical: the entailment must come from the internal structure, not from external assumptions.
3
Apply the exclusions
The author excludes: (a) single propositions, even compound modal ones, because they are not composed of two separate premises; (b) conjunctions that do not yield a conclusion, like 'Man is an animal and stone is inanimate'; (c) arguments that require external information to entail a conclusion.
The example of compound modal propositions shows that even if a single proposition is equivalent in meaning to two, it is still one proposition and not a syllogism.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Take a classic kalam argument: 'The world is originated. Everything originated has a creator. Therefore the world has a creator.' Check if it fits the definition: it has two premises, and the conclusion follows by itself. This is a valid syllogism.
In contrast, the statement 'Zayd is praying and Amr is fasting' does not entail any new proposition; it is a simple conjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مقاصد التصديقات وهي القياس المنطقي، وهو المقصود الأصلي من فن المنطق؛ هو عندهم في تحصيل المطالب التصديقية، التي هي العمدة وأشرف من التصورية . والقياس في اللغة مصدر قاس الشيء على الشيء، إذا قدره بقدره . وهو في اصطلاح المنطقيين : قول مؤلف من قضيتين فأكثر على وجه يستلزم لذاته قضية أخرى . فخرج بقولهم مؤلف من قضيتين ماليس بمؤلف، كالقضية الواحدة ولو كانت من الموجّهات المركبة التي هي في قوة قضيتين؛ لأنها تُسمى قضية واحدة، وإن كانت بالتركيب تكون في قوة اثنتين . ودخل بقولهم فأكثر القياس المركب كما سيأتي إيضاحه إن شاء الله . وخرج بقولهم : يستلزم لذاته قضية أخرى مالم يستلزم قضية أصلاً ، كما لو قلت: (الإنسان حيوان والحجر جماد)؛ فإنهما لا يستلزمان قضية . وخرج بقولهم : لذاته ما استلزم قضية أخرى لا لذاته، بل من خارج.
Chapter on the purposes of assents, which is the logical syllogism, and it is the primary objective of the art of logic; in their view, it is for acquiring assertoric (tasdiqī) demands, which are the mainstay and more noble than conceptual (tasawwurī) demands. The syllogism in language is the infinitive of qāsa shay'an 'alā shay', meaning he measured it by its measure. In the terminology of logicians: 'A statement composed of two or more propositions in such a way that it entails by itself another proposition.' Thus, by their saying 'composed of two propositions' excludes what is not composed, like a single proposition even if it is of the compound modal propositions that are equivalent to two propositions; because it is called a single proposition, even if by composition it is equivalent to two. And by their saying 'or more' includes the compound syllogism, as will be clarified later, God willing. And by their saying 'entails by itself another proposition' excludes that which does not entail a proposition at all, such as if you say: 'Man is an animal and stone is inanimate'; for these two do not entail a proposition. And by their saying 'by itself' excludes that which entails another proposition not by itself, but from an external [factor].
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the purposes of assents (tasdiqāt), identifying the logical syllogism (qiyas) as the central aim of logic. The author first notes that logicians consider tasdiqī inquiries superior to conceptual ones. Then he gives the linguistic and terminological definitions of qiyas. The key definition: 'a statement composed of two or more propositions such that it entails by itself another proposition.' The author then clarifies what is included and excluded by each part of the definition. 'Composed of two propositions' excludes single propositions, even compound modal ones that are equivalent to two, because they remain one proposition. 'Or more' includes compound syllogisms (chains). 'Entails by itself another proposition' excludes mere conjunctions that yield no conclusion, like 'Man is an animal and stone is inanimate', which are two independent statements. 'By itself' excludes arguments that require external information to yield a conclusion. In Islamic logic, this definition ensures the syllogism is self-sufficient, as seen in demonstrations of tawḥīd: e.g., 'The world is originated; everything originated has a creator; therefore the world has a creator' is a valid syllogism entailing its conclusion by itself.

Key Takeaway
A logical syllogism is a self-contained argument of two or more premises that yields a conclusion without external aid.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Syllogisms Requiring External Premises or Matter Specificity
Author gives examples of syllogisms needing external premise or matter specificity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish syllogisms that are formally valid from those that rely on an external premise or on the specific content of the premises.

Core Idea
A true conclusion does not guarantee a valid syllogism; formal necessity is what matters.

The author presents two kinds of syllogisms that seem to produce true conclusions but are not logically necessary: those requiring an unstated external premise, and those whose truth depends on the particular subject matter rather than the form. Understanding this distinction is crucial for assessing the strength of arguments in Islamic sciences, such as when evaluating analogies in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the external premise concept
In qiyas al-musawa (syllogism of equality), the conclusion 'Zayd is equal to Bakr' only follows if we assume the external premise: 'What is equal to the equal of something is equal to that something.' Without it, the argument does not necessitate the conclusion.
The same applies to qiyas al-nisfiyya (syllogism of half), requiring 'half of half is half'.
2
Test with a counterexample
The author shows that 'Man is different from horse, horse is different from speaking' does not entail 'Man is different from speaking' because the analogous external premise is false. This demonstrates that without a true external premise, the syllogism fails.
3
Recognize matter-dependent validity
The syllogism 'No man is a stone, no stone is a neighing thing' yields a true conclusion only because of the specific meanings. Replacing 'neighing' with 'speaking' preserves the exact form but gives a false conclusion, proving the truth was material, not formal.
In categorical logic, two negative premises yield no conclusion, so this syllogism is formally invalid.
4
Generalize the principle
A valid syllogism must be such that any substitution preserving the logical form also preserves truth. If a change in matter alone flips the truth value, the argument is not formally valid.
This is a key tool for critiquing arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمثال ما استلزم قضية لا لذاته بل لمقدمة أجنبية : قياس المساواة، كقولك : زيد مساو لعمرو، وعمرو مساوٍ لبكر. فإنه ينتج : زيد مساو لبكر؛ وذلك بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية وهي: مساوي المساوي لشيء مساو لذلك الشيء، فلو لم تكن هناك قضية أجنبية صادقة لم يستلزم شيئًا . كما لو قلت : الإنسان مباين للفرس، والفرس مباين للناطق فلا يستلزم كون الإنسان مبايناً للناطق؛ لأنك لو قلت: مباين المباين لشيء مباين لذلك الشيء لم تصدق؛ لأنه قد يكون غير مباين له، كالمثال المذكور . وكذلك قياس النصفيّة ؛ فإنه لا يستلزم شيئاً، كقولك : الاثنان نصف الأربعة، والأربعة نصف الثمانية، فلا يستلزم أن الاثنين نصفُ الثمانية ؛ لعدم صدق : نصف النصف نصف. ومثال الاستلزام لخصوص المادة قولك : لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، ولا شيء من الحجر بصاهل ، فإنه ينتج : لا شيء من الإنسان بصاهل، وهو صادق لخصوص المادة، لا لذات المقدمتين، بدليل أنك لو جعلت الناطق في الكبرى مكان الصاهل لما كانت صادقة، كما لو قلت: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، ولا شيء من الحجر بناطق فإنه ينتج : لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق وهو كاذب، وهيئة تركيب المقدمتين هي هي، وصدقها في الصاهل وكذبها في الناطق يدل على أن ذلك الصدق إنما لزم المقدمتين لخصوص المادة، لا لذات المقدمتين كماترى .
An example of what requires a proposition not by itself but by an external premise: the syllogism of equality, as when you say: Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr. Then it produces: Zayd is equal to Bakr; and that is by means of an external premise, namely: what is equal to the equal of something is equal to that something. So if there were no true external premise, it would not entail anything. As if you said: Man is different from horse, and horse is different from speaking, then it does not entail man being different from speaking; because if you said: what is different from what is different from something is different from that something, it is not true; for it may not be different from it, as in the mentioned example. Similarly, the syllogism of half: it does not entail anything, as when you say: Two is half of four, and four is half of eight, then it does not entail that two is half of eight, because the truth of 'half of half is half' is lacking. An example of entailment due to specific matter is your saying: Nothing of man is a stone, and nothing of stone is a neighing thing, then it produces: Nothing of man is a neighing thing, and it is true due to the specific matter, not due to the essence of the two premises. Evidence is that if you put 'speaking' in the major premise in place of 'neighing thing', it would not be true. As if you said: Nothing of man is a stone, and nothing of stone is speaking, then it produces: Nothing of man is speaking, and it is false. And the form of the composition of the two premises is the same, and their truth in the case of 'neighing thing' and their falsity in the case of 'speaking' indicates that that truth only attached to the two premises due to the specific matter, not due to the essence of the two premises, as you see.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage illustrates two ways a syllogism may fail to be intrinsically valid: (1) it requires an external premise (qadiyya ajnabiyya) that is not part of the syllogism itself, or (2) its validity depends on the specific subject matter (khhusus al-madda) rather than the logical form. The author first gives the example of the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawa): 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr' yields 'Zayd is equal to Bakr' only if we assume the additional premise 'what is equal to the equal of something is equal to that something.' Without that external premise, the syllogism does not logically necessitate the conclusion. To show this, the author contrasts it with a parallel but invalid syllogism using 'mubayin' (different): 'Man is different from horse, and horse is different from speaking' does not entail 'Man is different from speaking' because the external premise 'what is different from what is different from something is different from that something' is false. Next, the syllogism of half (qiyas al-nisfiyya) is similarly shown not to entail its conclusion without an external premise like 'half of half is half.' The second type is entailment due to specific matter. The example: 'Nothing of man is a stone, and nothing of stone is a neighing thing' yields 'Nothing of man is a neighing thing,' which is true in fact but only because of the particular meanings (man, stone, neighing) not due to the logical form. Replacing 'neighing' with 'speaking' gives a false conclusion, yet the form is identical. Thus the truth was not necessary from the premises alone. The author emphasizes that the logician's qiyas requires formal validity independent of matter.

قياس النصفيّة
مادة

Key Takeaway
Logical validity requires that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises regardless of the specific content; arguments that depend on external premises or particular matter are not formally valid.

The Syllogism

Division of Qiyas and Naming of the Conjunctive Syllogism
Author divides qiyas into two main types and explains naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms, and explain why the conjunctive syllogism is also called hamli and shumuli.

Core Idea
The syllogism splits into two: one for categoricals, one for conditionals; the first has three names.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational division of qiyas into two types: the conjunctive (iqtirani) and the exceptive (istithna'i). He then elaborates on the conjunctive syllogism, giving it three names—iqtirani, hamli, and shumuli—each reflecting a distinct logical property. By understanding this division and these names, you will be better equipped to follow the subsequent discussion of syllogistic forms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary divisions
Locate where the author says 'it is divided into two parts' and note what he calls 'the first part' and 'the second part'.
2
Determine the propositional scope of each
For the first part, note that it occurs in categorical and connected conditional propositions. For the second, note that it occurs only in conditionals (both connected and disjoined) and never in categoricals.
This distinction is crucial for constructing valid syllogisms later.
3
Understand the name iqtirani
It is called 'conjunctive' because its three terms are conjoined without an exceptive particle like 'lakin' (but).
4
Understand the name hamli
It is called 'categorical' (hamli) because categorical propositions (hamliyya) are specific to it.
5
Understand the name shumuli
It is called 'inclusion' because when the minor term falls under the middle and the middle under the major, the minor is included under the major.
This reflects the subsumption logic that makes the syllogism valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت المراد بالقياس عند المنطقيين فاعلم أنه بالتقسيم الأول ينقسم إلى قسمين : القسم الأول : هو المسمى بالقياس الاقتراني، ويسمى القياس الحملي، وقياس الشمول وهو يكون في القضايا الحملية والشرطيات المتصلة . والقسم الثاني : هو المسمى بالقياس الاستثنائي، ويسمى الشرطي، ولا يكون إلا في القضايا الشرطية خاصة، وهو يكون في الشرطيات المتصلة والشرطيات المنفصلة ولا يكون في الحمليات البتة . وإذا علمت هذا فدونك تفاصيل جميعه بإيضاح : اعلم أولاً أن القياس الحملي إنما سمي اقترانيا لاقتران حدوده الثلاثة من غير أن يتخللها حرف استثناء، الذي هو (لكن). وسمي حمليًا لأن الحمليات تختص به ويسمى شموليا لأن الحد الأصغر إذا اندرج في الأوسط واندرج الأوسط في الأكبر لزم اندماج الأصغر في الأكبر وشموله له، كما يأتي ذلك كله قريبا إن شاء الله - تعالى -. إيضاح وقد أردنا هنا أن نبدأ بإيضاح الاقتراني وأقسامه في القضايا الحملية، ونبين أن ذلك يُفهم منه نحوه في الشرطيات المتصلة، ثم
And when you know the intended meaning of qiyas according to the logicians, know that by the primary division it is divided into two parts: The first part: that which is called the conjunctive syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani), and it is called the categorical syllogism (al-qiyas al-hamli), and the syllogism of inclusion (qiyas al-shumul). It occurs in categorical propositions and connected conditionals. The second part: that which is called the exceptive syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i), and it is called the conditional syllogism. It occurs only in conditional propositions particularly, and it occurs in connected conditionals and disjoined conditionals, and it does not occur in categoricals at all. And when you know this, then here is a detailed explanation of all of it: Know first that the categorical syllogism is called conjunctive only because of the conjunction of its three terms without an exceptive particle such as 'but' intervening. And it is called 'hamli' because categorical propositions are specific to it. And it is called 'shumuli' because the minor term, when it falls under the middle term and the middle term falls under the major term, the subsumption of the minor under the major and its inclusion follows, as all of that will come soon, God willing. Clarification: And we intend here to begin with clarifying the conjunctive syllogism and its divisions in categorical propositions, and to show that from that its like in connected conditionals is understood, then...
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the two primary divisions of qiyas (syllogism) as understood by the logicians. The first division is al-qiyas al-iqtirani (conjunctive syllogism), also called al-qiyas al-hamli (categorical syllogism) and qiyas al-shumul (syllogism of inclusion). This type of syllogism occurs both in categorical propositions (hamliyya) and in connected conditionals (shartiyya muttasila). The second division is al-qiyas al-istithna'i (exceptive syllogism), also called al-qiyas al-sharti (conditional syllogism). It occurs only in conditional propositions, both connected (muttasila) and disjoined (munfasila), and never in categoricals. The author then explains the naming of the categorical syllogism: it is called 'iqtirani' because it conjoins three terms without an exceptive particle like 'but' (lakin). It is called 'hamli' because categorical propositions (hamliyya) are proper to it. It is called 'shumuli' because the inclusion relation: when the minor term falls under the middle, and the middle under the major, the minor is included under the major (i.e., the minor term is subsumed). The author announces that the explanation will begin with the conjunctive syllogism in categorical propositions, and then the analog for connected conditionals will be understood from that.

القياس الاستثنائي
القياس الحملي

Key Takeaway
The conjunctive syllogism conjoins three terms without an exceptive particle and covers categoricals and connected conditionals; the exceptive syllogism uses an exceptive particle and covers only conditionals.

The Syllogism
