The Two Main Divisions of Qiyas: Iqtirani and Istithna'i
Author divides qiyas into iqtirani and istithna'i and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the connective syllogism (iqtirani) and the exceptive syllogism (istithna'i) and identify which proposition types each applies to.

Core Idea
Qiyas divides into connective (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i) — each governs different proposition types.

The logicians' first division of qiyas is into two fundamental kinds: the connective syllogism (iqtirani) and the exceptive syllogism (istithna'i). This lesson clarifies their scope based on the passage, showing that each type applies to specific proposition forms—categoricals and conjunctive conditionals for iqtirani, and only conditionals (both conjunctive and disjunctive) for istithna'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
Read the passage and locate the two named sections: al-qiyas al-iqtirani and al-qiyas al-istithna'i. Note their alternative names: iqtirani is also called hamli (categorical) or shumul (inclusion); istithna'i is called sharti (conditional).
These names reveal the nature of each type.
2
Identify the proposition types for each division
For iqtirani, the passage says it occurs in categorical propositions (hamliyya) and conjunctive conditionals (shartiyyat muttasila). For istithna'i, it occurs only in conditional propositions, both conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila), and never in categoricals.
This is the key defining feature.
3
Understand the naming rationale
Iqtirani (connective) means the terms are connected without an exceptive particle; istithna'i (exceptive) requires an exceptive particle like 'lakin' (but) or 'illa' (except). This explains why istithna'i is restricted to conditionals.
The exceptive particle is what makes it 'exceptive'.
4
Classify sample arguments
Practice distinguishing: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is haram.' This is iqtirani because it uses categorical propositions. 'If this is wine, then it is haram. But it is wine. Therefore it is haram.' This is istithna'i because it uses a conditional with an exceptive 'but'.
Apply the rule to each example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت المراد بالقياس عند المنطقيين فاعلم أنه بالتقسيم الأول ينقسم إلى قسمين :
القسم الأول : هو المسمى بالقياس الاقتراني، ويسمى القياس الحملي، وقياس الشمول وهو يكون في القضايا الحملية والشرطيات المتصلة .
والقسم الثاني : هو المسمى بالقياس الاستثنائي، ويسمى الشرطي، ولا يكون إلا في القضايا الشرطية خاصة، وهو يكون في الشرطيات المتصلة والشرطيات المنفصلة ولا يكون في الحمليات البتة .
And if you know what is meant by qiyas according to the logicians, then know that it is divided, by the first division, into two sections: The first section is that which is called the connective syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani), and it is called the categorical syllogism (al-qiyas al-hamli), and the syllogism of inclusion (qiyas al-shumul), and it occurs in categorical propositions and conjunctive conditionals. The second section is that which is called the exceptive syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i), and it is called the conditional (al-sharti), and it occurs only in conditional propositions specifically, and it occurs in conjunctive conditionals and disjunctive conditionals, and it does not occur in categoricals at all.

Commentary
This passage establishes the primary division of qiyas (syllogism) into two fundamental types according to the logicians. The first type is al-qiyas al-iqtirani (connective syllogism), also known as al-qiyas al-hamli (categorical syllogism) or qiyas al-shumul (syllogism of inclusion). This type operates with categorical propositions (qadaya hamliyya) as well as conjunctive conditionals (shartiyyat muttasila). The second type is al-qiyas al-istithna'i (exceptive syllogism), also called al-sharti (conditional), and it is restricted to conditional propositions only. It applies to both conjunctive conditionals and disjunctive conditionals (shartiyyat munfasila) and never to categoricals. The teacher should emphasize the naming rationale: 'iqtirani' comes from 'iqtiran' (connection) because the terms are linked without an exceptive particle, while 'istithna'i' involves an exceptive particle like 'lakin' (but) or 'illa' (except). An example of iqtirani from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, nabidh is haram.' Here, the terms 'intoxicant', 'nabidh', and 'haram' are connected without an exception. An example of istithna'i: 'If this is wine, then it is haram. But it is wine. Therefore, it is haram.' The exceptive 'but' indicates the conditional.

Identifying the Three Terms of a Categorical Syllogism
Author explains the necessity of four words with one repeated, defining the three terms and two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle, minor, and major terms in any categorical syllogism and label the premises correctly.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism has three terms; the middle term appears twice.

This passage breaks down the categorical syllogism into its essential components. Although a syllogism uses two propositions, it actually contains only three distinct terms because the middle term is shared. Understanding these terms is the first step to evaluating any syllogism's validity.

Guided Path
1
Count the terms in the premises
Look at both premises; identify all subjects and predicates. There should be four word-positions, but one word appears twice.
2
Locate the repeated term
Find the word that occurs in both premises; that is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat).
3
Identify the unique terms
The term that appears only in the first premise is the minor term; the term only in the second premise is the major term.
4
Label the premises
The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise; the premise containing the major term is the major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أولاً أنا ذكرنا في تعريف القياس أنه «قول مؤلف من قضيتين .. إلخ، وقد علمت مما مضى (1) أن الحملية مركبة من موضوع ومحمول، فإذن لابد في القياس الاقتراني من أربع كلمات : موضوع القضية الأولى ومحمولها، وموضوع الأخيرة ومحمولها، فهذه أربع كلمات هي: موضوعان ومحمولان.
وهذه الكلمات الأربع لابد أن تكون واحدة منها مكررة؛ بأن تكون هي إحدى كلمتي القضية الأولى، وهي بعينها أيضًا إحدى كلمتي القضية الأخرى، فالكلمات في الحقيقة ثلاث؛ لأن واحدة من الأربع لابد من تكرارها .
فالكلمة المتكررة التي هي جزء كل واحدة من القضيتين هي التي تسمى بالحد الوسط، والكلمة التي تختص بها القضية الأولى هي التي تسمى بالحد الأصغر، والكلمة التي تختص بها القضية الأخيرة هي التي تسمى بالحد الأكبر.
والقضية المجعولة جزءً دليل كما هنا تسمى في اصطلاحهم مقدمة، فالمقدمة المشتملة على الحد الأصغر تسمى في الاصطلاح مقدمة صغرى والمقدمة المشتملة على الحد الأكبر تسمى في الاصطلاح مقدمة كبرى.
(١) ص ٦٥ .
Know first that we mentioned in the definition of qiyas that it is 'a statement composed of two propositions... etc.' And you have already learned from what has passed (1) that the categorical proposition is composed of a subject and a predicate. Therefore, in the connective syllogism there must be four words: the subject of the first proposition and its predicate, and the subject of the last proposition and its predicate. So these are four words: two subjects and two predicates. And among these four words, one must be repeated: such that it is one of the two words of the first proposition, and it is also exactly one of the two words of the other proposition. So the words in reality are three, because one of the four must be repeated. The repeated word, which is a part of each of the two propositions, is called the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). The word that is specific to the first proposition is called the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar). And the word that is specific to the last proposition is called the major term (al-hadd al-akbar). And the proposition made as part of the proof, as here, is called in their terminology a premise (muqaddima). So the premise containing the minor term is called in terminology the minor premise (muqaddimat sughra), and the premise containing the major term is called in terminology the major premise (muqaddimat kubra). (1) p. 65.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed breakdown of the components of a categorical (connective) syllogism. It recalls the definition of qiyas as composed of two propositions. Since categorical propositions consist of a subject and a predicate, a connective syllogism necessarily involves four terms: the subject and predicate of the first premise, and the subject and predicate of the second premise. However, for a valid syllogism, one of these four must be repeated in both premises; otherwise no conclusion follows. Thus the actual distinct terms are three. The repeated term is called the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). The term unique to the first premise is the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar), and the term unique to the second premise is the major term (al-hadd al-akbar). The premises themselves are named accordingly: the minor premise contains the minor term, and the major premise contains the major term. The teacher should illustrate with an Islamic example: For the syllogism 'Every intoxicant is haram; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is haram', the terms are: 'nabidh' (minor), 'intoxicant' (middle), 'haram' (major). The first premise 'Every intoxicant is haram' is the major premise; the second 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' is the minor premise. Note that the minor term is the subject of the conclusion, and the major term is the predicate.

Key Terms

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Three Terms of the Categorical Syllogism
Author defines the three terms and their functions in a categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three terms (minor, middle, major) in any categorical syllogism and correctly label the premises as minor and major.

Core Idea
A categorical syllogism uses exactly three terms: one repeated as the middle link.

In this passage, the author breaks down the structure of a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). You will learn how to spot the three essential terms—the minor, the middle, and the major—and understand why one term must be repeated. This is the foundation for evaluating whether a syllogism is valid.

Guided Path
1
List the four terms from the two premises
Each premise has a subject and a predicate, giving four words total. Write them down.
Ignore quantifiers like 'every'.
2
Identify the repeated term
The word that appears in both premises is the middle term (al-hadd al-wasat). It links the two premises.
3
Determine the minor and major terms
The term unique to the first premise is the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar); the term unique to the second premise is the major term (al-hadd al-akbar).
4
Label the premises
The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (muqaddima sughra); the premise containing the major term is the major premise (muqaddima kubra).
5
Form the conclusion
In the conclusion, the minor term becomes the subject and the major term the predicate. The middle term is omitted.
Example: from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is sentient', the conclusion is 'Every human is sentient'.

نعقد فصلاً مستقلاً للقياس الاستثنائي، فنوضح فيه جميع أقسامه اعلم أولاً أنا ذكرنا في تعريف القياس أنه «قول مؤلف من قضيتين .. إلخ، وقد علمت مما مضى أن الحملية مركبة من موضوع ومحمول، فإذن لابد في القياس الاقتراني من أربع كلمات : موضوع القضية الأولى ومحمولها، وموضوع الأخيرة ومحمولها، فهذه أربع كلمات هي: موضوعان ومحمولان. وهذه الكلمات الأربع لابد أن تكون واحدة منها مكررة؛ بأن تكون هي إحدى كلمتي القضية الأولى، وهي بعينها أيضًا إحدى كلمتي القضية الأخرى، فالكلمات في الحقيقة ثلاث؛ لأن واحدة من الأربع لابد من تكرارها . فالكلمة المتكررة التي هي جزء كل واحدة من القضيتين هي التي تسمى بالحد الوسط، والكلمة التي تختص بها القضية الأولى هي التي تسمى بالحد الأصغر، والكلمة التي تختص بها القضية الأخيرة هي التي تسمى بالحد الأكبر. والقضية المجعولة جزءً دليل كما هنا تسمى في اصطلاحهم مقدمة، فالمقدمة المشتملة على الحد الأصغر تسمى في الاصطلاح مقدمة صغرى والمقدمة المشتملة على الحد الأكبر تسمى في الاصطلاح مقدمة كبرى. والحد الأصغر هو موضوع النتيجة دائماً، والحد الأكبر هو محمولها، والحد الوسط يُلغى عند الإنتاج فيحذف، وتكون النتيجة متآلفة من الأصغر والأكبر، فالأصغر موضوعها، والأكبر محمولها . فلو قلت مثلاً : كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان حساس). فهذا قياس اقتراني يتألف من ثلاث كلمات : الأولى (الإنسان)، وقد اختصت بها المقدمة الأولى، فلفظة (الإنسان) في هذا المثال هي الحد الأصغر، والمقدمة المشتملة عليها التي هي (كل إنسان حيوان) هي المقدمة الصغرى . والثانية (الحيوان)، وهي متكررة ؛ لأنها في هذا المثال محمول في الأولى، وموضوع في الأخرى ، فلفظة (الحيوان) في هذا المثال هي المسمى بالحد الوسط . والثالثة (الحساس)، وقد اختصـت بهـا المقـدمـة الأخيرة، و (الحساس) في هذا المثال هو الحد الأكبر، والمقدمة المشتملة عليه هي المقدمة الكبرى التي هي كل حيوان حساس). أما لفظة (كل) في المقدمتين فإنما هي سور، جيىء به لبيان كمية الأفراد المحكوم عليها، فليست موضوعا - كما قدمنا ايضاحه ـ وإن أعربها النحويون مبتدأ، ولذا لم نَعدَّها في المثال المذكور من الكلمات ؛ لأنها ليست بمحمول ولا موضوع، وإنما هي لفظ جییء به
We will dedicate a separate chapter for the conditional syllogism, explaining all its divisions. Know first that we mentioned in the definition of qiyas that it is 'a statement composed of two propositions... etc., and you have already learned from what has passed that the categorical proposition is composed of a subject and a predicate. So then in the categorical syllogism there must be four words: the subject of the first proposition and its predicate, and the subject of the last and its predicate. These are four words: two subjects and two predicates. And these four words must have one of them repeated; such that it is one of the two words of the first proposition, and it is exactly also one of the two words of the other proposition. So the words in reality are three; because one of the four must be repeated. The repeated word that is a part of each of the two propositions is called the middle term. The word that is specific to the first proposition is called the minor term. And the word that is specific to the last proposition is called the major term. The proposition made a part of the proof, as here, is called in their terminology a premise. So the premise containing the minor term is called in terminology the minor premise, and the premise containing the major term is called in terminology the major premise. The minor term is always the subject of the conclusion, and the major term is its predicate. The middle term is cancelled at the time of production, so it is omitted. The conclusion is composed of the minor and the major; so the minor is its subject, and the major is its predicate. For example, if you say: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient.' This is a categorical syllogism composed of three words: the first is 'human', which is specific to the first premise, so the word 'human' in this example is the minor term, and the premise containing it, which is 'Every human is an animal', is the minor premise. The second is 'animal', and it is repeated because it is in this example the predicate in the first and the subject in the other, so the word 'animal' in this example is called the middle term. The third is 'sentient', which is specific to the last premise, and 'sentient' in this example is the major term, and the premise containing it is the major premise, which is 'Every animal is sentient'. As for the word 'every' in the two premises, it is a quantifier, brought to indicate the quantity of the individuals judged, so it is not a subject — as we have previously clarified — even if grammarians parse it as a subject. Therefore we did not count it in the mentioned example among the words; because it is not a predicate nor a subject, but it is a word brought for another purpose.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the three essential terms of a categorical syllogism and their respective roles. The author begins by recalling that a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) consists of two premises, each a categorical proposition with a subject and predicate, giving four terms in total: the subject and predicate of the first premise, and the subject and predicate of the second premise. However, for a valid syllogism, one of these four must be repeated—the middle term—so effectively there are only three distinct terms. The repeated term is called the middle term (had al-wasat), the term unique to the first premise is the minor term (had al-asghar), and the term unique to the second premise is the major term (had al-akbar). The premises are named accordingly: the one containing the minor term is the minor premise (muqaddima sughra), and the one containing the major term is the major premise (muqaddima kubra). In the conclusion, the minor term becomes the subject, the major term becomes the predicate, and the middle term is omitted. The author provides a concrete example: 'Every human is an animal; every animal is sentient.' Here, 'human' is the minor term, 'animal' is the middle term, and 'sentient' is the major term. The word 'every' (kul) is a quantifier (sūr) indicating universality, not a logical term; hence it is not counted among the three terms. This distinction between logical terms and grammatical particles is crucial for understanding the structure of a syllogism.

Key Takeaway
A categorical syllogism has exactly three terms: the minor, middle, and major; the middle term is repeated in both premises and omitted in the conclusion.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Three Terms of the Categorical Syllogism
Author defines minor, major, and middle terms with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism and label the minor and major premises.

Core Idea
The three terms are defined by the conclusion: minor = subject, major = predicate, middle = omitted.

In this passage, the author explains the three essential terms of a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). Understanding these terms is crucial because every valid syllogism in Islamic logic—whether used in kalam, fiqh, or usul—depends on correctly identifying them. The author provides a clear example to show how the terms relate to the premises and the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Look for the proposition that is produced by the syllogism. It will be composed of two terms: a subject and a predicate.
In the passage, the conclusion is 'Every human is sensitive'.
2
Find the minor term
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion. In the example, 'human' is the subject of the conclusion.
3
Find the major term
The major term is the predicate of the conclusion. In the example, 'sensitive' is the predicate.
4
Locate the middle term
The middle term appears in both premises but is absent from the conclusion. It is the term common to both premises. In the example, 'animal' appears in both premises.
5
Label the premises
The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise; the premise containing the major term is the major premise. In the example, the first premise contains 'human' (minor term) so it is the minor premise; the second premise contains 'sensitive' (major term) so it is the major premise.

The minor term is always the subject of the conclusion, and the major term is its predicate, and the middle term is cancelled at the time of production and thus omitted. The conclusion is composed of the minor and major terms, so the minor is its subject and the major is its predicate. For example, if you say: 'Every human is an animal and every animal is sensitive.' This is a categorical syllogism composed of three terms: the first is 'human', which is exclusive to the first premise, so the word 'human' in this example is the minor term, and the premise containing it, which is 'Every human is an animal', is the minor premise. The second is 'animal', which is repeated because in this example it is a predicate in the first and a subject in the other, so the word 'animal' in this example is called the middle term. The third is 'sensitive', which is exclusive to the last premise, and 'sensitive' in this example is the major term, and the premise containing it is the major premise, which is 'Every animal is sensitive'. As for the word 'every' in the two premises, it is a quantifier, brought to indicate the quantity of the individuals judged, so it is not a subject – as we have previously clarified – even if grammarians parse it as a subject. Therefore we did not count it among the terms in the mentioned example, because it is neither a predicate nor a subject, but rather a word brought to indicate the quantity of the individuals of the subject judged by the predicate. And if you consider this example we gave for the categorical syllogism, which is our statement: 'Every human is an animal and every animal is sensitive', and you know that 'human' is the minor term, 'sensitive' is the major term, and 'animal' is the middle term, then know that the mentioned syllogism produces: 'Every human is sensitive'. So 'human', which is the minor term, becomes the subject of this conclusion, and 'sensitive', which is the major term, becomes its predicate. And 'animal', which is the middle term, is cancelled and omitted at the time of production. And know that the mentioned words are called 'terms' only because they are the ends and limits of the syllogism, and the limit of a thing is its end and boundary. And know that the criterion of the categorical syllogism is that it contains the conclusion potentially and not actually, i.e., it contains its matter without its form. For your statement 'Every human is an animal and every animal is sensitive' is a categorical syllogism that produces 'Every human is sensitive', and this expression, which is 'Every human is sensitive', was not mentioned in this syllogism in its form, but rather in its matter, because 'human' is mentioned in the minor premise and 'sensitive' is mentioned in the major premise, and a thing exists with its matter potentially before obtaining its form. And if you know this, then know that the categorical syllogism is limited in its figures – that is

Commentary
This passage is an expository explanation of the structure of a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). The author begins by defining the three terms: the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar) is always the subject of the conclusion, the major term (al-hadd al-akbar) is the predicate of the conclusion, and the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) is cancelled and omitted in the conclusion. The conclusion is thus composed solely of the minor and major terms. The author then provides a concrete example: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sensitive.' He identifies 'human' as the minor term because it appears in the first premise (the minor premise) and becomes the subject of the conclusion. 'Animal' is the middle term because it appears in both premises—once as predicate in the minor and once as subject in the major—and is not present in the conclusion. 'Sensitive' is the major term because it appears in the second premise (the major premise) and becomes the predicate of the conclusion. The author clarifies that the word 'every' (kull) is a quantifier (saur) indicating the universality of the judgment; it is not a term in the syllogism, even though grammarians treat it as a subject. Next, the author explains that the conclusion 'Every human is sensitive' is contained potentially (bil-quwwa) in the syllogism, not actually (bil-fi'l). The syllogism contains the matter (madda) of the conclusion—the subject and predicate—but not the form (sura), which is the judgment that links them. The matter is present in the premises: 'human' in the minor and 'sensitive' in the major. The conclusion emerges when the middle term is removed and the connection becomes explicit. Finally, the author announces that the categorical syllogism has a limited number of figures (ashkal), which sets up the next topic. This passage is foundational for understanding how syllogistic reasoning works in Islamic logic, and it applies directly to legal and theological arguments, where terms are clearly identified to produce certain conclusions.

The Four Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Author defines syllogism terms and enumerates four figures with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism by locating the position of the middle term and produce examples of each figure from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The four figures are determined solely by where the middle term sits – no fifth possibility.

In this passage, the author introduces the four possible configurations of the middle term that define every categorical syllogism. Understanding these figures is the first step to evaluating whether a syllogism is valid. The examples here use terms like 'human', 'animal', and 'stone', but the same patterns apply directly to arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms: minor, major, middle
In the example 'Every human is an animal, every animal is sensitive': human is the subject of the conclusion (minor), sensitive is the predicate (major), animal appears in both premises (middle). The conclusion 'Every human is sensitive' drops the middle term.
The minor term is also the subject of the minor premise; the major is predicate of the major premise.
2
Determine the position of the middle term in each premise
Check whether the middle term is the subject or predicate in the first premise (minor) and in the second premise (major). In the example, 'animal' is predicate in the minor ('Every human is animal') and subject in the major ('Every animal is sensitive').
Use the author's definition: the middle term is the one that connects the premises.
3
Compare the positions to the four figure rules
The author lists four possibilities: (1) predicate-minor, subject-major (first figure); (2) predicate in both (second); (3) subject in both (third); (4) subject-minor, predicate-major (fourth). Match your observation to one of these.
No fifth possibility is possible because each premise gives only two positions.
4
Identify the figure and check the example
The example fits the first figure (middle predicate in minor, subject in major). The author calls it 'clear production' because it directly yields a valid conclusion without conversion.
Second and third figures require special conditions for validity (e.g., a negative or particular premise).
5
Practice with a new example from Islamic science
Take 'Every intoxicant is prohibited; every nabidh is an intoxicant.' Identify the terms: minor = nabidh, major = prohibited, middle = intoxicant. The middle is predicate in the minor and subject in the major? Actually: 'nabidh is intoxicant' has middle as predicate? Wait – 'every nabidh is an intoxicant' has 'intoxicant' as predicate. 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' has 'intoxicant' as subject. So it's first figure. Alternatively, use a second figure example: 'Every human is an animal; no stone is an animal' gives second figure.
Use Kalam or Fiqh examples to ensure Islamic relevance.

لبيان الكمية المحكوم عليها من أفراد الموضوع بالمحمول. وإذا نظرنا في هذا المثال الذي ذكرنا للقياس الاقتراني، الذي هو قولنا : كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان حساس)، وعرفت أن (الإنسان) حد أصغر، و(الحساس) حدٌ أكبر، و(الحيوان) حد وسط، فاعلم أن القياس المذكور ينتج: (كل إنسان حساس)، فصار (الإنسان) الذي هو الحد الأصغر موضوع هذه النتيجة، و (الحساس) الذي هو الحد الأكبر محمولها. و(الحيوان) الذي هو الحد الوسط ألغي وحذف لدى الإنتاج. واعلم أن الكلماتِ المذكورة إنما سميت حدودًا لأنها أطراف القياس ومنتهاه، وحد الشيء طرفه ومنتهاه . واعلم أن ضابط القياس الاقتراني هو أنه يشتمل على النتيجة بالقوة دون الفعل، بأن يكون مشتملاً على مادتها دون صورتها . فقولك : كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان حساس)، قياس اقتراني، يُنتج كل إنسان حساس، وهذا اللفظ الذي هو : (كل إنسان حساس) لم يكن مذكورًا في هذا القياس بصورته، بل بمادته؛ لأن (الإنسان) مذكور في المقدمة الصغرى، و(الحساس) مذكور في الكبرى، والشيء يوجد مع مادته بالقوة قبل حصول صورته . وإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم أن القياس الاقتراني تنحصر أشكاله ـ أي هيئاته التي يأتي عليها - في أربعة أشكال ؛ وذلك بحسب هيئات الحد الوسط ؛ فإنها محصورة في أربع حصرًا قطعيا ؛ لأنه لابد له من واحدة من أربع حالات، لا خامسة لها البتة : الأولى: أن يكون الحد الوسط محمولاً في الصغرى موضوعاً في الكبرى . الثانية : أن يكون محمولاً فيهما . الثالثة : أن يكون موضوعًا فيهما . الرابعة : أن يكون موضوعًا في الصغرى محمولاً في الكبرى. ولا خامسة البتة كما ترى . فإن كان الحد الوسط محمولاً في الصغرى موضوعا في الكبرى فهو المسمى بالشكل الأول، ويعرف ببين الإنتاج، ومثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس، يُنتج من الشكل الأول : (كل إنسان حساس). وإن كان الحد الوسط محمولاً فيهما فهو المسمى بالشكل الثاني ومثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان)، ينتج من الشكل الثاني : (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر) . وإن كان الحد الوسط موضوعًا فيهما فهو المسمى بالشكل الثالث ومثاله كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق)، ينتج من الشكل الثالث : بعض الحيوان ناطق .
To explain the quantity of what is predicated of the individuals of the subject by the predicate. And when we consider this example we gave for the categorical syllogism, which is our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sensitive,' and you know that 'human' is the minor term, 'sensitive' is the major term, and 'animal' is the middle term, then know that the mentioned syllogism produces: 'Every human is sensitive.' So 'human,' which is the minor term, became the subject of this conclusion, and 'sensitive,' which is the major term, its predicate. And 'animal,' which is the middle term, was removed and deleted upon the production. And know that the mentioned words are only called 'limits' because they are the sides and endpoints of the syllogism, and the limit of a thing is its side and endpoint. And know that the criterion of the categorical syllogism is that it contains the conclusion potentially, not actually, by containing its matter without its form. So your statement: 'Every human is an animal and every animal is sensitive' is a categorical syllogism that produces 'Every human is sensitive,' and this phrase which is 'Every human is sensitive' was not mentioned in this syllogism in its form, but in its matter; because 'human' is mentioned in the minor premise, and 'sensitive' is mentioned in the major premise, and a thing exists with its matter potentially before its form is obtained. And if you have understood this, then know that the categorical syllogism – its figures, i.e., the configurations it takes – are restricted to four figures; and that according to the configurations of the middle term, for they are restricted to four with a definitive restriction, because it must have one of four states, no fifth for it at all: First: that the middle term be a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major. Second: that it be a predicate in both. Third: that it be a subject in both. Fourth: that it be a subject in the minor and a predicate in the major. And no fifth at all, as you see. So if the middle term is a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major, it is called the first figure, and known for clear production, and its example: (Every human is an animal, and every animal is sensitive, producing from the first figure: 'Every human is sensitive'). And if the middle term is a predicate in both, it is called the second figure, and its example: (Every human is an animal, and no stone is an animal, producing from the second figure: 'No human is a stone'). And if the middle term is a subject in both, it is called the third figure, and its example: (Every human is an animal, and every human is rational, producing from the third figure: 'Some animal is rational').
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the structure of the categorical syllogism and enumerates its four figures. The author begins by revisiting the example used earlier: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is sensitive.' He identifies the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar) as 'human,' the major term (al-hadd al-akbar) as 'sensitive,' and the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) as 'animal.' The conclusion 'Every human is sensitive' has the minor term as subject and major term as predicate; the middle term disappears. The author then explains why these terms are called 'hudud' (limits): they are the ends or boundaries of the syllogism. Next, he clarifies the key nature of a categorical syllogism: it contains the conclusion potentially (bil-quwwa), not actually, meaning it holds the matter (the terms) but not the form (the conclusion as a statement). The premises have the terms scattered; the conclusion combines them. This distinguishes a syllogism from a mere recitation of the conclusion. After this groundwork, the author announces that the categorical syllogism has exactly four figures, based solely on the position of the middle term in the two premises. He lists the four possibilities: (1) middle term predicate in minor, subject in major (first figure); (2) predicate in both (second); (3) subject in both (third); (4) subject in minor, predicate in major (fourth). He states these are exhaustive, no fifth. Then he names each figure and provides an example. The first figure example is the one already given. The second figure example uses a universal negative: 'Every human is an animal, no stone is an animal, therefore no human is a stone.' The third figure example uses two universal affirmatives: 'Every human is an animal, every human is rational, therefore some animal is rational.' Note that the third figure requires a particular conclusion. The author does not give an example of the fourth figure here, but the pattern is clear. This passage is fundamental for understanding how to classify syllogisms by figure, which is essential for evaluating validity.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Four Figures of the Syllogism
Author enumerates the four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term and determine which figure a given syllogism belongs to.

Core Idea
The middle term's position determines the figure; only the first figure is immediately productive.

In this passage, the author systematizes the categorical syllogism into four figures by examining where the middle term appears in the premises. Understanding these figures is essential for evaluating whether a syllogism is valid and for reducing non-first-figure syllogisms to the first figure. The examples provided illustrate each figure using terms like human, animal, and stone, which are common in logical textbooks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat).
In the example 'Every human is an animal, every animal is sensitive, therefore every human is sensitive', the middle term is 'animal'.
2
Check the minor premise
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion. Determine whether the middle term is the subject or predicate in the minor premise.
In the example, the minor premise is 'Every human is an animal'. The middle term 'animal' is the predicate.
3
Check the major premise
The major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion. Determine whether the middle term is the subject or predicate in the major premise.
In the example, the major premise is 'Every animal is sensitive'. The middle term 'animal' is the subject.
4
Match to figure
Combine the positions: (predicate in minor, subject in major) = first figure; (predicate in both) = second; (subject in both) = third; (subject in minor, predicate in major) = fourth.
If there is no fifth possibility, as the author says.
5
Verify with example
Check the given example for that figure to confirm your identification. The first figure is called 'evident in production' because the conclusion follows directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هيئاته التي يأتي عليها - في أربعة أشكال ؛ وذلك بحسب هيئات الحد الوسط ; فإنها محصورة في أربع حصرًا قطعيا ; لأنه لابد له من واحدة من أربع حالات، لا خامسة لها البتة : الأولى: أن يكون الحد الوسط محمولاً في الصغرى موضوعاً في الكبرى . الثانية : أن يكون محمولاً فيهما . الثالثة : أن يكون موضوعًا فيهما . الرابعة : أن يكون موضوعًا في الصغرى محمولاً في الكبرى. ولا خامسة البتة كما ترى . فإن كان الحد الوسط محمولاً في الصغرى موضوعا في الكبرى فهو المسمى بالشكل الأول، ويعرف ببين الإنتاج، ومثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس، يُنتج من الشكل الأول : (كل إنسان حساس). وإن كان الحد الوسط محمولاً فيهما فهو المسمى بالشكل الثاني ومثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان)، ينتج من الشكل الثاني : (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر) . وإن كان الحد الوسط موضوعًا فيهما فهو المسمى بالشكل الثالث ومثاله كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق)، ينتج من الشكل الثالث : بعض الحيوان ناطق . وإن كان موضوعًا في الصغرى محمولاً في الكبرى فهو المسمى بالشكل الرابع، وهو أبعدها من الشكل الأول الذي هو بين الإنتاج. ومثاله : كل إنسان حيوان وكل ناطق (إنسان)، يُنتج من الشكل الرابع: (بعض الحيوان ناطق).
Its configurations that it comes upon - in four figures; and that according to the configurations of the middle term; for it is confined to four, a definitive confinement, because it must be in one of four states, with no fifth whatsoever: First: that the middle term be predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. Second: that it be predicate in both. Third: that it be subject in both. Fourth: that it be subject in the minor and predicate in the major. And there is no fifth whatsoever as you see. For if the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, it is called the first figure, and it is known as evident in production, and its example: (Every human is an animal, and every animal is sensitive, produces from the first figure: (Every human is sensitive). And if the middle term is predicate in both, it is called the second figure, and its example: (Every human is an animal and no stone is an animal), produces from the second figure: (No human is a stone). And if the middle term is subject in both, it is called the third figure, and its example: (Every human is an animal and every human is rational), produces from the third figure: (Some animal is rational). And if it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, it is called the fourth figure, and it is the farthest from the first figure which is evident in production. And its example: (Every human is an animal and every rational is a human), produces from the fourth figure: (Some animal is rational).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term in the two premises. The author begins by stating that the configurations of the syllogism come in four figures, and the confinement to four is definitive because the middle term must be in one of four possible positions: predicate in minor and subject in major (first figure), predicate in both (second), subject in both (third), or subject in minor and predicate in major (fourth). No fifth possibility exists. Then he defines each figure and gives an example. The first figure is called 'evident in production' because the conclusion follows directly without any conversion or reduction. For example, from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is sensitive' we deduce 'Every human is sensitive'. The second figure has the middle term predicate in both premises; e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'No stone is an animal' yields 'No human is a stone'. The third figure has the middle term subject in both; e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational' gives 'Some animal is rational'. The fourth figure has the middle term subject in the minor and predicate in the major; e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational is a human' produces 'Some animal is rational'. The author notes that the fourth figure is farthest from the first in terms of evidentness. The examples use universal terms like human, animal, stone, sensitive, and rational, which are common in logical textbooks.

Understanding Syllogistic Moods (Dharub)
Author defines moods as premise combinations and calculates the sixteen possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of possible moods for any given figure and distinguish between productive, sterile, and repetitive moods.

Core Idea
Moods are combinations of quantity and quality; only a subset yields valid conclusions.

The author introduces moods as the specific combinations of quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) that can appear in the premises of a syllogism. Understanding how these moods are generated is essential for later determining which syllogisms are valid. This lesson breaks down the combinatorial logic behind the sixteen moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of categorical propositions
Each premise can be one of four types: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O). This classification combines quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative).
These are the standard four forms in Aristotelian logic.
2
Calculate the number of possible minor premise types
The minor premise has 4 possibilities (A, E, I, O). Similarly, the major premise also has 4 possibilities.
3
Multiply the minor and major possibilities
Total possible combinations in any figure = 4 (minor) × 4 (major) = 16. This is how the author arrives at 'sixteen moods' per figure.
Some combinations are repetitive (same mood after conversion) and some are sterile (no valid conclusion).
4
Identify sterile and repetitive moods
Not all 16 combinations produce a valid conclusion. Those that do are 'productive'; the rest are 'sterile' or 'repetitive'. The author will only discuss productive moods.
This lesson sets the stage for the conditions that filter out sterile moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الصور الداخلة تحت الأشكال الأربعة باعتبار الكم والكيف تسمى ضروباً، فكل شكل مشتمل على ستة عشر ضرباً؛ لأن صغرى مقدمتي الشكل إما أن تكون كلية وإما أن تكون جزئية، وفي كل منهما إما أن تكون سالبة وإما أن تكون موجبة، فصورها أربعة من ضرب حالتي الكم في حالتي الكيف، وهذه الصور الأربعة في المقدمة الكبرى أيضاً، فتضرب حالات الصغرى الأربع في حالات الكبرى الأربع فيكون المجموع ستة عشر ضربًا ، منها المتكرر ومنها العقيم. قصدنا هنا أن نبين شروط الإنتاج وعدد الضروب المنتجة في كل شكل بأمثلتها، ونترك ذكر الضروب العقيمة والمتكررة اختصاراً . اعلم أن الشكل الأول الذي هو أفضل الأشكال وأظهرها نتيجة يُشترط لإنتاجه شرطان (۱) : الأول : بحسب الكيف، وهو كون صغراه موجبة . الثاني : بحسب الكم، وهو كون كبراه كلية . والشرطان المذكوران لا ينطبقان إلا على أربع صور وبه تعلم أن (١) سيأتي ص ١١٤، ١١٥ بيان وجه اشتراطهما .
And know that the forms falling under the four figures with respect to quantity and quality are called moods, so each figure contains sixteen moods; because the minor premise of the figure is either universal or particular, and in each case either negative or affirmative, so its forms are four from multiplying the two states of quantity by the two states of quality, and these four forms are also in the major premise, so the four states of the minor are multiplied by the four states of the major, giving a total of sixteen moods, among them repetitive and sterile. Our aim here is to state the conditions of productivity and the number of productive moods in each figure with their examples, and we omit mentioning the sterile and repetitive moods for brevity. Know that the first figure, which is the best of the figures and the clearest in result, requires for its productivity two conditions (1): First: with respect to quality, which is that its minor be affirmative. Second: with respect to quantity, which is that its major be universal. And the mentioned conditions apply only to four forms, and by this you know that (1) the explanation of the reason for their being conditions will come on pages 114, 115.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of moods (dharub) and the conditions for a productive syllogism in the first figure. The author begins by defining moods: the forms that belong to the four figures when we consider the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the premises. Each figure potentially has sixteen moods, because each premise can be one of four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. Multiplying the four possibilities of the minor premise by the four of the major gives sixteen. Among these, some are repetitive (because they yield the same mood after conversion) and some are sterile (not producing a valid conclusion). The author states that he will only discuss the productive moods and their conditions. Then he focuses on the first figure, which is the best and clearest. It has two necessary conditions for productivity: the minor premise must be affirmative (quality condition) and the major premise must be universal (quantity condition). These two conditions restrict the valid moods of the first figure to only four specific combinations. The author notes that the justification for these conditions will be given later (on pages 114-115). This passage sets the stage for a detailed enumeration of valid moods in each figure, emphasizing that only a subset of the theoretical sixteen moods actually yield valid conclusions.

The Syllogism

Conditions for the First Figure Productivity
Author states the two conditions for first figure productivity and notes they restrict valid moods to four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two conditions for the first figure to determine whether a given categorical syllogism is productive.

Core Idea
The first figure requires a minor affirmative and a major universal to yield a necessary conclusion.

After explaining moods in general, the author focuses on the first figure—the most clear and excellent—and states two essential conditions for its productivity. These conditions reduce the valid moods to exactly four. Understanding these conditions is crucial for anyone using categorical syllogisms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises of the syllogism
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion; the major premise contains the predicate. In a first-figure syllogism, the middle term is the subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
Example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (major), 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor).
2
Check the quality condition: the minor must be affirmative
If the minor premise is negative, the syllogism is sterile regardless of other factors. Ensure the minor is either universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I).
This condition prevents the middle term from being undistributed.
3
Check the quantity condition: the major must be universal
The major premise must be either universal affirmative (A) or universal negative (E). A particular major (I or O) will not yield a valid conclusion in the first figure.
This condition ensures that the major term is distributed properly.
4
Determine if the syllogism is productive
If both conditions are satisfied (minor affirmative and major universal), the syllogism is productive. Otherwise, it is sterile and cannot produce a necessary conclusion.
These two conditions together limit the first figure to four valid moods: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO (in standard notation).

Know that the first figure, which is the best of the figures and the clearest in result, requires for its productivity two conditions (1): First: with respect to quality, which is that its minor be affirmative. Second: with respect to quantity, which is that its major be universal. And the mentioned conditions apply only to four forms, and by this you know that (1) the explanation of the reason for their being conditions will come on pages 114, 115.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of moods (dharub) and the conditions for a productive syllogism in the first figure. The author begins by defining moods: the forms that belong to the four figures when we consider the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the premises. Each figure potentially has sixteen moods, because each premise can be one of four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. Multiplying the four possibilities of the minor premise by the four of the major gives sixteen. Among these, some are repetitive (because they yield the same mood after conversion) and some are sterile (not producing a valid conclusion). The author states that he will only discuss the productive moods and their conditions. Then he focuses on the first figure, which is the best and clearest. It has two necessary conditions for productivity: the minor premise must be affirmative (quality condition) and the major premise must be universal (quantity condition). These two conditions restrict the valid moods of the first figure to only four specific combinations. The author notes that the justification for these conditions will be given later (on pages 114-115). This passage sets the stage for a detailed enumeration of valid moods in each figure, emphasizing that only a subset of the theoretical sixteen moods actually yield valid conclusions.

Defining the Fourth Figure of the Syllogism
Author defines the fourth figure and gives an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of a categorical syllogism and distinguish it from the first figure based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The fourth figure is the most remote from the first in clarity, yet it can still yield valid conclusions.

In the previous lessons, you learned about the first three figures of the syllogism. Now we turn to the fourth figure, which is defined by a specific arrangement: the middle term is the subject in the minor premise and the predicate in the major premise. The author emphasizes that this figure is the farthest from the first figure in terms of evident productivity. Nevertheless, it has its own valid moods, as we will see through an example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In the example 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational is a human', find which term appears as subject in the minor and predicate in the major. That term is 'human'. Minor premise: subject 'human', predicate 'animal'; major premise: subject 'rational', predicate 'human'. So 'human' is the middle term.
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
2
Verify the figure
Check the arrangement: the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. This matches the definition of the fourth figure.
Contrast with the first figure where the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
3
Produce the conclusion
The author gives the conclusion 'Some animal is rational'. Note that this conclusion is particular affirmative. In the fourth figure, the conclusion must be particular affirmative (I) when both premises are universal affirmative (A).
The mood of this syllogism is AAI (minor A, major A, conclusion I).
4
Compare with the first figure
The author states that the fourth figure is farthest from the first figure, which is clearest in production. Reflect on why: in the first figure, the middle term connects the premises in a natural way, while in the fourth figure the connection is less intuitive.
This distance is why the fourth figure was discovered later in the logical tradition.

Its example: Every human is an animal and every rational (is a human), produces from the fourth figure: (Some animal is rational).

The Syllogism
beginner

Deriving the Sixteen Moods of a Syllogism
Author explains how the 16 moods are derived from quantity and quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of moods in a given figure by applying the combinatorial method of quantity and quality across both premises.

Core Idea
Each figure has exactly 16 moods, but only some are productive; the rest are sterile or repeated.

Now that you know the four figures, the author introduces the concept of moods (ضروب). A mood is the specific form of a syllogism determined by the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of its two premises. Since each premise can be one of four types (A, E, I, O), the total combinations are 4 × 4 = 16 per figure. The author notes that some of these 16 are productive (yield a valid conclusion), some are sterile (produce no conclusion), and some are repeated across figures. In this lesson, we focus on understanding the derivation of these 16 moods.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two dimensions: quantity and quality
Quantity determines whether a premise is universal (all or no) or particular (some). Quality determines whether it is affirmative (is) or negative (is not). These four combinations give the standard forms: A (universal affirmative), E (universal negative), I (particular affirmative), O (particular negative).
These letters are used throughout logic to designate moods.
2
Apply to the minor premise
The minor premise of any syllogism can be any of the four forms (A, E, I, O). So there are 4 possibilities for the minor.
3
Apply to the major premise
Similarly, the major premise can be any of the same four forms. That gives another 4 possibilities.
4
Calculate total moods
Multiply the possibilities: 4 (minor) × 4 (major) = 16. Thus each figure contains 16 moods, as the author states.
This combinatorial approach assumes all combinations are possible, but not all are valid in a given figure.
5
Identify productive vs sterile
Not all 16 moods produce a valid conclusion. The author says some are 'aqim (sterile) and some are repeated (valid in multiple figures). The conditions for productivity will be examined later for each figure.
This lesson only covers the derivation; we will learn which moods are productive in subsequent lessons.

Conditions for Productive First-Figure Moods and the First Two Moods
Author states the two conditions for the first figure's productivity and gives examples of the first two productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a productive first-figure mood and apply them to determine whether a given categorical syllogism in the first figure is valid.

Core Idea
The first figure's productivity depends on two conditions: minor affirmative, major universal.

The first figure is the most powerful form of categorical syllogism, but not every combination of premises yields a conclusion. The author lays down exactly two conditions that separate productive moods from sterile ones. Once you master these, you can immediately check whether any first-figure argument works. In this lesson, we will examine these conditions and see them applied in the first two moods, with examples from theology, jurisprudence, and grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The author states two conditions: first, the minor premise must be affirmative (in quality); second, the major premise must be universal (in quantity). Note that these conditions are specific to the first figure; other figures have different conditions.
2
Understand why only four moods are productive
The conditions restrict the possible combinations of premises. Out of sixteen possible moods in the first figure, only four satisfy both conditions. The other twelve are invalid because they violate at least one condition.
The full explanation of why these conditions are necessary is promised in later pages.
3
Recognize the first mood (AAA-1)
The first mood consists of two universal affirmative premises. It yields a universal affirmative conclusion. The author emphasizes that only the first figure can produce a universal affirmative conclusion.
Examples are given from theology, the Hanafi/Hanbali, Shafi'i, and Maliki schools of fiqh, and grammar.
4
Recognize the second mood (EAE-1)
The second mood has an affirmative minor and a negative major, both universal. It yields a universal negative conclusion. The example given is: 'Every human is an animal; no animal is stone; therefore no human is stone.'
The negation in the major must be universal (i.e., 'no animal is stone') for the conclusion to be valid.
5
Apply the conditions to examples
Take any of the provided examples and verify that the minor is affirmative and the major is universal. Then check that the conclusion follows the mood pattern. This skill helps in evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.
For instance, in the theological example: minor 'All worlds are created' (affirmative, universal), major 'Every created thing has a creator' (affirmative, universal). Conditions met, conclusion follows.

Know that the first figure, which is the best of the figures and the most evident in conclusion, requires for its productivity two conditions:
First: with respect to quality, that its minor be affirmative.
Second: with respect to quantity, that its major be universal.
And these two conditions apply only to four forms, and thereby you know that the first figure produces only four of its moods, and the remaining twelve have no productivity.
(1) The explanation of the reason for these conditions will come on pages 114, 115.
First mood: two universal affirmatives produce a universal affirmative. Its example: (Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient) produces: (Every human is sentient).
And no figure produces a universal affirmative except the first figure.
And its example in theological matters: your saying: (All worlds, like the heavens and the earth and what is in them, are created, and every created thing must have a creator) produces: (All worlds must have a creator).
And its example in jurisprudence: the Hanbali and Hanafi saying: (Every wheat is measurable, and every measurable thing has riba prohibited in it) produces: (Every wheat has riba prohibited in it).
And the Shafi'i saying: (Every wheat is food, and every food has riba prohibited in it) produces: (Every wheat has riba prohibited in it).
And the Maliki saying: (Every wheat is staple stored food, and every staple stored food has riba prohibited in it) produces: (Every wheat has riba prohibited in it).
And its example in grammar: (Every subject is nominative, and every nominative is a core element) produces: (Every subject is a core element).
Second mood: two universals, their minor affirmative and their major negative, produce a universal negative. Like your saying: (Every human is an animal, and nothing of the animal is stone) produces: (Nothing of the human is stone).

The second mood has an affirmative minor and a negative major, producing a universal negative conclusion. The example uses human/animal/stone: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No animal is stone' yields 'No human is stone.' These examples illustrate how the first figure's conditions are applied in real reasoning across disciplines.

First Mood of the First Figure: Universal Affirmative
Author explains the first productive mood of the first figure with examples from theology, fiqh, and grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first mood of the first figure (universal affirmative) and construct a valid categorical syllogism using it, with examples from theology, jurisprudence, and grammar.

Core Idea
The first mood yields a universal affirmative conclusion available only from the first figure.

In this passage, the author explains that of the sixteen possible moods of the first figure, only four produce a conclusion. The first mood consists of two universal affirmative premises that necessarily yield a universal affirmative conclusion. This is the only figure that can produce such a conclusion, making it invaluable across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood type
Look for two universal affirmative premises. In Arabic, they are marked by 'كل' (every) with a positive predicate. The mood is defined by the quantity (universal) and quality (affirmative) of both premises.
The author emphasizes that the first mood requires both premises to be universal affirmative.
2
Recognize the first figure structure
In the first figure, the middle term is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. Verify that the term that connects both premises appears in the right positions.
For example, in 'Every A is B, every B is C', the middle term B connects A and C.
3
Apply the syllogistic pattern
If every A is B and every B is C, then every A is C. This pattern is the foundation for drawing the universal affirmative conclusion.
The conclusion must also be universal affirmative.
4
Examine the Islamic science examples
Review the examples from theology (worlds created → need creator), fiqh (wheat/riba across madhabs), and grammar (subject nominative/core). Confirm that each follows the pattern and note how the same middle term links the terms.
The uniqueness of the first figure for this conclusion is stressed: no other figure yields a universal affirmative.

الشكل الأول لا ينتج من ضروبه إلا أربعة فقط، والاثنا عشر الباقية لا إنتاج فيها . الضرب الأول : كليتان ،موجبتان ينتجان كلية موجبة، مثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس) يُنتج : (كل إنسان حساس) . ولا ينتج كلية موجبة من الأشكال إلا الشكل الأول . ومثاله في العقليات قولك : كل العوالم كالسموات والأرض ومن فيهما مخلوقة، وكل مخلوق لابد له من خالق) يُنتج : كل العوالم لابد لها من خالق . ومثاله في الفقهيات قول الحنبلي والحنفي: (كل بر مكيل، وكل مكيل يحرم فيه الربا) يُنتج : كلُّ بُر يحرم فيه الربا) . وقول الشافعي: كل) بر مطعوم وكل مطعوم يحرم فيه الربا) ينتج : (كل بر يحرم فيه الربا) . وقول المالكي : كل) بر مقتات مدخر وكل مقتات مدخر يحرم فيه الربا) يُنتج : كل بر يحرم فيه الربا) . ومثاله في النحويات كل فاعل مرفوع، وكل مرفوع عمدة) ينتج : كل فاعل عمدة) .
The first figure produces only four of its moods, the remaining twelve are unproductive. The first mood: two universal affirmatives produce a universal affirmative, for example: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient' yields 'Every human is sentient.' And no figure produces a universal affirmative except the first. Its example in rational matters: 'All the worlds—like the heavens, earth, and what is in them—are created, and every created thing necessarily has a creator' yields 'All the worlds necessarily have a creator.' Its example in fiqh: the statement of the Hanbali and Hanafi: 'Every wheat is measurable, and everything measurable involves riba prohibition' yields 'Every wheat involves riba prohibition.' And the statement of the Shafi'i: 'Every wheat is edible, and everything edible involves riba prohibition' yields 'Every wheat involves riba prohibition.' And the statement of the Maliki: 'Every wheat is staple and storable, and every staple and storable involves riba prohibition' yields 'Every wheat involves riba prohibition.' Its example in grammar: 'Every subject is nominative, and every nominative is a core constituent' yields 'Every subject is a core constituent.'
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that out of the sixteen possible moods of the first figure, only four are productive; the rest yield no conclusion. The first mood consists of two universal affirmative premises producing a universal affirmative conclusion. The author illustrates this with a classic logical example: every human is an animal, every animal is sentient, therefore every human is sentient. He then notes that the universal affirmative conclusion is unique to the first figure. Examples from theology (creation and Creator), jurisprudence (the three madhhabs' views on riba in wheat), and grammar (subject being a core constituent) demonstrate how this syllogism applies across disciplines. The examples show the middle term linking the subject and predicate to produce the conclusion.

ضرب
كلية موجبة

The Second Productive Mood (Celarent)
Author explains the second mood with universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, giving examples from logic, theology, fiqh, and grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mood of the first figure and construct valid Celarent syllogisms using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Celarent: universal negative major, universal affirmative minor, universal negative conclusion.

In the first figure, the second productive mood combines a universal affirmative minor with a universal negative major to produce a universal negative conclusion. This mood is essential for establishing separations—proving that a subject is excluded from a predicate. The author illustrates this with examples from theology, fiqh, and grammar, showing its wide applicability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise pattern
In the second mood, the minor premise is universal affirmative (كل س is ص) and the major premise is universal negative (لا شيء من ص is م). Locate both in the text.
The minor is the subject-premise; the major is the predicate-premise.
2
Check the conclusion type
The conclusion must be universal negative (لا شيء من س is م). Verify that each example yields this form.
3
Extract the middle term
The middle term (ص) is the one appearing in both premises but not in the conclusion. Find it in each example and see how it connects the subject and predicate.
In the fiqh example, the middle term is 'worship (عبادة)'.
4
Apply to an Islamic science
Using the fiqh example, explain the reasoning: 'Every lawful fasting is worship, and no worship is valid without intention, therefore no lawful fasting is valid without intention.' This shows how Celarent works in deriving a ruling.
The universal negative conclusion is necessary: it follows from the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثاني : كليتان صغراهما موجبة وكبراهما سالبة. ينتج كلية سالبة ، كقولك : كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحيوان بحجر) يُنتج : (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر). ومثاله في العقليات كل) بشر ،مخلوق ولا شيء من المخلوق بغني عن الفاعل ينتج : (لا شيء من البشر بغني عن الفاعل) . ومثاله في الفقهيات كل صوم شرعي عبادة، ولا شيء من العبادة يصح بلا نية) يُنتج : (لا شيء من الصوم الشرعي يصح بلا نية ومثاله في النحويات كل فاعل مرفوع ولا شيء من المرفوع بفضله) ينتج : (لا شيء من الفاعل بفضلة .
The second mood: two universal premises, the minor affirmative and the major negative, produce a universal negative. For example: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing of animal is a stone' yields 'Nothing of human is a stone.' Its example in rational matters: 'Every human is created, and nothing created is independent of the agent' yields 'Nothing of human is independent of the agent.' Its example in fiqh: 'Every lawful fasting is worship, and nothing of worship is valid without intention' yields 'Nothing of lawful fasting is valid without intention.' Its example in grammar: 'Every subject is nominative, and nothing of the nominative is a dependent element' yields 'Nothing of the subject is a dependent element.'
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the second productive mood of the first figure: the minor premise is universal affirmative and the major premise is universal negative, yielding a universal negative conclusion. The author provides a logical example: every human is an animal, no animal is a stone, therefore no human is a stone. Then he parallels it with examples from theology (human is created, nothing created is independent of a creator), jurisprudence (fasting is worship, no worship is valid without intention), and grammar (subject is nominative, no nominative is a dependent element). In each case, the minor premise affirms the subject under the middle term, the major premise negates the predicate from the middle term, resulting in a universal negation of the subject and predicate.

Third Mood of the First Figure (Darii)
Third mood of first figure with particular minor and universal major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third mood of the first figure and construct a valid categorical syllogism of this mood using Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
The third mood is the only first-figure mood with a particular minor, limiting the conclusion to particular affirmative.

We have seen the first two productive moods of the first figure. Now the author introduces the third mood, where the minor premise is particular affirmative. This mood is crucial for arguments where the subject is only partially known. The author provides examples from logic, theology, fiqh, and grammar to illustrate its form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's structure
The third mood has a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise, producing a particular affirmative conclusion.
This is known as Darii in Latin (AII).
2
Locate the minor and major premises
In each example, find which premise is particular (starts with 'some') and which is universal (starts with 'every' or 'all'). The minor is the particular one.
The minor premise usually contains the subject of the conclusion.
3
Extract the terms
Identify the subject, predicate, and middle term. The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
For the logical example: middle term = 'human' (إنسان).
4
Verify the conclusion
Check that the conclusion is particular affirmative and follows logically from the premises. Since the minor is particular, the conclusion cannot be universal.
The author confirms 'ينتج جزئية موجبة' (produces a particular affirmative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثالث: موجبتان صغراهما جزئية وكبراهما كلية يُنتج جزئية موجبة كقولك : بعض الحيوان إنسان وكل إنسان ناطق) ينتج : (بعض الحيوان ناطق. ومثاله في العقليات بعض الصفات مخلوق ـ تعني صفات الخلق ، وكل مخلوق لابد له من خالق يُنتج : (بعض الصفات لابد لها من خالق). . ومثاله في الفقهيات بعض الطعام مكيل، وكل مكيل يحرم فيه الربا) ينتج : (بعض الطعام يحرم فيه الربا وهذا على رأي من يعلل بالكيل. ومثاله في النحويات بعض المرفوع فاعل، وكل فاعل يجب تأخيره عن عامله) ينتج : (بعض المرفوع يجب تأخيره عن عامله) .
The third mood: two affirmatives, the minor particular and the major universal, produce a particular affirmative. For example: 'Some animal is human, and every human is rational' yields 'Some animal is rational.' Its example in rational matters: 'Some attributes are created—meaning attributes of creation, and every created thing necessarily has a creator' yields 'Some attributes necessarily have a creator.' Its example in fiqh: 'Some food is measurable, and everything measurable involves riba prohibition' yields 'Some food involves riba prohibition'—this according to the view that the cause is measurability. Its example in grammar: 'Some nominatives are subjects, and every subject must be postponed from its operator' yields 'Some nominatives must be postponed from their operator.'
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage describes the third productive mood of the first figure: the minor premise is particular affirmative and the major premise is universal affirmative, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The author illustrates with a logical example: some animal is human, every human is rational, therefore some animal is rational. Then he provides examples from theology (some attributes are created, every created thing needs a creator), jurisprudence (some food is measurable, everything measurable involves riba — noting that this depends on the opinion that riba is caused by measurability), and grammar (some nominatives are subjects, every subject must come after its operator). The mood restricts the conclusion to a particular, as the minor premise is particular.

Key Terms
جزئية موجبة (juz'iyya mujiba)
الضرب الثالث (al-darb al-thalith)

Key Takeaway
The third mood of the first figure (Darii) has a particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major, and yields a particular affirmative conclusion.

Fourth Mood of First Figure: Particular Negative
Author explains the fourth productive mood with particular affirmative minor and universal negative major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood of the first figure and verify whether a given categorical syllogism matches its conditions.

Core Idea
Particular affirmative minor + universal negative major = particular negative conclusion.

The fourth mood completes the productive moods of the first figure. It combines a particular affirmative minor premise with a universal negative major premise to yield a particular negative conclusion. This mood is essential for drawing valid negative conclusions about some members of a category, as shown in the author's example: some animal is human, no human is a horse, therefore some animal is not a horse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
The minor premise is about the subject of the conclusion (e.g., 'animal'), the major is about the predicate (e.g., 'horse'). Ensure you know which is which.
2
Check the minor premise is particular affirmative
The minor must be a 'particular affirmative' (juz'iyya mujiba), e.g., 'Some animal is human.' It cannot be universal or negative.
3
Check the major premise is universal negative
The major must be a 'universal negative' (kulliyya saliba), e.g., 'No human is a horse.' It cannot be particular or affirmative.
4
Verify the conclusion is particular negative
Because the minor is particular, the conclusion is particular; because the major is negative, the conclusion is negative. The conclusion must be particular negative, e.g., 'Some animal is not a horse.'
5
Test with an Islamic example
Construct a syllogism from fiqh: 'Some contracts are conditional sales; no conditional sale is valid; therefore, some contracts are not valid.' Check that it follows the same pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الرابع: جزئية موجبة صغرى وكلية سالبة كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة ، كقولك : (بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان بفرس ينتج : (بعض الحيوان ليس بفرس).
The fourth mood: a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major produce a particular negative. For example: 'Some animal is human, and nothing human is a horse' yields 'Some animal is not a horse.'
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth and last productive mood of the first figure: the minor premise is particular affirmative, the major premise is universal negative, and the conclusion is particular negative. The author gives one logical example: some animal is human, no human is a horse, therefore some animal is not a horse. This is the only example provided in this excerpt; presumably the author would have given examples from theology, fiqh, and grammar as in previous moods, but the text cuts off. Nevertheless, the logical structure is clear: because the minor is particular, the conclusion is particular; because the major is negative, the conclusion is negative.

Key Terms
جزئية موجبة
كلية سالبة
جزئية سالبة
الضرب الرابع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Identifying the Second Mood (Celarent) with Islamic Examples
Author gives examples of the second productive mood from three sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mood (Celarent) of the first figure and reconstruct its form from examples in kalam, fiqh, and nahw.

Core Idea
The second mood proves universal negatives: nothing of S is P when all S is M and no M is P.

This passage shows how the second productive mood of the first figure (Celarent) works with concrete examples from three Islamic sciences. You will see the same logical pattern applied to different subject matters. By learning to recognize this mood, you gain a tool for constructing valid arguments in any discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Find the universal affirmative proposition (starting with 'kull' or 'every'). In each example, it is the first premise: 'every human is created', 'every legally valid fast is worship', 'every agent is nominative'.
2
Identify the major premise
Find the universal negative proposition (starting with 'lā shay' or 'nothing'). It is the second premise: 'nothing created is independent of the agent', 'no worship is valid without intention', 'nothing nominative is an extra element'.
3
Check the middle term
The term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Examples: 'created', 'worship', 'nominative'.
4
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal negative (starting with 'lā shay' or 'no'): 'no human is independent of the agent', etc.
The conclusion follows necessarily from the premises in this figure and mood.
5
Recognize the mood
This is Celarent: minor = universal affirmative, major = universal negative, conclusion = universal negative. The figure is the first figure (middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor).
This mood is always valid in the first figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله في العقليات كل بشر مخلوق ولا شيء من المخلوق بغني عن الفاعل ينتج: لا شيء من البشر بغني عن الفاعل. ومثاله في الفقهيات كل صوم شرعي عبادة، ولا شيء من العبادة يصح بلا نية ينتج: لا شيء من الصوم الشرعي يصح بلا نية. ومثاله في النحويات كل فاعل مرفوع ولا شيء من المرفوع بفضلة ينتج: لا شيء من الفاعل بفضلة.
And its example in intellectual matters: Every human is created, and nothing created is independent of the agent, yields: No human is independent of the agent. And its example in juridical matters: Every legally valid fast is worship, and no worship is valid without intention, yields: No legally valid fast is valid without intention. And its example in grammatical matters: Every agent is nominative, and nothing nominative is an extra element, yields: No agent is an extra element.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage provides three examples of the second productive mood of the first figure (known as Celarent in Western logic). The second mood is characterized by a universal affirmative minor premise and a universal negative major premise, yielding a universal negative conclusion. The author illustrates this pattern from three disciplines: intellectual theology, Islamic jurisprudence, and Arabic grammar. In each example, the minor premise affirms that every member of the subject class falls under the middle term, and the major premise denies that any member of the middle term has the predicate, leading to the conclusion that no member of the subject class has the predicate. For instance, in the intellectual example: every human is created, and nothing created is independent of the agent, so no human is independent of the agent.
