Key Takeaway
The second mood (Celarent) of the first figure consists of a universal affirmative minor premise and a universal negative major premise, yielding a valid universal negative conclusion.

Third and Fourth Productive Moods of the First Figure (Darii and Ferio)
Author defines and exemplifies the third (Darii) and fourth (Ferio) productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third and fourth moods of the first figure and construct valid categorical syllogisms in those moods using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The first figure yields both universal and particular conclusions; Darii and Ferio cover the particular ones.

We have learned that the first figure has four productive moods. In the previous lesson, we covered the first two (Barbara and Celarent) which produce universal conclusions. Now we turn to the third and fourth moods, Darii and Ferio, which produce particular conclusions. The author gives examples from theology, jurisprudence, and grammar to illustrate how these moods work in practice. By mastering these moods, you will be able to analyze and construct arguments that involve particular statements — essential for nuanced legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
In Darii and Ferio, the minor premise is always a particular affirmative (Some S is M). Locate the premise that contains the subject of the conclusion.
In the example 'Some food is measured', the minor premise is 'Some food is measured'.
2
Identify the major premise
In Darii, the major premise is a universal affirmative (Every M is P). In Ferio, it is a universal negative (No M is P). Locate the premise that contains the predicate of the conclusion.
For Darii: 'Every measured item involves riba prohibition'. For Ferio: 'No agent is permissible to precede its operator'.
3
Check the conclusion
Darii yields a particular affirmative (Some S is P). Ferio yields a particular negative (Some S is not P). Verify that the conclusion matches the mood.
Example Darii conclusion: 'Some food involves riba prohibition'. Example Ferio conclusion: 'Some nominatives are not permissible to precede their operator'.
4
Apply the mood to a new example
Given an Islamic argument, determine whether it fits Darii or Ferio by checking the minor (particular affirmative) and major (universal affirmative for Darii, universal negative for Ferio). If the pattern matches, the syllogism is valid.
For instance, 'Some prophets are messengers, and every messenger is infallible, so some prophets are infallible' is a valid Darii.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثالث: موجبتان صغراهما جزئية وكبراهما كلية يُنتج جزئية موجبة كقولك: بعض الحيوان إنسان وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج: بعض الحيوان ناطق. ومثاله في العقليات بعض الصفات مخلوق — تعني صفات الخلق، وكل مخلوق لابد له من خالق ينتج: بعض الصفات لابد لها من خالق. ومثاله في الفقهيات بعض الطعام مكيل، وكل مكيل يحرم فيه الربا ينتج: بعض الطعام يحرم فيه الربا وهذا على رأي من يعلل بالكيل. ومثاله في النحويات بعض المرفوع فاعل، وكل فاعل يجب تأخيره عن عامله ينتج: بعض المرفوع يجب تأخيره عن عامله. الضرب الرابع: جزئية موجبة صغرى وكلية سالبة كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة، كقولك: بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان بفرس ينتج: بعض الحيوان ليس بفرس. ومثاله في العقليات: بعض الصفات أزلي — أي لا أول لوجودها، تعني صفة الخالق، ولا شيء من الأزلي بمخلوق ينتج: بعض الصفات ليس بمخلوق. ومثاله في الفقهيات: بعض العبادة صلاة، ولا شيء من الصلاة بقابل للنيابة ينتج: بعض العبادة ليس بقابل للنيابة. ومثاله في النحويات بعض المرفوعات فاعل، ولا شيء من الفاعل بجائز التقديم على عامله ينتج: بعض المرفوع ليس بجائز التقديم على عامله. فهذه الضروب الأربعة المنتجة من الشكل الأول.
The third mood: two affirmative premises, their minor particular and their major universal, yields a particular affirmative, as in your saying: Some animal is human, and every human is rational, yields: Some animal is rational. And its example in intellectual matters: Some attributes are created—meaning attributes of creation, and every created thing must have a creator, yields: Some attributes must have a creator. And its example in juridical matters: Some food is measured, and every measured thing involves riba prohibition, yields: Some food involves riba prohibition, and this is according to the opinion of those who base the prohibition on measurement. And its example in grammatical matters: Some nominatives are agent, and every agent must be postponed after its operator, yields: Some nominatives must be postponed after their operator. The fourth mood: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, yields a particular negative, as in your saying: Some animal is human, and no human is a horse, yields: Some animal is not a horse. And its example in intellectual matters: Some attributes are eternal—meaning no beginning to their existence, referring to the attribute of the Creator, and nothing eternal is created, yields: Some attributes are not created. And its example in juridical matters: Some acts of worship are prayer, and no prayer is subject to deputization, yields: Some acts of worship are not subject to deputization. And its example in grammatical matters: Some nominatives are agent, and no agent is permissible to precede its operator, yields: Some nominatives are not permissible to precede their operator. These are the four productive moods of the first figure.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the third and fourth productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The third mood (Darii) consists of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The author provides a generic example from animal and human, then examples from theology, jurisprudence, and grammar. In the theology example, some attributes (of creation) are created, and every created thing has a creator, so some attributes have a creator. Similarly in jurisprudence, some food is measured, and every measured item involves riba, so some food involves riba (according to those who consider measurement as the effective cause). In grammar, some nominatives are agents, and every agent must follow its operator, so some nominatives must follow their operator. The fourth mood (Ferio) has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. Examples: some animals are human, no human is a horse, so some animals are not horses. In theology, some attributes (of the Creator) are eternal, and nothing eternal is created, so some attributes are not created. In jurisprudence, some acts of worship are prayer, and no prayer can be deputized, so some acts of worship cannot be deputized. In grammar, some nominatives are agents, and no agent can precede its operator, so some nominatives cannot precede their operator. The author concludes that these four moods are the productive ones of the first figure.

Key Terms
ضرب (ḍarb): Mood or form of a syllogism, determined by the quality and quantity of the premises.
جزئية موجبة (juz'iyya mūjiba): Particular affirmative proposition (some S is P).
كلية موجبة (kulliyya mūjiba): Universal affirmative proposition (every S is P).
كلية سالبة (kulliyya sāliba): Universal negative proposition (no S is P).
جزئية سالبة (juz'iyya sāliba): Particular negative proposition (some S is not P).
الشكل الأول (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism, where the major premise is predicate of the minor and the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Universal negative
C) Particular affirmative
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
The first figure's third and fourth moods (Darii and Ferio) produce particular conclusions from a particular minor and a universal major, covering the full range of categorical syllogisms in the first figure.

Why Only Four Moods Are Productive in the First Figure
Author explains the combinatorial reason for four productive moods and notes the first figure's unique output.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the four productive moods of the first figure from the two conditions and explain why the first figure uniquely produces universal affirmative conclusions.

Core Idea
The first figure is the only figure that yields a universal affirmative conclusion.

In this passage, the author explains the combinatorial logic behind the first figure's four productive moods. By understanding the constraints that the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal, you will see exactly how these four moods emerge and why the first figure has a unique place in syllogistic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for productivity
Recall that a productive mood in the first figure requires: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) the major premise must be universal. These are given as necessary conditions.
2
List the possible forms of the minor premise
Since the minor must be affirmative, it can only be either universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I). No negative forms are allowed.
3
List the possible forms of the major premise
Since the major must be universal, it can only be either universal affirmative (A) or universal negative (E). No particular forms are allowed.
4
Combine the possibilities to find the moods
Multiply the two states of the minor (A, I) by the two states of the major (A, E) to get four combinations: AA, AE, IA, IE. These correspond to the moods Barbara, Celarent, Darii, and Ferio.
5
Recognise the unique output of the first figure
The author notes that the universal affirmative conclusion (A) is only produced by the first figure. Moreover, the first figure can generate all four types of conclusions: A, E, I, O. The second figure, in contrast, only produces universal negative (E) among universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإيضاح كونه لا ينتج من ضروبه إلا أربعة بحسب الشرطين المشترطين لإنتاجه، اللذين قدمنا أنهما إيجاب صغراه وكلية كبراه: أن إيجاب الصغرى يستلزم أنها ليس لها إلا حالتان، وهما كونها كلية موجبة أو جزئية موجبة وكلية الكبرى تستلزم أنها ليس لها إلا حالتان وهما كونها كلية موجبة أو كلية سالبة، فتضرب حالتي الصغرى في حالتي الكبرى بأربع وهي الضروب المنتجة التي أوضحناها بأمثلتها. واعلم أنه لا ينتج الكلية الموجبة من الأشكال إلا الشكل الأول، فهو ينتج جميع المطالب الأربعة أعني الكليتين الموجبة والسالبة، والجزئيتين الموجبة والسالبة. أما الشكل الثاني فلا يُنتج من الكليات إلا السالبة.
And the clarification of why from its moods only four produce, according to the two required conditions for its production, which we previously mentioned: the affirmation of its minor and the universality of its major. That because the affirmation of the minor entails that it has only two states: either universal affirmative or particular affirmative; and the universality of the major entails that it has only two states: either universal affirmative or universal negative. So the two states of the minor multiplied by the two states of the major produce four, which are the productive moods that we have illustrated with their examples. And know that the universal affirmative is not produced by any figure except the first figure. So it produces all four types of conclusions: the universal affirmative, the universal negative, the particular affirmative, and the particular negative. As for the second figure, it produces only the universal negative among the universals.

Commentary
This passage explains why only four moods of the first figure are productive. The author recalls two conditions established earlier: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. From the condition that the minor is affirmative, it can only be either universal affirmative or particular affirmative (two possibilities). From the condition that the major is universal, it can only be either universal affirmative or universal negative (two possibilities). Multiplying the two possibilities of the minor by the two of the major yields four combinations, which are the four productive moods (Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio). The author then adds a valuable observation: the universal affirmative conclusion is only produced by the first figure. The first figure alone can generate all four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). In contrast, the second figure can only produce universal negative conclusions among universals. This underscores the primacy of the first figure in categorical logic.

The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author illustrates four productive moods of first figure with examples and derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a given first-figure syllogism is one of the four productive moods and justify your answer using the conditions of affirmative minor and universal major.

Core Idea
The first figure's four productive moods arise from the constraints that the minor must be affirmative and the major universal.

This passage presents concrete examples of the first figure's productive moods from Islamic theology, fiqh, and grammar, and then logically explains why only four moods are valid. By dissecting the conditions, you will see how the number of moods is not arbitrary but follows from the structure of the syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two conditions for productivity
The author states that for a first-figure syllogism to produce a necessary conclusion, the minor premise must be affirmative (not negative) and the major premise must be universal (not particular). Read the Arabic sentence starting with 'وإيضاح كونه لا ينتج...' and identify these two conditions.
The conditions are 'إيجاب صغراه' and 'كليه كبراه'.
2
Map each condition to the allowed proposition types
An affirmative premise can be either universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I). A universal premise can be either universal affirmative (A) or universal negative (E). Write down the two possibilities for the minor and two for the major.
Minor: A or I; Major: A or E.
3
Compute the number of combinations
Multiply the number of possible states for the minor (2) by the number for the major (2) to get 4. These four combinations are the only productive moods of the first figure.
4
Match each combination to a mood and an example
List the four combinations: (Minor A, Major A) → Barbara; (Minor A, Major E) → Celarent; (Minor I, Major A) → Darii; (Minor I, Major E) → Ferio. Identify which of these corresponds to the fiqh example in the passage (Minor I, Major E → Ferio).
The fiqh example: 'Some acts of worship are prayer' (I), 'Nothing of prayer is susceptible to substitution' (E), conclusion 'Some acts of worship are not susceptible to substitution' (O).

That the affirmation of the minor entails that it has only two states: being universal affirmative or particular affirmative. And the universality of the major entails that it has only two states: being universal affirmative or universal negative. So multiplying the two states of the minor by the two states of the major yields four, which are the productive moods that we have clarified with their examples.

Commentary
This passage illustrates the four productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism and explains why only these four are productive. The author begins by giving concrete examples from three sciences: intellectual theology, Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh), and Arabic grammar (nahw). In each example, the minor premise is particular affirmative (Some attributes are eternal; Some acts of worship are prayer; Some nominatives are agent) and the major premise is universal negative (Nothing eternal is created; Nothing of prayer is susceptible to substitution; Nothing of the agent is permissible to precede its operator), yielding a particular negative conclusion. These represent the fourth mood of the first figure (IEO, in traditional terms, but the author presents it as one of four productive moods). After the examples, the author recaps that these are the four productive moods. Then he provides a logical justification: the conditions for production are that the minor must be affirmative (giving two possibilities: universal or particular affirmative) and the major must be universal (giving two possibilities: universal affirmative or universal negative). Combining these yields exactly four moods. The author does not enumerate the other three moods explicitly in this passage but implies that they involve combinations of the other states (e.g., universal minor affirmative with universal major affirmative yields universal affirmative conclusion; universal minor affirmative with universal major negative yields universal negative; particular minor affirmative with universal major affirmative yields particular affirmative; and the illustrated example yields particular negative). The explanation is tightly tied to the conditions, showing that the first figure's productivity is derived from these constraints.

Which Figures Produce Which Conclusions
Author explains which figures produce which conclusion types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which syllogistic figure can produce each of the four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) based on the rules given in this passage.

Core Idea
The first figure alone produces all four conclusion types; the second only negatives; the third and fourth only particulars.

In syllogistic logic, different figures (shakl) have different capacities for producing conclusions. The author explains that the first figure is the most powerful, producing all four types of categorical propositions. The second figure is limited to negative conclusions, while the third and fourth figures can only yield particular conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conclusion types (maṭālib)
The passage lists universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Ensure you know these four types.
2
Understand each figure's capacity
First figure: all four. Second figure: only negative (universal negative and particular negative). Third and fourth: only particular (affirmative and negative).
3
Note the exception for the fourth figure
The author mentions that a mood of the fourth figure can produce a universal if both premises are universal and the minor is negative; this will be clarified later.
4
Apply the rule to a kalam example
If a mutakallim wants to prove 'All temporally originated things have a cause' (universal affirmative), only the first figure can yield that conclusion. For 'No action is free of divine will' (universal negative), first or second figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه لا ينتج الكلية الموجبة من الأشكال إلا الشكل الأول، فهو ينتج جميع المطالب الأربعة أعني الكليتين الموجبة والسالبة، والجزئيتين الموجبة والسالبة. أما الشكل الثاني فلا يُنتج من الكليات إلا السالبة . والثالث والرابع لا ينتجان إلا الجزئيات وقد يوجد ضرب من الرابع ينتج كلية كالمركب من كليتين صغراهما سالبة، كما سترى إيضاحه إن شاء الله . تنبيه : اعلم أن النتيجة تتبع ما في القياس من الخستين، والخستان هما السلب والجزئية فكل قياس فيه سالبة فنتيجته سالبة، وكل قياس فيه جزئية فنتيجته جزئية، وكل قياس فيه جزئية وسالبة فنتيجته جزئية سالبة، فالنتيجة تتبع الخسة دائمًا، كما قال الشاعر : إن الزمان لتـابـع أرذاله تبع النتيجة للأخس الأرذل والسلب خسة الكيف، والجزئية خسة الكم. ولا نريد الآن أن نتتبع جميع الضروب العقيمة الخارجة بالشرطين المذكورين، ولكنا أردنا أن نمثل لها دون تتبع جميعها للاختصار . فوجه اشتراط الإيجاب في الصغرى أنها إذا كانت سالبة لا تلزم المقدمتين نتيجة صادقة، بل قد تكون كاذبة، مع أن القياس على هيئته
And know that the universal affirmative is produced only by the first figure among the figures. So it produces all four (types of) demands, namely the universal affirmative and negative, and the particular affirmative and negative. As for the second figure, it produces only the negative among universals. And the third and fourth produce only particulars, though there may be found a mood of the fourth that produces a universal, like the one composed of two universals whose minor is negative, as you will see its clarification, God willing. A note: Know that the conclusion follows the inferiority (khissa) that is in the syllogism. And the two inferiorities are negation and particularity. So every syllogism in which there is a negative, its conclusion is negative; and every syllogism in which there is a particular, its conclusion is particular; and every syllogism in which there is a particular and a negative, its conclusion is particular negative. So the conclusion always follows the inferiority, as the poet said: 'Verily, time follows its base ones; the conclusion follows the basest, the meanest.' And negation is the inferiority of quality, and particularity is the inferiority of quantity. And we do not now intend to enumerate all the infertile moods excluded by the two mentioned conditions, but we intended to give examples of them without enumerating all for brevity. So the reason for stipulating affirmation in the minor is that if it were negative, the two premises would not yield a true conclusion; rather it might be false, even though the syllogism is in its correct form.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous discussion by addressing the productive capacity of the four figures. The author first states that only the first figure can produce a universal affirmative conclusion. In fact, the first figure is unique in producing all four types of conclusions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The second figure only produces negative conclusions among the universals (i.e., universal negative and particular negative). The third and fourth figures produce only particular conclusions (particular affirmative and particular negative), but the author notes an exception: a certain mood of the fourth figure can produce a universal, namely when both premises are universal and the minor is negative. This will be clarified later. Then he gives a rule: the conclusion always follows the 'inferiority' (خسة) present in the premises. Inferiority comes in two kinds: negation (salb) and particularity (juz'iyya). Negation is the inferior quality, and particularity is the inferior quantity. Thus, if any premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if any premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if both occur, the conclusion is particular negative. This is illustrated with a poetic line. The author then explains why the minor must be affirmative: if the minor were negative, the premises might not yield a true conclusion even though the syllogism is valid in form, indicating that the conditions for productivity are not only formal but also material. This clarifies why the first figure's moods require an affirmative minor.

The Principle of Inferiority (Khissa) in Syllogisms
Author introduces the principle that the conclusion follows the inferior premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the quality and quantity of a syllogism's conclusion by applying the principle of inferiority (khissa) to its premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion always follows the 'inferior' premise: if any premise is negative, conclusion is negative; if any is particular, conclusion is particular.

The author introduces a powerful rule: the conclusion's quality and quantity are determined by the 'inferior' premise. Inferiority comes in two forms: negation (inferior quality) and particularity (inferior quantity). This rule simplifies checking whether a given syllogism's conclusion matches its premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two inferiorities
Negation (salb) is the inferior quality, particularity (juz'iyya) is the inferior quantity.
2
Check each premise for negation
If any premise is negative, the conclusion must be negative. If no premise is negative, conclusion is affirmative.
3
Check each premise for particularity
If any premise is particular, the conclusion must be particular. If all premises are universal, conclusion can be universal.
4
Combine both checks
If a syllogism has both a negative and a particular premise, the conclusion is particular negative.
5
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative). 'Some nabidh is an intoxicant' (particular affirmative). Therefore 'Some nabidh is forbidden' (particular affirmative). The conclusion follows the particular (inferior) premise.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And negation is the inferiority of quality, and particularity is the inferiority of quantity. And we do not now intend to enumerate all the infertile moods excluded by the two mentioned conditions, but we intended to give examples of them without enumerating all for brevity. So the reason for stipulating affirmation in the minor is that if it were negative, the two premises would not yield a true conclusion; rather it might be false, even though the syllogism is in its correct form.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous discussion by addressing the productive capacity of the four figures. The author first states that only the first figure can produce a universal affirmative conclusion. In fact, the first figure is unique in producing all four types of conclusions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The second figure only produces negative conclusions among the universals (i.e., universal negative and particular negative). The third and fourth figures produce only particular conclusions (particular affirmative and particular negative), but the author notes an exception: a certain mood of the fourth figure can produce a universal, namely when both premises are universal and the minor is negative. This will be clarified later. Then he gives a rule: the conclusion always follows the 'inferiority' (خسة) present in the premises. Inferiority comes in two kinds: negation (salb) and particularity (juz'iyya). Negation is the inferior quality, and particularity is the inferior quantity. Thus, if any premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if any premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if both occur, the conclusion is particular negative. This is illustrated with a poetic line. The author then explains why the minor must be affirmative: if the minor were negative, the premises might not yield a true conclusion even though the syllogism is valid in form, indicating that the conditions for productivity are not only formal but also material. This clarifies why the first figure's moods require an affirmative minor.

سلب
جزئية
نتيجة

Key Takeaway
The conclusion always follows the inferior premise: negation overrides quality, particularity overrides quantity.

The Two Essential Conditions for First Figure Syllogisms
Author states the two conditions for first figure productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a first-figure categorical syllogism satisfies the two conditions for productivity and determine if the conclusion follows necessarily.

Core Idea
A first-figure syllogism must have an affirmative minor and a universal major or it fails.

The first figure is the most powerful form of categorical syllogism, but only when its premises meet two conditions. Without them, even true premises can lead to a false conclusion. Let's examine these conditions and see why they are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the first figure
The first figure has the middle term as subject of major and predicate of minor. The standard format: minor premise (subject = minor term, predicate = middle), major premise (subject = middle, predicate = major term), conclusion (subject = minor, predicate = major).
2
Identify the condition on the minor premise
The minor premise must be affirmative. The author explains that if it is negative, the premises can be true but the conclusion false, as in the example: 'No animal is a stone; every stone is a body' yields false 'No animal is a body'.
The defect enters from the minor being negative.
3
Identify the condition on the major premise
The major premise must be universal. If it is particular, the truth of the conclusion may not follow. Example: 'Every human is an animal; some animals are horses' yields false 'Some humans are horses'.
The defect enters from the major being particular.
4
Test a syllogism that violates one condition
Take a first-figure syllogism with a negative minor or particular major. Even if both premises are true, check the conclusion. You will find it can be false, showing that the syllogism is invalid.
Use the author's examples to verify.
5
Generalize: why both conditions are necessary
The first figure is productive only when both conditions are met. If either is violated, the syllogism may not yield a true conclusion necessarily. This is why they are stipulated.
These conditions are specific to the first figure.

The Rule of Inferiority: How the Conclusion Follows the Weaker Premise
Author states the rule that conclusion follows the inferior quality (negation) and quantity (particularity).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict the quality and quantity of a first-figure conclusion by applying the rule of khissa (inferiority).

Core Idea
The conclusion adopts the inferior (weaker) quality and quantity from the premises.

Besides the structural conditions, the conclusion of a valid first-figure syllogism is determined by a simple principle: it follows the 'inferior' (al-khissa) in terms of negation and particularity. This rule ensures the conclusion is always at most as strong as the weakest premise.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of inferiority
Negation (salb) is the inferior quality; particularity (juz'iyya) is the inferior quantity. The conclusion adopts the lower of each.
2
Apply the rule for quality
If any premise is negative, the conclusion is negative. If both are affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative.
Example: 'Every human is an animal; no animal is a stone' => 'No human is a stone.'
3
Apply the rule for quantity
If any premise is particular, the conclusion is particular. If both are universal, the conclusion is universal.
Example: 'Some humans are writers; every writer is a human' => 'Some humans are humans' (trivial), but better: 'Some animals are dogs; every dog is an animal' => 'Some animals are animals' tautology. Use a proper example: 'Every horse is an animal; some animals are brown' yields 'some horses are brown' if valid.
4
Combine quality and quantity
If a syllogism has both a negative and a particular premise, the conclusion is particular negative.
Example: 'Every human is an animal; some animals are not horses' would yield 'some humans are not horses' if valid.
5
Verify with the author's poetic couplet
The poet says: 'Verily, time follows its villains, the conclusion follows the inferior and viler.' This reinforces that the conclusion always takes the weaker aspect.
The rule is a general principle for the first figure.

Failure Conditions for First-Figure Syllogisms
Author shows invalid moods when conditions violate affirmative minor or universal major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid first-figure categorical syllogisms by checking whether the minor premise is affirmative and the major premise is universal.

Core Idea
A valid first-figure syllogism requires an affirmative minor and a universal major.

In the first figure, certain conditions must hold for the conclusion to follow necessarily. The author explains two key violations: a negative minor premise or a particular major premise. Using examples from common knowledge, he shows that when these conditions are broken, the conclusion can be false even if the premises are true. This lesson will teach you to spot these invalid moods and avoid them in your own reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
In a first-figure syllogism, the minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion, and the major premise contains the predicate. Locate these in the author's examples.
2
Check the minor premise for negativity
The author's first example has a negative minor ('No animal is a stone'). According to the condition, the minor must be affirmative. This violation leads to a false conclusion.
A negative minor makes the syllogism invalid regardless of the major.
3
Check the major premise for particularity
The second example has a particular major ('Some animal is a horse'). The condition requires the major to be universal. This violation also yields a false conclusion.
A particular major also invalidates the syllogism.
4
Apply the conditions to your own examples
Practice by constructing a valid first-figure syllogism with an Islamic topic, then deliberately break one condition to see how the conclusion fails.
For instance, use a fiqh ruling as the major and a specific case as the minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصحيحة، وإنما تطرق إليه الخلل من جهة كون صغراه سالبة، كقولك : (لا شيء من الحيوان بحجر وكل حجر جسم)، فإنه ينتج : (لا شيء من الحيوان بجسم) وهو كاذب؛ وذلك لكون الصغرى سالبة .
وكذلك لو جعلت الكبرى جزئية لم يلزم صدق النتيجة، كما لو قلت كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الحيوان فرس) فإنه ينتج : (بعض الإنسان فرس)، وهو كاذب، فتبين أنه إن اختل أحد الشرطين لم يلزم الإنتاج .
The correct [syllogism], but defect enters into it from the aspect of its minor premise being negative, as in your saying: 'No animal is a stone, and every stone is a body.' It yields: 'No animal is a body' — and it is false; this is because the minor is negative. Similarly, if you make the major particular, the truth of the conclusion does not necessarily follow, as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse.' It yields: 'Some human is a horse' — and it is false. So it is clear that if either condition is disturbed, production [of a true conclusion] does not necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage illustrates the two conditions required for a valid first-figure syllogism: the minor must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. The author demonstrates violations with concrete examples. First, if the minor is negative, as in 'No animal is a stone; every stone is a body' — the conclusion 'No animal is a body' is false because animals are indeed bodies. Second, if the major is particular, as in 'Every human is an animal; some animal is a horse' — the conclusion 'Some human is a horse' is false because no human is a horse. Thus, any deviation from the conditions invalidates the syllogism. These examples are drawn from commonsense knowledge, but the logical point is general: in the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. The author notes that if either condition is violated, the conclusion is not guaranteed — it may be false even if premises are true.

Key Terms
صغرى (sughra): The minor premise of a syllogism, containing the subject of the conclusion.
كبرى (kubra): The major premise of a syllogism, containing the predicate of the conclusion.
سالبة (saliba): A negative proposition (e.g., 'No S is P' or 'Some S is not P').
جزئية (juz'iyya): A particular proposition (e.g., 'Some S is P' or 'Some S is not P').

C) Every scholar is respected; some respected is learned; therefore some scholar is learned.
D) Every believer is truthful; every truthful is honest; therefore every believer is honest.

Key Takeaway
In the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and the major universal; violation of either condition breaks the necessity of the conclusion.

Two Methods for Identifying Productive Moods: Tahsil vs Isqat
Author introduces two methods: tahsil and isqat, preferring tahsil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the method of tahsil and the method of isqat for identifying productive syllogistic moods, and explain why tahsil is preferred.

Core Idea
Tahsil lists productive moods directly; isqat drops sterile ones—both achieve the same goal, but tahsil is more efficient.

In the study of categorical syllogisms, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. Scholars developed two approaches to separate productive moods (those that give a necessary result) from sterile ones. This lesson introduces these two methods and explains why the author chose the method of tahsil over isqat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
The author names two methods: tahsil (collection) and isqat (dropping). Tahsil directly enumerates the productive moods; isqat starts with all possibilities and eliminates sterile ones by applying conditions one by one.
2
Understand how isqat works
The author gives a glimpse: by requiring the minor premise to be affirmative, eight moods are immediately dropped. This is because if the minor is not affirmative, it is either universal negative or particular negative. Each of those combined with four possible majors gives eight sterile moods.
This sequential elimination is the core of isqat.
3
Note the author's choice
The author states they abandoned isqat and adopted tahsil for conciseness (ikhtisar). This implies tahsil is more direct and requires fewer steps for the final list.
4
Contrast the two approaches
Tahsil builds the set of productive moods from validity conditions; isqat subtracts sterile moods from the full set of combinations. Both arrive at the same list, but tahsil is more efficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذه الطريقة التي أردنا سلوكها في بيان المنتج من الضروب والعقيم هي المعروفة بطريق التحصيل .ولبيان العقيم طريقة أخرى تسمى طريق الإسقاط، كأن يقال : يَسقط بالشرط الأول الذي هو إيجاب الصغرى ثمانية ضروب ؛ لأنها إن لم تكن موجبة فهي إما أن تكون سالبة كلية أو سالبة جزئية، وكل واحدة منهما إنتاجها مع الكبريات الأربع ... إلخ .فقد تركنا هذه الطريقة المعروفة بطريقة الإسقاط، وبينا المقصود بالطريقة المسماة طريق التحصيل اختصارا .
Know that this method which we intended to follow in explaining the productive [moods] from the sterile is known as the method of tahsīl. For explaining the sterile there is another method called the method of isqāt, as in saying: 'By the first condition, which is affirmation of the minor, eight moods are dropped; because if they are not affirmative, then they are either universal negative or particular negative, and each of them with the four majors... etc.' So we have abandoned this method known as the method of isqāt, and we explained the intended by the method called tahsīl concisely.

Commentary
This passage introduces two approaches for determining which moods (ضروب) of a syllogistic figure are productive (produce a necessary conclusion) and which are sterile. The first method, called 'tahṣīl' (attainment/collection), directly enumerates the productive moods based on the validity conditions. The second method, called 'isqāṭ' (dropping), starts with all possible mood combinations (e.g., 4×4=16) and eliminates those that violate a condition. The author gives a glimpse: by requiring the minor to be affirmative, eight moods are immediately dropped (since the minor could be universal negative or particular negative, each combined with four major premises). The author states that they have chosen the method of tahṣīl for brevity and clarity, leaving aside the isqāṭ method. Note that 'tahṣīl' here refers to this specific logical method, not the contrast with 'ta'dīl' (modification) mentioned earlier in the book (p.91).

The Self-Evidence of the First Figure and Reduction of Other Figures
Author explains why the first figure is self-evident and how other figures reduce to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify the self-evident validity of the first figure and reduce a non-first figure categorical syllogism to it using simple conversion or premise exchange.

Core Idea
The first figure is self-evident because subsumption is transitive: if A is in B and B in C, then A is in C.

Why is the first figure of the categorical syllogism considered the most fundamental? In this passage, the author explains that its validity is an immediate rational judgment, based on the transitivity of class inclusion (indiraj). He also notes that all other figures can be reduced to this first figure through logical operations like simple conversion (عكس مستوي) or swapping premises. Understanding this lays the groundwork for mastering syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of transitivity
Find where the author describes the relationship of subsumption (indiraj) between the minor, middle, and major terms. Note that the mind judges necessarily that if the minor is under the middle and the middle under the major, then the minor is under the major.
This is the core rational basis.
2
Confirm the conditions for first figure validity
The author adds that the syllogism must be in the correct form fulfilling the conditions (e.g., affirmative minor, universal major). Without these, the transitivity does not guarantee truth.
These conditions are essential for the first figure's perfect validity.
3
Learn the two methods of reduction
The author mentions 'simple conversion' (عكس مستوي) and 'exchanging one premise with the other'. These operations transform second, third, or fourth figure syllogisms into the first figure.
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate; premise exchange swaps the two premises.
4
Test reduction with a concrete example
Take a second figure syllogism: 'No intoxicant is permissible; Khamr is an intoxicant; therefore Khamr is not permissible.' Convert the major premise (No intoxicant is permissible) to No permissible is intoxicant (simple conversion). Now the syllogism becomes first figure: 'No permissible is intoxicant; All khamr is intoxicant; No khamr is permissible.' The conclusion follows directly.
This shows how reduction works in practice.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Warning: Know that the basis of the rational judgment of the truth of the conclusion of this figure — which is the first figure — if its composition is in the correct form that fulfills the conditions, is that the mind judges with certainty that if the minor is subsumed under the middle, and the middle is subsumed under the major, then the minor is subsumed under the major. This is something about which no rational person doubts. And you will see that all figures reduce to the first by using a rational necessary implication among the implications of propositions — which is simple conversion — or by exchanging one of the two premises with the other, as you will see explained, God willing — He is Exalted.

Commentary
This passage explains why the first figure is self-evidently valid. The author states that when a syllogism is correctly formed in the first figure (affirmative minor, universal major), the mind necessarily judges that if the minor term (e.g., 'human') falls under the middle term (e.g., 'animal'), and the middle term falls under the major term (e.g., 'body'), then the minor falls under the major. This is the principle of transitivity of class inclusion. No rational person can doubt this. The author then mentions that all other figures (second, third, fourth) can be reduced to the first figure using logical operations: either by 'simple conversion' (عكس مستوي) of one premise, or by interchanging the premises (i.e., converting the syllogism to a first-figure form). This reduction shows the primacy of the first figure. The passage thus provides both an epistemological justification (intuitive certainty) and a methodological note about reduction.

Second Figure Conditions and Productive Moods
Author gives conditions and lists productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a valid second-figure categorical syllogism and enumerate its four productive moods.

Core Idea
Second figure requires one affirmative and one negative premise, and a universal major.

In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. This structure imposes strict conditions for the syllogism to yield a valid conclusion. The author explains that the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. Under these conditions, exactly four moods are productive. Let us work through them step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term position
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. So the form is: All/No/Some S is M, All/No/Some P is M, where M is the middle term.
The major term is the predicate of the conclusion, and the minor term is the subject.
2
Apply the quality condition
The two premises must differ in quality: one must be affirmative, the other negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, no conclusion follows.
3
Apply the quantity condition
The major premise (the one containing the major term, i.e., the predicate of the conclusion) must be universal. A particular major makes the syllogism invalid.
4
Derive the four moods
Consider two cases: (a) minor is affirmative → major must be universal negative → two moods based on minor quantity (universal or particular). (b) minor is negative → major must be universal affirmative → two moods based on minor quantity.
The two moods under (a) are: Universal affirm + universal neg; Particular affirm + universal neg. Under (b): Universal neg + universal affirm; Particular neg + universal affirm.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Example: 'No wine is permissible' (universal negative), 'Every nabidh is permissible' (universal affirmative) → Conclusion: 'No wine is nabidh'. This is a valid second figure mood (Cesare). Check conditions: premises differ in quality, major (every nabidh is permissible) is universal.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the second figure — which is that the middle term is predicated in both premises — two conditions are also required for its productivity: The first is with respect to quality, which is that its two premises differ in quality; so it is necessary that one be affirmative and the other negative. Thus, two negatives and two affirmatives do not produce. The second condition is with respect to quantity, which is that its major be universal; thus it does not produce with a particular major. Its productive moods according to the conditions are also four. So if you suppose that the minor is affirmative, then the major must be universal negative. This includes two forms: first, universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; second, particular affirmative minor and universal negative major. And if you suppose that the minor is negative, then the major must be universal affirmative. This includes two forms:

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the predicate in both premises (e.g., 'All A are B, No C is B'). The author states two necessary conditions for a valid second-figure syllogism. First, regarding quality: the two premises must differ in quality — one affirmative, one negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, no conclusion follows. Second, regarding quantity: the major premise must be universal. If the major is particular, the syllogism is sterile. Given these conditions, there are exactly four productive moods. The author explains them by considering two cases: (1) If the minor is affirmative, then the major must be universal negative. This yields two moods: universal affirmative + universal negative (e.g., Every/S is P, No M is P → No S is M), and particular affirmative + universal negative (e.g., Some S is P, No M is P → Some S is not M). (2) If the minor is negative, then the major must be universal affirmative. This yields two moods: universal negative + universal affirmative (e.g., No S is P, Every M is P → No S is M or Some S is not M?), and particular negative + universal affirmative. The text cuts off after 'صورتان', but the intended four moods correspond to the two cases each with two quantity combinations. The passage illustrates the systematic derivation of valid moods by applying the conditions.

The Syllogism

Second Figure: Conditions and Four Productive Moods
Author defines the second figure's conditions and lists its four productive moods with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of the second figure and construct its four productive moods.

Core Idea
The second figure always yields a negative conclusion; its premises must differ in quality and the major must be universal.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism is defined by the middle term being predicate in both premises. To produce a valid conclusion, two conditions must be met: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. These conditions generate exactly four productive moods (ضروب منتجة). Understanding them allows you to check syllogisms in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure's structure
Note that the middle term is the predicate in both premises. In the second figure, the major term is subject of the major premise, and the minor term is subject of the minor premise.
This differs from the first figure where the middle term is subject in one and predicate in the other.
2
Apply the quality condition
The two premises must differ in quality: one affirmative, one negative. Two affirmatives or two negatives yield no conclusion.
This condition is unique to the second figure.
3
Apply the quantity condition
The major premise must be universal. A particular major premise produces no conclusion.
The minor premise can be universal or particular.
4
Enumerate the four moods
Based on which premise is affirmative and the quantities of the premises, four combinations are valid: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major; (2) universal negative minor + universal affirmative major; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major; (4) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major.
All four moods yield a negative conclusion (universal or particular).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الثاني : الذي هو كون الحد الوسط محمولاً فيهما فيشترط لإنتاجه أيضًا شرطان:
الأول بحسب الكيف، وهو اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف، فلابد من كون إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة، فلا ينتج فيه سالبتان ولا موجبتان.
والشرط الثاني بحسب الكم، وهو كون كبراه كلية، فلا ينتج مع جزئية الكبرى.
وضروبه المنتجة بحسب الشرطين أربعة أيضاً، فلو فرضت أن الصغرى هي الموجبة لزم أن تكون الكبرى سالبة كلية، فيدخل في ذلك صورتان:
أولاهما: كلية موجبة صغرى، وكلية سالبة كبرى.
وثانيتهما: جزئية موجبة صغرى، وكلية سالبة كبرى.
ولو فرضت أن الصغرى هي السالبة لزم أن تكون الكبرى كلية موجبة، فيدخل في ذلك صورتان:
أولاهما: كلية سالبة صغرى، وكلية موجبة كبرى.
وثانيتهما: جزئية سالبة صغرى، وكلية موجبة كبرى.
وهذه هي ضروبه الأربعة المنتجة، وسنذكر بعض الأمثلة باختصار؛ لأنه يفهم منه غيرُه في العقليات والفقهيات والنحويات.
الضرب الأول: كليتان صغراهما موجبة وكبراهما سالبة، ينتج كلية سالبة، ومثاله: (كل ياقوت حجر، ولا شيء من إنسان بحجر)، ينتج: (لا شيء من الياقوت بإنسان).
الضرب الثاني: كليتان صغراهما سالبة وكبراهما موجبة، ينتج كلية سالبة، ومثاله: (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، وكل ياقوت حجر) ينتج: (لا شيء من الإنسان بياقوت).
الضرب الثالث: جزئية موجبة صغرى، وكلية سالبة كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة، ومثاله: (بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا شيء من الفرس بإنسان) ينتج: (بعض الحيوان ليس بفرس).
الضرب الرابع: جزئية سالبة صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة، ومثاله: (ليس بعض الحيوان ناطق، وكل إنسان ناطق) ينتج: (ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان).
As for the second figure, which is that the middle term is the predicate in both [premises], it also requires two conditions for its productivity:
The first is in terms of quality, which is that its two premises differ in quality; so one must be affirmative and the other negative. Thus neither two negatives nor two affirmatives produce [a conclusion] in it.
The second condition is in terms of quantity, which is that its major premise is universal; thus it does not produce with a particular major premise.
Its productive moods according to the two conditions are also four. If you suppose that the minor premise is the affirmative, then the major premise must be a universal negative. Two forms fall under this:
First: universal affirmative minor, universal negative major.
Second: particular affirmative minor, universal negative major.
If you suppose that the minor premise is the negative, then the major premise must be a universal affirmative. Two forms fall under this:
First: universal negative minor, universal affirmative major.
Second: particular negative minor, universal affirmative major.
These are its four productive moods. We will mention some examples briefly, because from them other [examples] are understood in intellectual, jurisprudential and grammatical matters.
The first mood: [two universals,] minor affirmative and major negative, produces a universal negative. Its example: 'Every ruby is a stone, and nothing of man is a stone' produces 'Nothing of the ruby is a man.'
The second mood: [two universals,] minor negative and major affirmative, produces a universal negative. Its example: 'Nothing of man is a stone, and every ruby is a stone' produces 'Nothing of man is a ruby.'
The third mood: particular affirmative minor, universal negative major, produces a particular negative. Its example: 'Some animal is a man, and nothing of the horse is a man' produces 'Some animal is not a horse.'
The fourth mood: particular negative minor, universal affirmative major, produces a particular negative. Its example: 'Some animal is not speaking, and every man is speaking' produces 'Some animal is not a man.'
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the second figure of the categorical syllogism (الشكل الثاني). The author first states the distinguishing feature of this figure: the middle term (الحد الوسط) is the predicate in both premises. Then he gives two conditions (شرطان) for a valid conclusion: (1) quality condition: the two premises must differ in quality—one must be affirmative and the other negative; two negatives or two affirmatives yield no conclusion; (2) quantity condition: the major premise (الكبرى) must be universal; a particular major premise does not produce a conclusion. After stating the conditions, the author divides the possible combinations based on which premise is affirmative. If the minor premise (الصغرى) is affirmative, then the major must be universal negative, giving two moods: (i) universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, and (ii) particular affirmative minor and universal negative major. If the minor is negative, then the major must be universal affirmative, giving two moods: (iii) universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, and (iv) particular negative minor and universal affirmative major. These are the four productive moods. The author then provides examples for each mood. The first mood (كلية موجبة صغرى، وكلية سالبة كبرى) yields a universal negative conclusion: 'Every ruby is a stone' and 'Nothing of man is a stone' validly conclude 'Nothing of the ruby is a man.' The second mood (كلية سالبة صغرى، وكلية موجبة كبرى) also yields a universal negative: 'Nothing of man is a stone' and 'Every ruby is a stone' conclude 'Nothing of man is a ruby.' The third mood (جزئية موجبة صغرى، وكلية سالبة كبرى) yields a particular negative: 'Some animal is a man' and 'Nothing of the horse is a man' conclude 'Some animal is not a horse.' The fourth mood (جزئية سالبة صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى) yields a particular negative: 'Some animal is not speaking' and 'Every man is speaking' conclude 'Some animal is not a man.' The examples use natural kinds (stone, ruby, man, horse, animal) and are suitable for intellectual sciences (عقليات), jurisprudence (فقهيات), and grammar (نحويات). The commentary follows the author's order: first the figure's definition, then conditions, then enumeration of moods, then examples.

The Syllogism

Recognizing the Four Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Enumerates four productive moods and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four productive moods of the second figure a given categorical syllogism belongs to.

Core Idea
Only four premise combinations in the second figure yield necessary conclusions; all others are sterile.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms places the middle term as the predicate of both premises. Not every pair of premises produces a valid conclusion. The author enumerates exactly four productive moods and illustrates each with an example from different sciences. In this lesson, you will learn to recognize these four moods by their premise patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Check if the middle term is the predicate of both premises. If yes, it is the second figure. If not, this guide does not apply.
2
Determine the quantity and quality of each premise
Classify each premise as universal or particular, affirmative or negative. Write them down symbolically (e.g., A, E, I, O).
Recall: A=universal affirmative, E=universal negative, I=particular affirmative, O=particular negative.
3
Match to one of the four productive patterns
The productive moods are: (1) minor A, major E → universal negative (E); (2) minor E, major A → universal negative (E); (3) minor I, major E → particular negative (O); (4) minor O, major A → particular negative (O). If the pattern matches one of these, it is productive; otherwise it is sterile.
Note that the major premise must be universal, and the minor premise must have the opposite quality of the major.

First: universal negative minor, universal affirmative major. Second: particular negative minor, universal affirmative major. These are its four productive moods, and we will mention some examples briefly because from them the rest can be understood in intellectual matters, jurisprudential matters, and grammatical matters. The first mood: two universals, minor affirmative and major negative, produces a universal negative. Example: 'Every ruby is a stone, and no human is a stone' produces 'No ruby is a human'. The second mood: two universals, minor negative and major affirmative, produces a universal negative. Example: 'No human is a stone, and every ruby is a stone' produces 'No human is a ruby'. The third mood: particular affirmative minor, universal negative major, produces a particular negative. Example: 'Some animal is human, and no horse is human' produces 'Some animal is not a horse'. The fourth mood: particular negative minor, universal affirmative major, produces a particular negative. Example: 'Some animal is not rational, and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is not a human'. Other than these four are sterile.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four productive moods of the second figure of categorical syllogism. The author first specifies the two forms of the minor premise: the first is universal negative and the second is particular negative, both paired with a universal affirmative major. Then he lists the four moods with examples from different sciences: mineralogy, anthropology, zoology, and psychology, indicating that these examples suffice to understand others in intellectual, legal, and grammatical fields. The first mood has both premises universal, minor affirmative and major negative, yielding a universal negative. The second mood also has both universal but with minor negative and major affirmative, also yielding a universal negative. The third mood has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative. The fourth mood has a particular negative minor and universal affirmative major, also yielding a particular negative. All non-conforming combinations are sterile (do not produce a conclusion). In Islamic logic, these moods are used to derive rulings; for example, from 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden' and 'No water is an intoxicant' we conclude 'No water is forbidden' (though that conclusion may be false in reality, it shows the logical form).

The Syllogism

Constructing Valid Second-Figure Syllogisms in Islamic Sciences
Enumerates four productive moods and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid second-figure categorical syllogism on an Islamic legal topic and verify its conclusion.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the conclusion is always negative; the major premise must be universal, the minor opposite in quality.

Building on the previous recognition of the four moods, this lesson trains you to actively construct second-figure syllogisms. Using examples from fiqh and kalam, you will learn to select appropriate premises that yield a valid negative conclusion. The author’s examples from mineralogy, anthropology, etc., illustrate the pattern; we will replace them with Islamic materials.

Guided Path
1
Choose your major term (P) and middle term (M)
The conclusion will be of the form 'No S is P' or 'Some S is not P'. The major term (P) is the predicate of the conclusion. The middle term (M) must be the predicate of both premises. For example, to prove 'No water is haram', choose M=intoxicant, P=haram.
Ensure M can serve as a proper predicate for S and P.
2
Decide the quantity of the conclusion
If you want a universal conclusion (all S are not P), both premises must be universal. If you want a particular conclusion (some S are not P), the minor premise must be particular.
All productive moods in the second figure require the major premise to be universal.
3
Formulate the major premise
The major premise (containing P and M) must be universal. Choose its quality opposite to the intended minor premise. For a universal negative conclusion (E), the major must be negative (E) and the minor affirmative (A). For a particular negative conclusion (O), the major must be negative (E) and the minor particular affirmative (I). Alternatively, you can use the all-negative forms: major A, minor E for universal; major A, minor O for particular.
There are two patterns for each conclusion type: one with major affirmative and minor negative, the other with major negative and minor affirmative. Both yield the same conclusion type.
4
Formulate the minor premise
The minor premise (containing S and M) must have the opposite quality to the major. If the major is affirmative (A), you need a negative minor (E or O). If the major is negative (E), you need an affirmative minor (A or I). Also, if you want a universal conclusion, both premises must be universal; for particular, the minor must be particular (I or O) while the major remains universal.
5
Derive the conclusion and verify
The conclusion follows automatically if the premises are in one of the four moods. The subject of the conclusion is the minor term (S), and the predicate is the major term (P). The conclusion is a universal or particular negative accordingly.
Double-check that your premises are true (or assumed true) to ensure soundness, but validity only concerns form.

Why the Second Figure Produces Only Negative Conclusions
Author explains the rational basis for the second figure's production of only negative conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rational basis for why the second figure of categorical syllogism yields only negative conclusions.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the same predicate is affirmed of one subject and denied of another, forcing the subjects to differ.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms is unique because it can only produce negative conclusions. This lesson unpacks the rational justification provided by the author: the middle term is the same in both premises, but its quality (affirmation or negation) differs, which forces the subjects to be different. Understanding this will deepen your appreciation of the syllogistic system used in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical structure of the second figure
Notice that in the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. The subjects of the premises are the extremes (major and minor terms).
This is a defining feature of the second figure.
2
Understand the condition of productivity
The author states that the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) for a conclusion to follow. This is the condition for productivity (sharṭ al-intāj).
If both premises were affirmative or both negative, no valid conclusion would be possible.
3
Trace the effect of quality difference
Because the middle term is the same in both premises, the difference in quality means that the predicate is affirmed of one subject and denied of the other subject.
This is the key insight: the same thing is both asserted and denied, but of different subjects.
4
Infer the necessary consequence
If the two subjects were the same, then the same predicate would be both affirmed and denied of that single subject, which is contradictory. Therefore, the subjects must be different in some respect.
This is the rational necessity (ʿaql) that the author emphasizes.
5
Conclude that the conclusion is negative
Since the subjects differ, the conclusion must state that they are different, which is expressed as a negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B' or 'Some A is not B').
Thus the second figure only produces negative conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهان يتعلقان بهذا الشكل الذي هو الشكل الثاني : لأحدهما الأول: اعلم أن وجه الحكم العقلي في إنتاجه أن المحمول فيه مثبت لأحد موضوعين، وهو أي المحمول بعينه منفي عن الموضوع الآخر، فيلزم عقلاً اختلاف الموضوعين؛ إذ لو لم يختلفا لما ثبت منفي شيء عن الآخر؛ وذلك لأن المحمول في الشكل الثاني واحد في المقدمتين، وأن اختلافهما في الكيف الذي هو شرطي إنتاجه يلزمه ثبوته لأحدهما ونفيه عن الآخر، وذلك يلزمه اختلافهما ـ أعني موضوعي المقدمتين - في الجملة، ولذلك لا يُنتج الشكل الثاني إلا سالبة.
Two notes related to this figure, which is the second figure: As for the first one: Know that the rational basis for its productivity is that the predicate in it is affirmed of one of the two subjects, and that same predicate is denied of the other subject, so rationality necessitates the difference of the two subjects; for if they did not differ, then the negation of a thing from the other would not be established; that is because the predicate in the second figure is one in both premises, and their difference in quality, which is the condition for its productivity, necessitates its affirmation for one and its negation from the other, and that necessitates their difference—I mean the subjects of the two premises—in general. Therefore the second figure produces only a negative [conclusion].
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage provides the rational justification for why the second figure only yields negative conclusions. The author explains that the key is the middle term (predicate in both premises) being the same, but the quality (affirmative/negative) differs between the two premises. This difference forces the subjects to be different in some respect, leading to a negative conclusion. The logical reasoning is that if the subjects were identical, then the same predicate would be both affirmed and denied of the same subject, which is contradictory. In Islamic logic, this underlies why from 'Every khamr is intoxicating' and 'No water is intoxicating' we validly conclude 'No water is khamr'—the predicate 'intoxicating' is affirmed of khamr and denied of water, so they must be different things.

Key Takeaway

Reducing the Second Figure to the First by Conversion
Author shows reduction of second figure to first via conversion and failure when conditions are violated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a second figure categorical syllogism to the first figure by converting its major premise, and determine when no conclusion follows due to violated conditions.

Core Idea
Conversion of the major premise turns second figure into first—but only if the conditions hold.

The second figure of categorical syllogism can be transformed into the first figure by applying simple conversion to its major premise. However, this procedure only works when the syllogism's premises satisfy the productivity conditions of the second figure: difference in quality and universality of the major premise. If one condition is violated, no conclusion follows. In this lesson, we examine the reduction technique and its limitations through concrete examples, drawing from Islamic juridical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
Locate a second figure syllogism: two premises sharing a common predicate (the middle term is predicate of both). Example: 'Every ruby is a stone' (minor) and 'No human is a stone' (major). The major premise contains the major term (human) and the middle term (stone).
2
Apply simple conversion to the major premise
Convert the major premise by swapping subject and predicate without changing quality or quantity. For the E proposition 'No human is a stone', simple conversion yields 'No stone is a human'. Keep the minor premise unchanged.
Simple conversion is valid only for E and I propositions.
3
Check the resulting figure
After conversion, the middle term (stone) is now subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise—that is the first figure. The syllogism 'Every ruby is a stone; no stone is a human' yields a valid Celarent syllogism with the conclusion 'No ruby is a human'.
The original conclusion 'No human is a ruby' is the converse of the new conclusion.
4
Test the conditions for failure
The author states that if either condition (difference in quality or universality of the major) is violated, no conclusion follows. Example: two affirmative premises 'Every human is an animal; every horse is an animal'—both same quality. Conversion of the major (still affirmative) does not create a valid first figure. Show that no conversion can fix a violation of the conditions.
The example from the passage illustrates this: no conclusion results from two affirmatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني : اعلم أن هذا الشكل الثاني إذا عكست كبراه بالعكس المستوي رجع إلى الشكل الأول بعينه . فالمثال الأول الذي مثلنا به وهو كل ياقوت حجر، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر) لو عكست كبراه بالعكس المستوي فقلت: (كل ياقوت حجر، ولا شيء من الحجر بإنسان) رجع إلى الشكل الأول كما ترى والنتيجة فيهما كلية سالبة إلا أنها في الأول: (لا شيء من الياقوت بإنسان، وفي الثاني : (لا شيء من الإنسان بياقوت)، فانعكست النتيجة بعكس الكبرى ، ولو اختل أحد الشرطين المذكورين في هذا الشكل الذي هو الثاني لم تلزم نتيجة . فمثال الموجبتين : كل إنسان حيوان وكل فرس حيوان ينتج :
The second note: Know that this second figure, if you convert its major premise by simple conversion, it returns to the first figure itself. So the first example we gave, which is 'Every ruby is a stone, and no human is a stone,' if you convert its major premise by simple conversion and say: 'Every ruby is a stone, and no stone is a human,' it returns to the first figure as you see, and the conclusion in both is universal negative, except that in the first it is 'No ruby is a human,' and in the second it is 'No human is a ruby,' so the conclusion is reversed by the conversion of the major. And if one of the two conditions mentioned in this figure, which is the second, is violated, no conclusion follows. For example, with two affirmatives: 'Every human is an animal, and every horse is an animal' produces...
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This note explains how the second figure can be reduced to the first figure by converting the major premise using simple conversion (عكس مستوي). The author illustrates with the earlier example: after converting the major premise 'No human is a stone' to 'No stone is a human,' the syllogism becomes a first figure syllogism with the same universal negative conclusion, though the subject and predicate are swapped. He then states that if either of the two conditions for second figure productivity (quality difference and the major being universal? The text says 'الشرطين المذكورين' - the two conditions mentioned earlier, likely the conditions for production) are violated, no conclusion follows. The example given is two affirmative premises, which do not meet the condition of differing quality; hence no conclusion. In Islamic logic, this shows that 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every horse is an animal' yield no necessary conclusion about human and horse because the predicate is affirmed of both.

Key Takeaway
The second figure can be reduced to the first by converting the major premise, but only if the premises satisfy the conditions of differing quality and universal major; otherwise, no conclusion is produced.

The Syllogism

Reducing the Second Figure to the First via Conversion
Author shows that converting the major premise of the second figure transforms it into the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a second figure categorical syllogism into the first figure by applying simple conversion to its major premise and derive the corresponding transformed conclusion.

Core Idea
The second figure reduces to the first through a single conversion step—unveiling the foundational role of the first figure.

In this lesson, we learn a powerful transformation: any valid second figure syllogism can be turned into a first figure syllogism by converting its major premise. This shows the primacy of the first figure and ties together two seemingly different argument forms. The author illustrates this with a concrete mineralogy example, but the technique applies in any Islamic science where syllogisms are used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure syllogism
Locate the major premise (contains the major term as subject and middle term as predicate) and minor premise (contains the minor term as subject and middle term as predicate). In the example: 'Every ruby is a stone' (minor) and 'No human is a stone' (major).
The middle term here is 'stone'.
2
Apply simple conversion to the major premise
Swap the subject and predicate of the major premise while preserving truth and the universal negative (E) quality. 'No human is a stone' becomes 'No stone is a human'.
For E and I propositions, simple conversion preserves truth.
3
Rewrite the syllogism with the converted major
Now the premises are: 'Every ruby is a stone' and 'No stone is a human'. This matches the first figure: the middle term 'stone' is subject in the major and predicate in the minor.
The first figure requires the middle term to be predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
4
Derive the new conclusion
From the first figure, the conclusion follows directly: 'No ruby is a human'. Compare to the original conclusion of the second figure: 'No human is a ruby'. The new conclusion is the converse of the original.
The conversion of the major premise also converts the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني: اعلم أن هذا الشكل الثاني إذا عكست كبراه بالعكس المستوي رجع إلى الشكل الأول بعينه. فالمثال الأول الذي مثلنا به وهو كل ياقوت حجر، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر) لو عكست كبراه بالعكس المستوي فقلت: (كل ياقوت حجر، ولا شيء من الحجر بإنسان رجع إلى الشكل الأول كما ترى والنتيجة فيهما كلية سالبة إلا أنها في الأول: (لا شيء من الياقوت بإنسان، وفي الثاني : (لا شيء من الإنسان بياقوت)، فانعكست النتيجة بعكس الكبرى.
The second note: Know that this second figure, if you convert its major premise by simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi), it returns exactly to the first figure. So the first example we gave — 'Every ruby is a stone, and no human is a stone' — if you convert its major by simple conversion and say: 'Every ruby is a stone, and no stone is a human', it returns to the first figure as you see, and the conclusion in both is a universal negative, except that in the first it is 'No ruby is a human' and in the second it is 'No human is a ruby'. So the conclusion is converted by the conversion of the major.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage explains a key logical technique: reduction of the second figure to the first. The author says that if you take the second figure syllogism and apply simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) to its major premise (the premise containing the middle term as predicate and the major term as subject), it becomes a first figure syllogism. He illustrates with a concrete example from minerals: 'Every ruby is a stone; no human is a stone' (second figure). Converting the major 'No human is a stone' to 'No stone is a human' yields 'Every ruby is a stone; no stone is a human', which is now the first figure. The conclusion originally was 'No ruby is a human', and after conversion it becomes 'No human is a ruby' — the conclusion is also converted. This shows the interdependence of the figures and how the first figure is the foundational one.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
كبرى
الشكل الأول

The Syllogism
