Conditions for a Valid Second Figure Syllogism
Author lists the two conditions for the second figure and shows violations with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify violations of the two conditions for second figure syllogisms and predict whether a conclusion follows.

Core Idea
A valid second figure requires differing premise qualities and a universal major.

In the previous lesson, you learned the structure of the second figure (P – M, S – M). Now the author explains the two conditions that must hold for a second figure syllogism to produce a valid conclusion. Without these conditions, the syllogism yields absurd results.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The author states two conditions: (1) the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal.
2
Examine violation 1: both affirmatives
The example 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every horse is an animal' leads to 'Every human is a horse', which is false. Both premises are affirmative, violating the first condition.
3
Examine violation 2: both negatives
The example 'Nothing human is a stone' and 'Nothing rational is a stone' leads to 'Nothing human is rational', which is false. Both premises are negative, again violating the first condition.
4
Examine violation 3: major not universal
The example 'Every human is an animal' (universal) and 'Some body is not an animal' (particular) leads to 'Some human is not a body', which is false. The major premise is not universal, violating the second condition.
5
Generalize the rule for validity
For a second figure syllogism to be valid, the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. If either condition is missing, no valid conclusion follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختل أحد الشرطين المذكورين في هذا الشكل الذي هو الثاني لم تلزم نتيجة. فمثال الموجبتين: كل إنسان حيوان وكل فرس حيوان ينتج: كل إنسان فرس، وهو كذب كما ترى. ومثال السالبتين: (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، ولا شيء من الناطق بحجر) ينتج: (لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق)، وهو كذب كما ترى؛ وذلك لعدم اختلاف الكيف. ولو اختل الشرط الثاني — وهو كون كبراه كلية — لم يلزم الإنتاج أيضاً، كما لو قلت: كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الجسم ليس بحيوان) ينتج: (بعض) الإنسان ليس بجسم، وهو كذب كما ترى؛ وذلك لعدم كلية الكبرى. وقس على ذلك.
And if one of the two mentioned conditions in this figure, which is the second, is violated, no conclusion follows. So the example of two affirmatives: 'Every human is an animal, and every horse is an animal' yields 'Every human is a horse', which is false as you see. And the example of two negatives: 'Nothing human is a stone, and nothing rational is a stone' yields 'Nothing human is rational', which is false as you see. And that is because of the lack of difference in quality. And if the second condition — which is that its major premise is universal — is violated, production also does not occur, as when you say: 'Every human is an animal, and some body is not an animal' yields 'Some human is not a body', which is false as you see. And that is because the major is not universal. And measure accordingly.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the indispensability of the two conditions for the second figure: (1) the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. The author gives three counterexamples. First, if both premises are affirmative, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every horse is an animal', the absurd conclusion 'Every human is a horse' follows. Second, if both are negative, e.g., 'No human is a stone' and 'No rational being is a stone', the false conclusion 'No human is rational' follows; these both violate the first condition. Third, if the major is particular, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some body is not an animal', the conclusion 'Some human is not a body' is false. Thus both conditions are necessary for a valid second figure syllogism.

Defining the Third Figure: Middle Term as Subject
Defines the third figure's structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining feature of the third figure by locating the middle term's position in both premises.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises.

The third figure is one of the four figures of categorical syllogism. Its unique structure determines which moods are valid. Understanding this figure helps you analyze arguments where the middle term functions as a subject in both premises, a common pattern in theological and juridical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term in the passage
Find the term that connects the two premises. The author says it is the subject in both premises.
The middle term is defined as al-hadd al-wasat.
2
Distinguish from other figures
In the first figure, the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor. In the third, it is subject in both. Compare these roles.
Use the key terms and definitions.
3
Construct a sample third figure syllogism
Build a syllogism where the middle term is subject in both premises. Example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every intoxicant is a substance. Therefore some substances are forbidden.' Here the middle term 'intoxicant' is subject in both premises.
The conclusion is particular due to figure conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الثالث: وهو ما كان الحد الوسط فيه موضوعًا في كلتا المقدمتين، فيشترط لإنتاجه أيضًا شرطان: الأول بحسب الكيف، وهو كون صغراه موجبة، والثاني بحسب الكم، وهو كون إحدى مقدمتيه كلية.
As for the third figure: it is that in which the middle term is the subject in both premises. For its production, two conditions are also required: the first concerning quality, which is that its minor premise be affirmative; the second concerning quantity, which is that one of its two premises be universal.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
The author now introduces the third figure of the categorical syllogism. In this figure, the middle term (hadd wasat) acts as the subject term in both premises — meaning it is the subject of the minor premise and also the subject of the major premise. The conditions for its validity are: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative (so it cannot be negative), and (2) at least one of the two premises must be universal (they cannot both be particular). This sets the stage for listing the six productive moods that satisfy these conditions.

Conditions for Productive Third Figure Syllogisms
Lists the two conditions for the third figure's productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two conditions (minor affirmative and one premise universal) to verify whether a given third figure mood is productive.

Core Idea
A third figure syllogism must have an affirmative minor premise and at least one universal premise to yield a conclusion.

After understanding the third figure's structure, the author presents two necessary conditions for it to produce a valid conclusion. These conditions govern the quality (kayf) and quantity (kamm) of the premises. Mastering them allows you to quickly assess any third figure mood without memorizing all valid forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is the one that contains the subject of the conclusion. In a third figure syllogism, both premises share the middle term as subject. Determine which premise is the minor.
The minor premise usually has the minor term (subject of conclusion) as predicate.
2
Check the quality of the minor premise
The first condition: the minor premise must be affirmative (mujiba). Look for a negative word like 'laysa' or 'la'. If the minor is negative, the syllogism is unproductive.
This condition ensures that the minor term is included in the middle term.
3
Check the quantity of both premises
The second condition: at least one premise must be universal (kulliya). If both premises are particular (juz'iyya), no conclusion follows. Identify universal quantifiers like 'kull' or 'la ahad'.
If both are particular, there isn't enough logical coverage.
4
Apply to an example
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every intoxicant is a substance. Therefore some substances are forbidden.' Minor premise: 'Every intoxicant is a substance' (affirmative, universal). Major: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (affirmative, universal). Both conditions satisfied: minor affirmative, at least one universal. So it is productive (Darapti).
Now try a mood with negative minor to see it fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الثالث: وهو ما كان الحد الوسط فيه موضوعًا في كلتا المقدمتين، فيشترط لإنتاجه أيضًا شرطان: الأول بحسب الكيف، وهو كون صغراه موجبة، والثاني بحسب الكم، وهو كون إحدى مقدمتيه كلية.
As for the third figure: it is that in which the middle term is the subject in both premises. For its production, two conditions are also required: the first concerning quality, which is that its minor premise be affirmative; the second concerning quantity, which is that one of its two premises be universal.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
The author now introduces the third figure of the categorical syllogism. In this figure, the middle term (hadd wasat) acts as the subject term in both premises — meaning it is the subject of the minor premise and also the subject of the major premise. The conditions for its validity are: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative (so it cannot be negative), and (2) at least one of the two premises must be universal (they cannot both be particular). This sets the stage for listing the six productive moods that satisfy these conditions.

Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Author enumerates the six productive moods of the third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six productive moods of the third figure and determine whether a given set of third-figure premises yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Only six premise combinations are productive in the third figure—governed by two conditions.

In the third figure of the categorical syllogism, both premises share a middle term that is the subject. Not every combination yields a valid conclusion. In this passage, the author systematically shows exactly six moods that work. This lesson breaks down the enumeration and equips you to recognize productive third-figure moods in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two conditions for third-figure productivity
The passage states that for a third-figure syllogism to be productive, the minor premise must be affirmative and one of the premises must be universal. Identify these conditions in the Arabic.
2
Count moods with universal affirmative minor
If the minor is universal affirmative, it satisfies both conditions (affirmative and universal), so it can combine with all four types of major (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). This yields four moods.
3
Count moods with particular affirmative minor
If the minor is particular affirmative, it is not universal, so the major must be universal to meet the second condition. The major can be affirmative or negative, giving two additional moods.
4
Sum the two groups to get six total moods
Four moods from the universal minor case plus two from the particular minor case equal six productive moods.
5
Write the abstract form of the first mood
The first mood uses two universal affirmative premises: كل أ ب (Every A is B) and كل أ ج (Every A is C), yielding the conclusion بعض ب ج (Some B is C). This is the pattern Darapti.
The text cuts off after 'كقولك:' but the standard example is 'كل إنسان حيوان، كل إنسان ناطق، ينتج بعض الحيوان ناطق'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصور الداخلة فيه بحسب الشرطين ستة، فضروبه المنتجة ستة. وإيضاحه أن الصغرى لا تكون إلا موجبة، وحينئذ إما كلية فتنتج مع الكبريات الأربع؛ لأن الصغرى إن كانت كلية موجبة اجتمع فيها الشرطان فأنتجت مع صور الكبرى الأربع، وإما جزئية موجبة فيلزم كون الكبرى كلية، وهي إما موجبة وإما سالبة، فتضم هاتين الصورتين إلى الأربع التي قبلها فيكون المجموع ستة أضرب: الضرب الأول كليتان موجبتان ينتج جزئية موجبة، كقولك:
The forms that enter into it according to the two conditions are six, so its productive moods are six. Its clarification: the minor is only affirmative, and then either universal, so it produces with the four majors; because if the minor is universal affirmative, both conditions are met, so it produces with the four forms of the major; or it is particular affirmative, then the major must be universal, and it is either affirmative or negative. So these two forms are added to the preceding four, making a total of six moods. The first mood: both universal affirmatives produce a particular affirmative, as when you say: ...
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage systematically lists the six productive moods (adrib) of the third figure. The author explains that the minor premise must be affirmative. It can be either universal or particular. If the minor is universal affirmative, then it satisfies both conditions (minor affirmative and one premise universal), so it can combine with all four possible major premises (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), yielding four moods. If the minor is particular affirmative, then the major must be universal to fulfil the second condition; the major can be either affirmative or negative, giving two additional moods. Thus total six. The author begins to illustrate the first mood: both universal affirmatives yield a particular affirmative conclusion. An example would be 'Every A is B, every A is C, therefore some B is C' (a standard third figure mood). The text cuts off but the pattern is clear.

The Syllogism

Third Figure: Conditions and Six Valid Modes
Author states conditions and enumerates six productive modes of the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure, verify its two conditions, and enumerate its six productive modes.

Core Idea
The third figure forces a particular conclusion and requires an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise.

The third figure is a powerful syllogistic form where the middle term is the subject in both premises. Its rules are strict: the minor must be affirmative and one premise must be universal. This lesson walks through its six valid modes, shows how they are derived from the conditions, and provides examples that reveal the underlying logical structure. Mastering these modes equips you to recognize valid third-figure syllogisms in Islamic scholarly arguments.

Guided Path
1
Define the third figure
Identify that in the third figure, the middle term (al-ḥadd al-wasaṭ) is the subject in both premises. For example, if the minor is 'Every human is an animal' and the major is 'Every human is rational', the middle term 'human' is the subject in both.
This contrasts with the first figure where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
2
State the two conditions
The minor premise must be affirmative (condition of quality). One of the two premises must be universal (condition of quantity). Without both conditions, no conclusion necessarily follows.
The condition 'one premise universal' means at least one is universal; both can be universal.
3
Derive the six modes
Since the minor is always affirmative, two cases arise: (a) minor is universal affirmative – then it satisfies both conditions, so the major can be any of the four categorical forms (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) – giving four modes. (b) minor is particular affirmative – then the major must be universal to satisfy the universality condition, and the major can be affirmative or negative – giving two more modes. Total six.
The author uses the fact that the minor is only affirmative to split the derivation.
4
Identify each mode's premises and conclusion
For each mode, note the quantity and quality of the premises and confirm that the conclusion is always particular (juz'iyya): affirmative or negative. The six modes are: 1) UAUA→P A; 2) UAUEN→P E; 3) PAUA→P A; 4) UAPA→P A; 5) PAUEN→P E; 6) UAPEN→P E (where U=universal, P=particular, A=affirmative, E=negative).
Memorizing the patterns helps in quick recognition.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Apply the third figure to a fiqh example: 'Every khamr (wine) is haram (forbidden); every khamr is an intoxicant; therefore, some intoxicant is haram.' This is mode 1 (both universal affirmative). Check the conditions: minor 'Every khamr is haram' is affirmative and universal; major 'Every khamr is an intoxicant' is affirmative universal; conclusion is particular affirmative. The syllogism is valid.
Ensure the terms are arranged correctly: middle term 'khamr' is subject in both premises, minor term 'haram' is predicate of minor, major term 'intoxicant' is predicate of major, conclusion has subject 'intoxicant' and predicate 'haram'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الثالث : وهو ما كان الحد الوسط فيه موضوعًا في كلتا المقدمتين، فيشترط لإنتاجه أيضًا :شرطان الأول بحسب الكيف، وهو كون صغراه موجبة، والثاني بحسب الكم، وهو كون إحدى مقدمتيه كلية .والصور الداخلة فيه بحسب الشرطين ستة ،فضروبه المنتجة ستة وإيضاحه أن الصغرى لا تكون إلا موجبة، وحينئذ إما كلية فتنتج مع الكبريات الأربع ؛ لأن الصغرى إن كانت كلية موجبة اجتمع فيها الشرطان فأنتجت مع صور الكبرى الأربع، وإما جزئية موجبة فيلزم كون الكبرى كلية، وهي إما موجبة وإما سالبة، فتضم هاتين الصورتين إلى الأربع التي قبلها فيكون المجموع ستة أضرب :الضرب الأول كليتان ،موجبتان ينتج جزئية موجبة، كقولك: كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج : (بعض الحيوان ناطق). الضرب الثاني: كليتان صغراهما موجبة وكبراهما سالبة، ينتج جزئية سالبة، كقولك : كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بفرس ينتج : ليس بعض الحيوان بفرس) .الضرب الثالث : موجبتان صغراهما جزئية، وكبراهما كلية ينتج جزئية موجبة ومثاله بعض الحيوان إنسان وكل حيوان حساس ينتج : (بعض الإنسان حساس).الضرب الرابع موجبتان صغراهما كلية وكبراهما جزئية، ينتج جزئية موجبة مثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الإنسان ناطق ينتج : (بعض الحيوان ناطق).الضرب الخامس : موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة مثاله : (بعض) الحيوان ناطق، ولا شيء من الحيوان بجماد ينتج : (بعض الناطق ليس بجماد).الضرب السادس موجبة كلية صغرى وجزئية سالبة كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة . مثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الإنسان ليس بفرس ينتج : ( بعض الحيوان ليس بفرس).
As for the third figure: it is that in which the middle term is subject in both premises. For its productivity, two conditions are also required: the first with respect to quality, namely that its minor premise is affirmative; the second with respect to quantity, namely that one of its two premises is universal. The forms included in it according to the two conditions are six, so its productive modes are six. Its explanation is that the minor premise is only affirmative; then it is either universal, so it produces with the four major premises, because if the minor is universal affirmative, both conditions are met, so it produces with the four forms of the major; or it is particular affirmative, so the major must be universal, and it is either affirmative or negative; so these two forms are added to the previous four, making six modes. The first mode: both universal, both affirmative, produces a particular affirmative, as if you say: Every human is an animal and every human is rational, produces 'Some animal is rational'. The second mode: both universal, minor affirmative and major negative, produces a particular negative, as if you say: Every human is an animal and no human is a horse, produces 'Some animal is not a horse'. The third mode: both affirmative, minor particular and major universal, produces a particular affirmative; its example: Some animal is a human and every animal is sentient, produces 'Some human is sentient'. The fourth mode: both affirmative, minor universal and major particular, produces a particular affirmative; its example: Every human is an animal and some human is rational, produces 'Some animal is rational'. The fifth mode: minor particular affirmative and major universal negative, produces a particular negative; its example: Some animal is rational and no animal is inanimate, produces 'Some rational is not inanimate'. The sixth mode: minor universal affirmative and major particular negative, produces a particular negative; its example: Every human is an animal and some human is not a horse, produces 'Some animal is not a horse'.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism and enumerates its six valid modes. The author begins by identifying the third figure: it is the figure in which the middle term is the subject in both premises. For the syllogism to be productive (i.e., yield a necessary conclusion), two conditions are required: first, regarding quality, that the minor premise is affirmative; second, regarding quantity, that at least one of the premises is universal. The author then explains how these conditions yield exactly six productive modes. Since the minor must be affirmative, it is either universal or particular. If the minor is universal affirmative, both conditions are satisfied, and the major can be any of the four categorical forms (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) – this gives four modes. If the minor is particular affirmative, then the major must be universal to satisfy the universality condition, and the major can be either affirmative or negative – this adds two modes, totaling six. The six modes are then listed with examples. Each mode uses terms like 'human', 'animal', 'rational', 'horse', 'sentient', 'inanimate'. For instance, the first mode has both premises universal affirmative, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion: 'Every human is an animal; every human is rational; therefore, some animal is rational.' The second mode has universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion: 'Every human is an animal; no human is a horse; therefore, some animal is not a horse.' And so on for all six, illustrating the combinations.

صغرى
كبرى
موجبة
سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third figure, what must be true about the minor premise?
A) It must be universal.
B) It must be affirmative.
C) It must be particular.
D) It must be negative.

Key Takeaway
The third figure yields only particular conclusions and requires an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid third figure syllogism on a fiqh topic. Use 'every intoxicant is forbidden' as one premise and 'every intoxicant is a beverage' as the other. Determine which mode it is and verify the conclusion.
?
Analyze the following syllogism: 'Every wine is impure; some wine is intoxicating; therefore, some intoxicant is impure.' Is this a valid third figure syllogism? Check the conditions and identify the mode.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Why the Third Figure Yields Only a Particular Conclusion
Author begins explaining that the third figure yields only particular conclusions due to structural constraints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structural reason why third-figure syllogisms always produce a particular conclusion, and distinguish it from other figures.

Core Idea
The third figure's conclusion has its subject from the minor's predicate, which may be particular, so universality is impossible.

In this lesson, we explore a fundamental property of the third figure of categorical syllogisms. The author explains that the conclusion is always particular due to the roles of the terms. We will trace why the structure forces particularity, even when both premises appear universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term positions in third figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. The predicate of the minor premise is the minor term, and the predicate of the major premise is the major term.
Contrast this with first figure where middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor.
2
Analyze the conclusion's composition
The text states: the conclusion consists of the predicate of the minor (as subject) and the predicate of the major (as predicate). So the subject of the conclusion is the minor term (predicate of minor premise).
This is unique to the third figure; other figures have different term arrangements.
3
Recognize why the conclusion must be particular
In the third figure, the minor premise is always affirmative. In an affirmative premise, the predicate (minor term) is not distributed (i.e., not taken universally). Therefore, the subject of the conclusion (the minor term) cannot be taken universally, so the conclusion must be particular.
Even if both premises are universal, the minor term remains undistributed, forcing a particular conclusion (e.g., Darapti: All A are B, All A are C → Some B are C).
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the syllogism: 'Every human is mortal; Every human is rational; Therefore, some mortal is rational.' The minor premise is 'Every human is mortal', predicate 'mortal' is undistributed. Thus the conclusion's subject 'mortal' cannot be universal, yielding 'some'.
This illustrates the rule: third figure cannot produce a universal conclusion.

Source Text

تنبيهات تتعلق بهذا الشكل الثالث :الأول: اعلم أن كون هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا جزئية سببه في الحقيقة أن نتيجته متركبة من محمول الصغرى، ومحمول الكبرى ومحمول الصغرى هو موضوع النتيجة ومن الجائز عقلاً أن يكون
Remarks pertaining to this third figure: First: Know that this figure yields only a particular conclusion; its reason in truth is that its conclusion is composed of the predicate of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise, and the predicate of the minor premise is the subject of the conclusion, and it is intellectually possible that it be...
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage begins a set of remarks about the third figure. The first remark explains why the third figure never yields a universal conclusion, only a particular one. The reason given is that the conclusion is composed of the predicate of the minor premise (which becomes the subject of the conclusion) and the predicate of the major premise (which becomes the predicate of the conclusion). The predicate of the minor premise, being the subject of the conclusion, could in principle be universal or particular. However, the text is cut off; it likely continues by pointing out that because the minor premise is always affirmative and may be particular, the subject of the conclusion may be particular, and since the third figure's conditions do not force universality, the conclusion is always particular. This is a deeper logical insight into why third-figure syllogisms cannot produce universal conclusions.

The Syllogism

Six moods of the third figure of syllogism
Author enumerates the six moods of the third figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantity and quality of premises in any valid third-figure syllogism and determine the resulting particular conclusion.

Core Idea
All valid third-figure moods yield only particular conclusions, never universal.

The third figure of categorical syllogism is characterized by the middle term being the subject in both premises. Unlike the first two figures, which can produce universal conclusions, the third figure always yields a particular conclusion. In this lesson, we will work through the six valid moods (adrab) of the third figure, as enumerated in classical logic texts, learning how to parse each mood's premises and derive the correct conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third figure
Recognize that the middle term is the subject in both premises. In the given examples, the middle term is 'human' or 'animal' — it appears as subject in both the minor and major premises.
The third figure is defined by the position of the middle term.
2
Extract the quantity and quality of each premise
For each mood, note whether the minor premise (صغرى) and major premise (كبرى) are universal (كلية) or particular (جزئية), and affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة). For example, the first mood has two universal affirmatives.
Knowledge of proposition types (A, E, I, O) is helpful.
3
Determine the conclusion
Apply the rule: the conclusion is always particular. It is affirmative only if both premises are affirmative; otherwise it is negative. Confirm with the example given by the author.
The third figure never yields a universal conclusion.
4
Verify against the six moods
Check that your derived conclusion matches the author's example for each mood. The six moods cover all valid combinations of premise types in the third figure.
Moods 1 and 2 use two universal premises; moods 3-6 use mixtures of universal and particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج: (بعض الحيوان ناطق). الضرب الثاني: كليتان صغراهما موجبة وكبراهما سالبة، ينتج جزئية سالبة، كقولك: كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بفرس ينتج: (ليس بعض الحيوان بفرس). الضرب الثالث: موجبتان صغراهما جزئية، وكبراهما كلية ينتج جزئية موجبة ومثاله: بعض الحيوان إنسان وكل حيوان حساس ينتج: (بعض الإنسان حساس). الضرب الرابع: موجبتان صغراهما كلية وكبراهما جزئية، ينتج جزئية موجبة مثاله: (كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الإنسان ناطق ينتج: (بعض الحيوان ناطق). الضرب الخامس: موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة مثاله: (بعض الحيوان ناطق، ولا شيء من الحيوان بجماد ينتج: (بعض الناطق ليس بجماد). الضرب السادس: موجبة كلية صغرى وجزئية سالبة كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة. مثاله: (كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الإنسان ليس بفرس ينتج: (بعض الحيوان ليس بفرس).
Every human is an animal and every human is rational produces: (some animal is rational). The second mood: two universals, the minor affirmative and the major negative, produces a particular negative, as you say: Every human is an animal and no human is a horse produces: (not every animal is a horse). The third mood: both affirmatives, the minor particular and the major universal, produces a particular affirmative; its example: some animal is a human and every animal is sentient produces: (some human is sentient). The fourth mood: both affirmatives, the minor universal and the major particular, produces a particular affirmative; its example: (every human is an animal, and some human is rational produces: (some animal is rational)). The fifth mood: a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major produces a particular negative; its example: (some animal is rational, and no animal is an inanimate object produces: (some rational being is not an inanimate object)). The sixth mood: a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major produces a particular negative. Its example: (every human is an animal, and some human is not a horse produces: (some animal is not a horse)).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage enumerates the six valid moods (adrab) of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author lists each mood by describing the quantity and quality of the two premises (minor and major) and the resulting conclusion. The first mood is implicitly given: two universal affirmatives produce a particular affirmative (e.g., every human is an animal, every human is rational → some animal is rational). The second mood has a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative (e.g., every human is an animal, no human is a horse → not every animal is a horse, i.e., some animal is not a horse). The third mood has a particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, producing a particular affirmative (e.g., some animal is a human, every animal is sentient → some human is sentient). The fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, also producing a particular affirmative (e.g., every human is an animal, some human is rational → some animal is rational). The fifth mood has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a particular negative (e.g., some animal is rational, no animal is an inanimate object → some rational being is not an inanimate object). The sixth mood has a universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, producing a particular negative (e.g., every human is an animal, some human is not a horse → some animal is not a horse). Note that all conclusions are particular (either affirmative or negative), never universal. This is a characteristic of the third figure.
